But he should at least have waited another day, copied more, compared pages, found out whether the second diagram matched the first.

Instead he looked at `fixed frame required` and thought of the abandoned greenhouse beyond the working row, the one with the warped ventilators and the potting benches nobody used because the heating charm there ran mean and sour and all the glass wanted replacing. It had old stone footings. It had dead vines in plenty. It had doors and latches he considered, very briefly and very stupidly, part of the virtue of the place. If anything went wrong, what was there to break that did not already look halfway broken?

He shut the manuscript and sat with his hand over the cover until the heat in the burn eased down from pain to warning.

Then he opened his notebook to a clean page and began copying out the exercise properly, in the narrow exact script he used when he wanted a thing to behave.

***

By the time he slipped through the greenhouse door, dusk had already flattened the grounds into blue glass and soot.

The abandoned house sat at the far end of the Herbology row with two panes painted over from inside where something had once spat acid at them. The path was slick from an earlier shower. Owen's shoes left dark chevrons on the flags. He had his satchel under his robe, the Grinlow Leaves wrapped in a pillowcase inside it because he did not trust canvas against rain or himself against panic, and a pocket full of bits he had nicked or borrowed without asking: chalk, two lengths of copper wire, a plant label, a jar lid, and the stump of a candle.

He did not need the candle. The greenhouse held heat like a bad temper.

The moment he shut the door behind him he stepped into damp air thick with old compost and the sweet rot of leaves left too long in trays. The glass overhead had gone cloudy in patches, so the last of the light came through blurred and greenish. One of the side benches had collapsed months ago and been left where it fell. Earthenware pots nested under it like dull skulls. At the back, a run of dead vine still clung to a trellis nailed to the brick pier between two ventilation cranks, all of it crisped brown and silver with dust.

Hot, but neglected. Private, but not tidy. He found this reassuring.

He put his satchel on the least filthy section of bench and took out the notebook first, then the wrapped manuscript. Even alone he felt furtive handling it. The pages gave a faint drag against one another, heavier than paper ought to be. He opened to the copied exercise rather than the original text because he was not enough of an idiot to prop the manuscript in direct potting-shed damp if he could help it.

The procedure, as far as he had it, did not look theatrical. It looked irritating. He had to bind the marks to a fixed frame. He had to use dead vegetal matter, not living tissue and not loose litter. He had to make admission the asked quality rather than the named object. That last phrase still felt wrong in his head. Asked quality. It sounded like something Binns would mumble just before the class died of boredom.

He chose a vine about as thick as his little finger and snapped it free of the trellis. It broke with a dry tick. Under the dust it still held a faint twisted pattern where it had once tried to spiral round wire. He laid it on the flagstone sill under the trellis because the manuscript's drawing had shown a stem crossed by frame lines, and a sill at least had the virtue of being stone and fixed and already part of the greenhouse rather than something portable he might accidentally enchant into the lake.

He drew the first mark with chalk. A bracket, then a second, then three short lines crossing the space between. At either end he wound the copper lightly round the stem and pinned it under the chalk marks with bent bits of label wire. Bound marks. Fixed frame. He even used the jar lid to hold a spoonful of water because one note seemed to suggest the dead thing ought to be reminded what admission had once felt like.

That done, he stopped and looked at the arrangement with the kind of dissatisfaction he usually reserved for nearly-right Arithmancy diagrams. It looked flimsy. It looked like nonsense. It looked, if he were honest, like the sort of thing a desperate person would set up because he could not bear another day of being told to wait.

He almost packed it away.

Then he remembered Neville saying, very evenly, that if Owen had time for manuscripts he had time to carry messages.

That settled it in the worst possible way.

He opened the Grinlow Leaves to the exercise page and laid his right hand against the margin until the hidden gloss surfaced. The heat lanced through his palm hard enough that he hissed. He kept reading.

Ask the frame, not the fibre.

Let admission be asked by bound marks.

Do not--

Still blank.

"Fine," he muttered to the page. "Fine. Small, then."

He set the manuscript back on the bench, crouched over the vine, and rested two fingers of his burned hand lightly on the middle chalk line. No wand movement presented itself. No familiar shape of spell. The notes suggested phrasing mattered, but not the way Latin mattered in charms. More like pinning a question so the room could not wriggle out of it.

He spoke quietly, feeling foolish and not letting that stop him.

"Let what's shut in this open enough to take water."

Nothing.

Of course nothing. He bit down on his irritation, looked back at the copied gloss, and tried again, narrower.

"Ask admission here. In the bound thing. In the frame."

For a second he thought he had failed so completely that even humiliation would have to wait its turn.

Then the dead vine gave a papery sigh.

Its skin split cleanly from end to end. Four dry curls peeled back from the pith like ribbon from a parcel, every inch of it still dead.

Owen leaned closer despite himself.

"Oh," he said.

Behind him, a cupboard latch clicked.

He turned in time to see the potting cabinet under the far bench spring open by an inch. Then another sound came from above, a metal jerk and slam, and both roof ventilators kicked outward together with enough force to shower old paint flakes down the brick pier. Cold evening air plunged into the trapped heat. The candle stump rolled off the bench.

Owen stood up too fast and cracked his shoulder on the trellis.

The greenhouse began opening.

Not everywhere at once. That would almost have been easier to understand. The rule ran the room in a rushing sequence. Drawer. Hasp. Latch. Vent. Door to the little tool lean-to. Lid of the sand barrel. The glazed cold frame at the far wall snapped up hard enough that one of its hinges screamed. Shrivelled seed pods spilled from a split packet and skittered over the flags. His satchel clasp sprang. Then the greenhouse door behind him thudded wide and bounced off its stop.

"No, no, don't," he said, to the room, because there was no single thing left to address.

He lunged for the satchel first. The pillowcase-wrapped manuscript had started sliding out of it, tugged by nothing visible except the fact that the flap no longer believed in closure. He jammed it back with his forearm and reached for his wand.

"Colloportus."

The spell hit the greenhouse door and flashed a thin bright orange line across the latch. For one hopeful second the metal held. Then the latch shot upward of its own accord with a cheerful clack and the door banged wider than before.

Everywhere in the greenhouse, hardware answered.

The tool lean-to spit open. Trowels and a rusted pair of shears slid clattering across the boards. The potting cabinet dumped a drift of mouldy twine and a sack of bone meal at his ankles. Above him the ventilators jerked another inch and the crank handles on the wall started to turn by themselves in small eager jumps. The dead vine on the sill kept patiently opening, each strip curling farther back as if it had all the time in the world.

He tried a second spell, a plain locking charm so easy a first-year could manage it half asleep. The wand kicked in his grip and threw sparks into the damp air with a noise like fat spitting in a pan. One of the water-butt lids leapt off and struck the opposite bench.

Owen swore properly then.

The burn in his palm pulsed once, hot enough that he nearly dropped the wand, and he understood at last what he'd actually asked. He had not asked the vine. He had asked for admission in a room built almost entirely out of things with latches: vents, frames, doors, cupboards, lids, catches, valves.

The greenhouse did not care which answer he had meant.

***

He got the manuscript back into the satchel and tied the flap shut with copper wire because clasps had plainly ceased to deserve trust.

After that the problem became physical.

The outer door would not stay put. He shoved it closed with his hip, wedged a stack of seed trays against it, and turned to find both cold frames up again and the tool lean-to door slamming itself against the inside wall like someone trying to break in from the wrong side. The roof vents were the worst of it. Every time the gusting evening air caught them they yanked wider, and every yank tugged the wall cranks in a little enthusiastic circle, which seemed only to remind the rest of the greenhouse that opening was still the rule of the hour.

He went for the nearest crank and hauled it down.

The handle bit into the freshened burn through the cloth he had wrapped round his hand before coming out. Pain went white and immediate. He lost his grip, snatched it back, and smelled singed linen before he realised it was his own handkerchief scorching against skin that had no business being that hot. He set his feet, took the crank in both hands this time, and leaned his full weight on it until the ventilator overhead grudgingly shut with a bang that shook warm dust down into his hair.

At once the opposite vent flew wider.

He laughed once, short and ugly, because the choice was that or shout.

Bootsteps sounded on the path outside.

He froze with one hand on the crank and listened. Two sets, quick, with the skittering scuff of girls' shoes not bothering to keep in time. Then voices through the glass, blurred by the panes.

"I told you it wasn't this one."

"Well, I can't see the number if it's dark, can I?"

Third-years, maybe. Out after the bell and trying not to get caught somewhere they should not have been. The greenhouse door gave another traitorous thump against the seed trays. One of the girls yelped.

Owen swore under his breath, crossed the floor in three strides, and caught the handle before it could leap again. Through the glazed panel he could make out two shapes in cloaks, one small and square in the shoulders, the other tugging her sleeve over a hand as if she had a habit of chewing the cuff.

"Occupied," he said through the crack before they could peer in properly.

There was a startled silence. Then the cuff-chewer said, "What?"

"Sprout's put me on repairs," he lied. "Go the other way if you don't want it on your shoes."

The smaller girl, who had a nose flattened slightly to one side as though it had once been broken and poorly persuaded back, said, "On whose shoes?"

"Compost," Owen said. "Obviously."

This at least sounded plausible enough that they fled, the pair of them half-running, half-sliding on the wet path, muttering at one another. A moment later another sound came from farther off: adult boots on gravel, heavier, unhurried.

That would be the patrol.

He had no time left for elegant solutions, if there had ever been any. He seized the sack of bone meal, dumped half of it by accident over his shoes, and used the empty sack as a wedge under the cold frame lid. He tied the lean-to handle to the bench leg with mouldy twine. He shut the potting cabinet with both knees and jammed a broken terracotta pot through its handles. Every closure felt provisional. The rule still wanted the room open. He could feel it in the way latches trembled under his palms, in the constant tiny ticks from all around him as metal tested itself against restraint.

He made the mistake of trying another charm.

It was not even ambitious. He only wanted the spilled seed trays stacked out of the way before whoever was outside looked in. He pointed his wand and said, "Wingardium Leviosa."

The wand gave a nauseating sideways jerk. The seed trays twitched but did not rise. Instead every lid left unsecured in the room snapped up at once: the butt lid, the sand barrel, the biscuit tin some long-ago worker had forgotten on the top shelf and which now disgorged a drift of fossilised crumbs and dead blue mould onto Owen's shoulder.

He stared at the crumbs on his robe for one stupid second, then wiped them off with the back of his wrist.

All right. No more wand.

He tucked it away and trusted his hands instead, though one of them was rapidly becoming a liability. The burn had deepened from the neat old brand the coffer had left. New red lines had risen out of it, crossing the original mark at angles that made no sense as skin and far too much sense as notation. When he gripped the final roof crank and dragged it shut, the raw places in his palm stuck to the iron for a horrible instant before peeling free. His eyes watered. He kept pulling.

Outside, the patrol slowed.

Amycus Carrow's voice reached him through the glass, thick with boredom and stale amusement. "If that's more of you little shits hiding out there, I'd rather you just say so. Saves me the walk."

Another voice, lower and dutiful, said something Owen could not catch.

He had the outer door pressed shut with both shoulders now, the seed trays braced against his shins. Cold air came in through the badly mended panes and over the top of the door, carrying wet earth and the sharper smell of the lake. Inside, the greenhouse crackled softly around him, held for the moment but no nearer calm.

The patrol boots stopped just outside.

Owen stood very still and discovered he was breathing in short, hot draughts that tasted of bone meal and rust.

Amycus tried the handle.

It lifted at once under his hand. Only Owen's weight and the trays held the door. The amended rule thrilled through the latch like a plucked wire. Owen thought, with complete and useless clarity, that if Carrow yanked harder the whole arrangement would come apart and he'd be found in a disused greenhouse covered in mould, gripping a satchel that could not survive inspection.

Instead Amycus let the handle drop.

"Locked wrong," he said. "Sprout's lot never could keep a house."

The other man made a noise that might have been agreement. Boots moved away.

Owen stayed where he was until the sounds had gone completely and even then another count of fifty besides, because panic made fools of arithmetic and he wanted at least one thing in the evening to obey.

When he finally let go, the door trembled but held.

The greenhouse looked ridiculous.

That was nearly the worst part. If it had looked solemn or blasted or beautifully uncanny, he might have granted himself the dignity of terror. Instead it looked petty and interrupted. Twine crossed handles. Bone meal crusted the flags white. Dead crumbs spotted the bench. The vine on the sill had opened itself into four brittle ribbons and now lay there innocent and useless.

He picked up one strip and turned it over. The inside showed pale pith, dry and fibrous, exposed rather than revived.

That was the first exercise. So it wasn't mending after all. The vine lay harmless in his fingers while the rest of the greenhouse kept giving little restless ticks around him. The room still wanted more mouths open.

He stood there with the opened vine in his fingers and felt the shape of the thing settle in him, cold now rather than hot.

The work was real. His hand would not stop throbbing, and sweat had gone cold under his shirt despite the greenhouse heat. He put the strip down and went back over the nearest catches with his left hand, pressing each one shut and waiting to see whether it meant to argue.

By the time he left, he had the worst of the place lashed shut and the rest looking merely shabby rather than bewitched. Merely shabby was about the best standard Hogwarts offered anyone that year.

***

He scrubbed his hands in the cellar lavatory until the water in the basin ran the colour of weak tea from compost and dust. The right hand did not improve.

Under the tap the old burn showed itself more clearly than it had in the greenhouse. The original brand from the coffer ran across the palm in a dark curved score, but the new lines branching from it were redder, slightly raised, and too regular to be random splits. They crossed the base of his thumb and ran down towards the wrist in measured strokes, a half-finished diagram written into flesh. When the cold water hit them, the pain did not ease. It compacted. The heat sank inward and sat there.

He dried off, wrapped the palm in a clean strip torn from the lining of an old shirt, and went to the common room because not going would have invited questions.

Hufflepuff at evening always tried to carry on as though routine had moral force. The round room glowed gold from the lamps sunk in the walls. Wet cloaks steamed by the hearth. Someone had smuggled up a plate of rock buns from the kitchens and they were disappearing one blunt bite at a time. Homework sprawled over tables among bottles of ink, mended quills, and socks drying on the fender. The occupation had thinned people, sharpened them, made them quicker to glance over a shoulder before laughing, but habit was stubborn. It kept putting chairs in circles and sending students looking for hot tea.

Martha Sorrell had one foot tucked under her and the other boot off while she darned the heel of a sock with a strand of bright green embroidery silk that turned the repair into an insult. Her plait had mostly come loose. She looked up when Owen came in, took in his face, his filthy cuffs, and the state of his shoes, and clicked her tongue once.

"You smell like a plant funeral," she said.

"Thanks."

"Not a compliment." She bit the thread clean with her front teeth and held the sock up to squint at it. "What happened to your hand?"

He had forgotten the bandage showed below his cuff.

"Caught it on wire."

"Where?"

"Greenhouse."

She gave him a flat look over the top of the sock. "That would explain why you smell like greenhouse, yes."

He might have laughed on another night. Instead he shrugged and started pulling books from his satchel with his left hand. The copper wire he had used to tie it shut had bitten into the canvas hard enough that it took him a clumsy half minute to unwind. Martha watched without comment, which was the kindest thing anyone had done for him all day.

Around them the room went on in scraps of ordinary talk.

"Have you got the Charms notes?"

"No, Finch took them."

"Well, get them back. I've got detention."

"You always have detention."

"Yeah, and?"

The harmlessness of it made him feel briefly unsteady.

Martha set the sock aside and pushed the tin of thread toward him with two fingers. "You want that stitched properly, ask Hannah. If you put dittany on it now you'll only stick the cloth in."

"I wasn't going to."

"Right."

She said it without edge, only tired certainty. She reached for a rock bun, broke it cleanly in half, and ate the drier piece first as if saving the less disappointing half for later. That was so exactly the sort of thing she did that it nearly undid him.

He looked down before she could see anything in his face and found that his quill case had split along one seam in the greenhouse. Feathers and nibs were inching out through the gap. A simple repair would do it. A first-year repair. He wanted very badly for one thing to behave the way it had yesterday.

He drew his wand.

Martha's eyes flicked up. "You all right?"

"Fine."

He pointed the wand at the torn seam. "Reparo."

The magic left his hand wrong.

It wasn't a matter of strength. The whole thing had gone sideways. He felt it as a slip in the wrist and then in the room, the way you felt a stair that was not where your foot had expected it. The split seam in the quill case did not mend. Instead the latch on Martha's thread tin sprang open, the lid of the sugar bowl on the next table flipped back, three satchel clasps clicked loose in quick succession, and somewhere by the hearth a trunk gave a startled wooden cough as its lid lifted an inch and dropped again.

The common room went briefly still.

A second-year nearest him snatched her schoolbag off the floor and tucked it under both knees. By the hearth, the boy with the trunk put one hand flat on the lid and kept it there. Another girl gathered her parchment and her inkpot and carried them to the far armchair without a word.

"What was that?" the boy by the fire said.

Owen lowered the wand at once.

"Sorry," he said. "Bad cast."

"Bad how?" someone asked from behind the sofa.

"I don't know."

A first-year near the window said, not quite under her breath, "Can he not do more of it in here?"

No one shushed her. The room had already started protecting itself in small practical ways.

Martha did not laugh. She looked at the open thread tin, then at Owen's wand hand, then back at him.

"You're white as paper," she said.

"I'm tired."

"Everybody's tired."

"Yes. I know."

He heard the edge in his own voice and hated it at once. Martha heard it too. Her mouth tightened, but she only took the wand gently out of his hand and laid it on top of his books as if removing a kitchen knife from near somebody drunk.

"Don't do more of that near my homework," she said. "Or anybody else's. Look at them."

He did look then. Satchels had come up onto laps. The sugar bowl lid had been set aside rather than trusted. A freckled fourth-year at the next table was threading her scarf through the hasp of her trunk with quick angry fingers.

"It was thread."

"Today it was thread." Martha kept her hand on the wand when he reached for it. "No more spellwork in here. Not tonight."

"Martha, that's a bit much."

"Is it?" said the boy by the fire, still holding his trunk shut.

Owen shut his mouth.

Martha waited. "Say it back."

He looked at the room pretending not to listen and failed to miss how thoroughly they were listening.

"No spells in the house," he said at last. "Not till I know what's happening."

"Good."

Martha pushed half the rock bun across to him. "Eat something."

"I'm not hungry."

"Then hold it and look convincing."

He took the rock bun because refusing would have turned the moment into something he did not have the steadiness for. It was still faintly warm in the middle. He sat with it in one hand and the other wrapped under the table in cooling pain while homework resumed around them in soft scratchings and mutters.

It resumed differently. No satchel went back on the floor. The girl in the far armchair kept her inkpot on her knee. The thread tin stayed open between Martha's elbow and Owen's books as if nobody was inclined to test whether lids deserved trust tonight.

After a minute Martha picked up her sock again.

"Green silk was all I had," she said, not looking at him.

"It suits the sock."

"Shut up."

That, at least, came out right.

Later, in the dormitory, he kept the promise because even wanting to break it told him Martha had been right. He hid the manuscript under the false bottom he had made in his trunk from a cracked Charms text and two strips of packing wood. He tied the trunk shut by hand instead of trusting the latch. Then he sat on the edge of the bed with the wand across his knees, opened his notebook to the last page, and wrote three things in the narrow exact script he used when he wanted a thing to behave: no spellwork in Hufflepuff, no repair or locking charms, and as little trust in his wand as he could manage.

The rules looked absurd. They also looked, tonight, like the nearest thing he had to sense.

He unwrapped the bandage.

The new lines in his palm had darkened. They no longer looked like the random after-effects of damaged skin. They looked appended.

He turned the wand once between thumb and fingers. Ordinary magic had always felt, if not easy, then legible. You learned the angle, the emphasis, the bit of language that made the world agree to a small temporary impossibility. Tonight the wand gave him nothing back. It was only varnished wood in his hand and the mean suspicion that the next mistake would go sideways again.

He had wanted proof the Grinlow Leaves were more than a hidden old book full of lunatic marginalia.

He had it now in the lashed-up greenhouse with its tied doors and humiliated cupboards, in the common room with half the house quietly guarding their clasps from him, and in the rules on the back of his notebook. The hot added lines crossing his hand made it plain enough that the manuscript had begun making notes on him in return.


# Chapter 5

By November the library windows had gone black by half past four, and the blown-out north bay held the evening cold like a cistern. Owen sat on the floor between two displaced cases with Binns's old *History* across his knees and his ash-linen notebook open beside one shoe. Since the greenhouse business, the rules on the notebook's last page had hardened into habit: no spellwork in Hufflepuff, no repair or locking charms, and as little trust in his wand as he could manage. He had come up there because the damage still made a pocket no prefect bothered with, a wedge of cracked shelving and dust where the draught reached first and people reached last. Also because his wand had turned sulky enough that writing with a pen had lately been less humiliating than trusting a charm to do what he asked.

The burn across his right palm had learned the weather before the rest of him. It pulled tight whenever the stone went cold. Tonight a thin hot line sat under the skin. Each time he flattened his hand to steady the page, it drew tighter in small patient increments.

He had copied the same blank table three times and disliked all three copies. The original in the Grinlow Leaves was not properly blank anymore, not to him. It had pressure in it. The page answered his burned hand with ruled spaces and something almost written just beyond them. The text waited on the far side of a nearly shut door. On paper from the stationer it collapsed back into ordinary failure.

A shadow crossed the floorboards and then held still.

"Owen."

He looked up too fast and knocked his pen. Ink dotted the floor, his sock, the cuff of his robe. Prue Hollis stood over the gap between the shelves with three books braced against her chest and her scarf knotted badly enough to suggest she had done it while walking. Her spectacles had fogged at the edges from the change in temperature. She pushed them up with the back of her wrist rather than her fingers, which were occupied by the books and by one orange fingerless mitten with the thumb seam split open.

"The library's closing," he said, because it plainly was, and because literal truths came quicker than useful ones.

"In twenty minutes." She nudged one of the loose boards with her shoe. "Move your notebook. Unless you want ink all over Bagshot."

"It's not panic."

"Fine. Your method, then."

She crouched before he could stop her and laid the books out in a row on the floorboards with a care that looked brisk only until one watched it. She lined the spines, squared the lower edges, then took a folded paper parcel from under one arm and set it between them. The parcel smelled of bread and cold onion. Only after that did she sit back on her heels and look at him properly.

Owen shut the notebook by reflex. It was too late for dignity. She had seen enough of the page to know he had been copying something and hiding it badly.

"I said one thing to you," he said. "At the Room. One citation."

"You said a great deal at the Room. Most of it was appalling." She tucked a curl behind her ear and failed; it sprang loose at once. "The citation was the interesting bit."

He ought to have left. The corridor beyond the damaged bay was empty. Madam Pince's tread had gone off toward the front desk. Owen could have taken the books as obstruction, Prue as risk, the whole thing as a reason to go to supper late and alone. Instead he stayed where he was and watched her open the first volume.

They were all copies of *A History of Magic*, but they were not identical. One had the old classroom binding with the boards wrapped in green cloth so drab it had gone almost brown. Another was a later school issue with newer buckram and a repaired hinge. The last was so over-handled the title on the spine had worn off completely.

Prue turned them all to the same page and pointed with a bitten thumbnail.

"Look there," she said.

"I'm looking."

"No, you're waiting to tell me it proves nothing. Look first."

Above the footnotes, the body text matched line for line. He could see that much at a glance. The paragraph break was the same. The hyphen fell in the same place. The word municipal carried the same tiny blot where a damaged bit of type had filled with ink. But the bottom of the page changed as it went across the row. In the oldest copy the footnotes ran six deep. In the next there were still six, but the lines sat tighter and the final one cramped itself into the lower margin. In the latest there were five, neat and even, and the line where his impossible citation ought to have been had simply gone.

"The page was reset," he said.

"The bottom third was reset," Prue corrected. "Badly. Everything above the paragraph mark is from the old standing type. You can see the nick in municipal, and the broken r in wizardry, and the little tilt on the numeral three. Then in this one" - she tapped the middle copy - "someone has squeezed the notes. In the later school issue they set the lower block afresh and tidy the numbering. Which would be normal if they'd revised the page. They didn't. They revised one citation."

Owen leaned in despite himself. The pages smelled different. The old cloth copy smelled of dust, the middle one of glue and old damp, and the newest of the sour paper left by too many student hands. The missing line was not absence anymore once she had pointed to it. It was surgery.

"That still might be authorial," he said, though the words came out thin.

"No." She opened the second book further, flattening it with the heel of her hand. "Because the footnote numbers in the running index at the back are off by one until chapter nineteen, where whoever did it noticed and overcorrected. And because this copy" - she touched the middle volume, almost fondly - "is a mixed one. Water damage, then repair. Two replacement leaves from a later issue bound into an earlier impression. Sloppy library work, bless them. One of the replaced leaves carries the tidy reset. The original one didn't. That means the citation survived in the earlier printing line and disappeared later. It was there, not just in Professor Binns's fossil but in the lineage."

She said the last word quietly, as if lowering her voice made the evidence less indecent. It did not.

Owen felt the small, mean wish that she had made some mistake. It wasn't exactly that he wanted her wrong. If she was right, the footnote had ceased to be his private, ridiculous disaster. It had roots outside him.

"How long did that take you?" he asked.

"Most of yesterday and all of this afternoon."

"Why?"

Prue looked at him as if the question itself was a way of wasting her time.

"Because you quoted a shelf mark that can't exist in any school issue after 1883. Because you looked ill enough that I thought you might actually have found something and then made a complete hash of explaining it. Because I was curious. Pick one."

From the corridor came the thud of a distant door and the faint scrape of a trolley wheel. Neither of them moved. The cold had got under Owen's robe and was working methodically through his knees.

Prue nudged the paper parcel toward him.

"Take half before I change my mind."

It held two thick slices of bread, a wedge of hard cheese wrapped in waxed paper, and an apple that had bruised brown at one shoulder. It wasn't much, but it was more than he had expected after missing most of supper. He broke the bread without thanking her quickly enough and felt rude too late.

"There were currant buns," she said. "I left them. Sultanas in bread are an offence."

That, absurdly, made him look at her again. She had spoken with the same severity she had used about print lineages. One of the curls at her temple had flattened where her hat or hood had pressed it; the rest were escaping in damp little hooks around her face. Her cuffs were inked through in two places. One lens of her spectacles carried a pale smear where she had tried and failed to polish it on the scarf.

Owen ate. The bread was dry and the cheese cold enough to squeak against his teeth. It was still excellent.

Prue waited until he had swallowed before she said, "So. Is the thing real?"

He could feel the notebook under his left hand, the copied tables trapped inside it like an accusation.

"That's a large question."

"You can answer a smaller one first, then. Did you find something at the shelf mark?"

"Yes."

"Can you show me?"

"No."

She bit into the apple, considered him over the sound of it, and said nothing for a while. The silence lengthened without sharpening. Somewhere in the front half of the library Madam Pince murmured a locking spell. A book settled in the damaged bay with a papery sigh. Owen finished the bread and looked at the bruised wood grain between his shoes.

"Do you go home for Christmas?" Prue asked.

He looked up.

"What?"

"Christmas," she said. "In December. Everyone pretends to be surprised by it and then it arrives anyway. Do you go home?"

"Usually."

"On the full train?"

"Yes."

"God." She took another bite of apple. "King's Cross in winter makes people forget they were ever civilized."

It was so plainly not the conversation he had prepared for that he answered without much guard.

"My mother wraps the sandwiches too tightly," he said. "Every year. Greaseproof paper folded under itself about six times. You have to tear it open with your teeth by Peterborough."

Prue's mouth twitched, not quite to a smile.

"What sort?"

"Egg. Or cheese and pickle if she's in a temper."

"That's a train sandwich," she said. "My father insists on buying them on the platform so he can complain about the price. He does it every year. He says if he doesn't complain they win."

"Who wins?"

"The railway, presumably."

Owen snorted before he could stop himself. The sound startled him almost as much as it pleased her. She used the moment to steal the cheese paper back and divide what was left into two more exact pieces than his had been.

"I hate the bit after York," she said. "Everybody goes to sleep upright and then wakes offended by it. My aunt always brings boiled sweets for her ears and passes them round as if she's distributing medicine to the condemned."

"We change at Nottingham sometimes. If the connection goes wrong my father reads the timetable aloud as if that helps."

"Does it?"

"No."

They ate the rest without much talk. The apple went last. Prue cut away the bruised part with a blunt little knife she produced from nowhere and offered him the cleaner half first. Owen took it and wondered, not for the first time, how many habits of someone else's he had missed in the Room of Requirement simply because he had been busy collapsing in public.

When she spoke again her tone had altered only slightly, enough for him to hear the question underneath it.

"That page you were copying," she said. "The one you shut. Was it from the thing?"

He hesitated.

"Sort of."

"That isn't a category."

"It's what I've got."

"It looked wrong."

He frowned. "Wrong as in damaged, or wrong as in -"

"As in not a table." She wiped the knife on the waxed paper and folded it away. "Or not only a table. You'd ruled it that way because the lines repeat, but the spacing wasn't clerical. It wanted sky. I only saw it for a second, so possibly I'm being insufferable, but there it is."

Owen's hand tightened on the notebook.

"From one glance."

"Yes, from one glance." She brushed crumbs from her skirt with the flat of her palm. "I had Astronomy after supper. I've still got the overlays in my bag. Monthly transparencies. Sinistra makes us do them because she enjoys other people's finger joints freezing off. If your page is built on declination bands, or on something near enough, we'd see whether it lines up."

"You wouldn't see anything."

"Not at once," she said. "You can keep that bit. We'd freeze first. Then we'd be annoyed. Then maybe we'd see something."

He should have said no again. He had already said yes to too much: yes, he had found something; yes, the shelf mark had led somewhere; yes, his ridiculous story had matter outside himself. The manuscript had stayed his partly because he never had to watch someone cleverer than he was put their hands near it.

But the copied pages remained stupidly blank in his notebook. His own efforts had got him as far as ruined latches, a worsening burn, and the knowledge that he was now being out-thought on printing history by a girl with one mitten and a violent opinion about sultanas.

"Where?" he asked.

Prue blinked once. "For what?"

"For trying it."

"Well, not the tower."

"I know. Not the tower."

"I don't know that." She gathered the books back into a stack. "Somewhere with glass. Somewhere not watched. A greenhouse would do, if there's one you haven't already catastrophically improved."

He stared at her.

"That was a guess," she said. "Your cuffs smell faintly of tomato stems and burnt copper. I don't actually know what you did."

He rubbed at one sleeve, which made the smell no less real.

"Half an hour," he said. "After evening study."

"All right."

"One page."

She rose with the books braced back against her chest.

"One page," she agreed, in the tone of someone agreeing because more argument would only slow the next part.

At the end of the bay she stopped and turned back.

"Owen."

"What?"

"It isn't panic," she said. "Whatever you've done with it after that might be. The footnote isn't."

Then she went out between the shelves and the library took back its ordinary sounds: the faint clack of locks, the long wooden settling of old cases, the draught moving through the damaged north wall. Owen sat with his closed notebook under one hand and the ache in his palm bright as a coal. Keeping her out had been the rational thing. He could still tell himself that. It had just stopped being the easier one.

* * *

The greenhouse Prue chose was not the one Owen had misused, though it stood only two down the line and carried some of the same smell in its bones: damp soil, old sap, the mineral breath of watering cans left half-full too long. This one had been emptied for winter save for two benches of dormant bulbs and a row of cracked terracotta pots stacked by the door. Frost had started along the lower panes and worked upward in ferny white fans. The heating pipe clicked without conviction under the staging.

Owen reached the latch and hesitated before touching it. Since October every hinge had felt faintly personal to him.

The door opened normally. That ought not to have been a relief.

Prue was already there. She had commandeered a stubby hurricane lamp from somewhere and set it on the central bench between a bag of tools and a tray of dead labels. Her scarf was off now, wound around the base of the lamp to stop the draught licking the flame. She had rolled her transparencies into a cardboard map tube tied with black ribbon, and she was rubbing a clear patch in the condensation on one pane with the heel of her hand.

"You're late," she said.

"By three minutes."

"I was cold for all of them."

He set his satchel down. The Grinlow Leaves were wrapped inside his spare shirt at the bottom, with the ash-linen notebook on top as decoy and working copy. He had brought a second candle as well, because his wand had refused a steady *Lumos* in the corridor and he was not in the mood to be shamed by it in front of her.

"Did anyone see you?" he asked.

"No. I came round the back by the potting sheds."

She jerked her chin at the bench. "Put the page down. If we're going to do this, let's not waste the cold."

He opened the notebook first. The copied table sat in the middle like all the worst bits of homework he had ever done: painstaking, ugly, and still not right. He had traced the repeating lines, the blind little pinpricks at the corners, the faint curve he could only see when the page lay almost flat to the light. Prue bent over it with her hands braced either side and went still in that total way some people did when reading. Her curls slid forward. One caught in the orange wool of the mitten and stayed there until she blew it away.

"You haven't copied the depth," she said after a moment.

"What depth?"

"The under-light. No, that's woolly." She glanced at him. "You've copied where the marks are, but not what they do when the light hits sideways."

"I know that."

"I know you do. I'm saying it aloud because that's the problem."

She slid the notebook nearer the lamp and untied the ribbon round the tube. Four sheets of translucent paper came out, each larger than foolscap and stitched with inked lines so fine they might have been cracks. She had pricked the brighter stars through with a needle and numbered them in cramped pencil. In the lamplight the pinholes showed white.

"These are this term," she said. "September through December. Monthly sky paths for the southern view from the tower. Don't laugh at my November one. My hands froze."

"I wasn't going to laugh."

"Good."

The paper was colder than it ought to have been. Owen held one corner while she laid October over the copied table, then November, aligning the lower edge to his ruled lines. Nothing happened. She rotated the sheet ninety degrees. Still nothing showed. The lamplight shone through ink, paper, and Owen's bad copy without the smallest hint of answer.

Prue frowned and adjusted again, narrower now, moving by increments no thicker than a fingernail. Owen could feel impatience stirring in him, not at her exactly but at surfaces, at the insufficiency of copies, at the way the whole business kept presenting itself as nearly legible and then refusing the last inch.

"If you're going to say it isn't a table after all," he said, "that's not actually helpful."

"I'm not saying that." She shifted the sheet left. "I'm saying your copy is obedient in a way the original probably isn't. Which is inconvenient and very rude of it."

The heating pipe clicked again. Wind dragged softly across the glass above them. Beyond the roof the night was clear in patches, clouds moving thin and fast.

Prue tried the November sheet upside down. The pinpricks of the larger stars crossed the drawn lines in his copy and stayed meaningless.

"This is boring," she said.

"Yes."

"Good. It would be suspicious if it wasn't."

They worked through another stretch in the same cold silence. Prue lifted the sheet, turned it, and lowered it again. She tried December, aligning not the edges but the corner pinpricks to the faint blind marks he had copied from the margins. That gave them a pattern for half a second - or Owen thought it did - then lost it.

Prue sat back, flexing her fingers. "How exact is the copy?"

"Exact enough."

"For what?"

He looked at her. "For copying what I could see."

"That is very nearly an answer."

"No, it is the answer."

"No," she said softly, "the answer is whether the original sits wrong in the same places or whether your notebook's been politely lying to me."

Owen looked at the notebook. The page lay there with all his effort in it and none of the original's stubborn pressure. The right answer was no. The safe answer was also no. He disliked the way those had ceased to match.

"The original behaves differently," he said. "Under light. Under touch. Differently."

"Right," she said. "I thought so."

She did not pounce on that. She only rubbed at the split seam of her mitten with her thumb and waited. It was, Owen realized, a particularly vicious kind of patience. He could feel the satchel against his leg like another presence in the room.

"Did you bring it?" she asked.

He should have lied. Instead he said, "Yes."

"May I see the page, then? I don't mean the whole thing. I just mean the page."

The not the whole thing was kind enough that it nearly annoyed him more. He crouched by the satchel, unbuckled it, and lifted out the notebook to keep up the appearance of choosing. Beneath it lay the spare shirt, rolled around the leaves. He unwound the cloth with hands that did not feel attached to the rest of him. The burn in his palm sharpened as soon as the outer fold came away, a quick bright pull. The manuscript knew the cold and disliked it.

Prue did not lean in. She stayed where she was, though her breathing changed.

The leaf he chose was one of the bad ones, or what he still thought of as bad: mostly blank to anyone else, restless under his hand, ruled in faint pressure and scattered marginal punctures that might have been damage if they repeated less exactly. The surface was not parchment and not paper. It had the dry, dense feel of fabric treated until it forgot softness.

"Can I touch it?" Prue asked.

She hadn't asked to keep it, and she hadn't asked what it was. She had only asked to touch it.

"One finger," he said. "At the edge."

She gave him a brief look that might have been amusement and rested her forefinger on the lower corner. Her nail was bitten square. She touched the page as if testing heat from a stove.

"That's horrible," she said quietly. "It's like starched skin."

Owen had thought something similar and hated hearing it said aloud. He set the leaf on the bench instead and flattened the corner himself. The burn in his palm lit hard under contact. The blind lines in the page pressed up against his skin from below.

Prue took the November transparency again.

"Let's not waste time pretending your notebook matters," she said. "Hold the top edge."

They carried the leaf and overlay to the cleared pane she had rubbed free of condensation. Beyond the glass the grounds were a blur of dark lawn and paler frost, with a slice of sky above the black line of the greenhouses. There was no full moon, just winter stars when the cloud unstitched enough to allow them.

Prue held the transparency over the leaf and began to turn it in minute adjustments. Owen kept the page steady against the glass with his left hand and the lower margin pinned under his burned right palm. The cold from the pane went straight through the leaf and into the bones of his fingers.

"Lower," she murmured.

He lowered it.

"No, the other lower. Sorry. I hate words with directions in them."

The sheet moved again. One of the pricked stars on her overlay sat suddenly over a marginal hole in the manuscript and vanished into it. Then another. Then a thin line, brown-black and mean, bled up between two of the blind rulings. Owen stopped breathing.

Prue saw it too. He knew she had because the paper trembled once in her hands and then steadied.

"Don't move," she said.

"I wasn't-"

"I know. I mean it kindly."

More cloud moved off the slice of sky. Through three pinholes the real stars burned white. Under them the page answered.

The blind ruling darkened into columns. A heading surfaced along the top, not all at once but letter by letter, the strokes seeping into visibility with reluctant patience:

*FIRST WARD TABLES*

Below it, narrower lines rose and locked into place. He saw symbols he did not know and a repeated mark like a hooked gate. Four short columns sat under four quarter-signs. A note in a different hand ran up the side: *read against the winter sky; confirm at anchor; do not trust copied distances.*

Prue made a sound deep in her throat, not wonder exactly, more the involuntary sound of a person whose argument had just been vindicated by the furniture.

"Owen," she said. Then, because she had sense, "No. Never mind. Keep holding it."

He wanted all his hands and had only two. "Notebook."

"Right." She let go of the overlay for half a second, caught it again when it slipped, swore once under her breath with enough force to surprise him, then dragged the notebook open on the bench beneath the window. "Read."

"I can't read it all."

"Read what you can."

He did. He read the title, the side note, and two of the column headers once he had the angle. One might have been burden, or weather, or a word near enough both that translation did not help. Another repeated a sign he had seen elsewhere in the manuscript beside exercises that involved edges, thresholds, places where one state admitted another.

Prue wrote fast and ugly when she was in a hurry. That, too, humanized her abruptly. Her ordinary notes were compact and even; these sprawled where the lamp and the changing sky forced them. Ink caught on the paper fibers. She blew on one line and smudged it with the edge of her hand.

"Again," she said.

He read again, narrower, and corrected himself twice. She accepted the revisions without ceremony. Together they got enough down to matter before the clouds thickened and the lower lines sank back into blind pressure.

When the page went quiet again the greenhouse seemed louder. Wind worried the far door. Water ticked somewhere from a cracked pipe. Owen became aware that his right hand hurt fiercely. He took it off the lower margin and saw the burn had gone the angry red of fresh sealing wax.

Prue set the pen down. Her fingers were stained black to the second knuckle. "That wasn't a code," she said. "Or if it was, it wasn't just a code."

"No."

"It's an instrument."

"Probably."

She looked from the page to him. "How long have you been doing this alone?"

He flexed his hand once. The cold made the skin feel split without actually breaking.

"Since September," he said.

Prue swore again, softer this time and without weight enough to count as anger. More like a person placing a fact somewhere she disliked it.

"And the burn?"

"From the box it was in. Mostly."

"Mostly."

He said nothing.

She lifted the notebook page they had just filled and looked at the sprawl of copied words, the scratched columns, the crude little quarter-signs she had drawn to catch the structure before it disappeared. Then she looked back at the leaf on the bench, dull and blind again in the lamplight.

"You weren't mad," she said. "You were just impossible."

"I didn't think I was mad."

"You stood up in front of Neville Longbottom and proposed what sounded very much like a library haunting. I left room for the possibility."

It was a fairer account than he wanted. He sat on the edge of the bench opposite her and found that his knees were shaking slightly from cold. The lamp made a small island between them. Around it the greenhouse remained all old glass, dark soil, and winter air sneaking through putty gaps.

Prue took off the orange mitten with her teeth and rubbed her cramped hand. Then she noticed where his right palm had folded in on itself against his knee.

"Let me see."

"It's nothing."

"Owen."

He gave it over because refusing suddenly felt more childish than private. She turned his hand up into the lamplight. The burn had gone from sealing-wax red to something wetter and angrier. The added lines stood ridged under the skin.

"Christ," she said, very quietly.

"It's always uglier after."

"After it answers?"

He did not say yes. Her thumb stopped short of the bright part, careful not to touch it.

"My mother does this thing with the gas bill," she said abruptly. "Every January. She carries it into the kitchen as if it's a telegram from the front and then spends half an hour turning down fires we already aren't using."

Owen blinked. She was still holding his wrist.

"What?"

"Nothing. I just..." She let out a breath through her nose. "This is very large and I suddenly thought of my mother trying to shame the boiler into costing less."

He looked down at the page with *FIRST WARD TABLES* half-copied across it in Prue's hurried hand. The hidden lines, the sky, the impossible footnote with its own ancestry of removal. It was large. His palm gave a hard little throb inside her cold fingers.

"My father mends plugs at Christmas," he said before thinking better of it. "Nothing's wrong with them. He just lines them on newspaper and says if he doesn't do it now he'll never do it. He never finishes. There are always screws left."

Prue huffed a laugh and pressed the heel of her bare hand briefly against her eye. She let his wrist go only then.

"That's worse than the gas bill."

"Yes."

They sat with that for a bit. The talk was small on purpose. His palm kept beating, and every few seconds Prue's glance went back to his hand or to the blind leaf on the bench. Parents and boilers were the only manageable facts in the room just then.

Prue was the first to return to the actual trouble.

"The side note said confirm at anchor," she said. "Do you know what that means?"

"No. Not in a way I'd trust."

She looked at him sidelong. "Fine. Do you have a worse guess?"

"Three, maybe. That's the problem."

"And the quarter-signs?"

"I've seen them elsewhere. Near thresholds. That isn't the same as knowing them."

"So these are ward tables."

"Something like that."

"They aren't spells."

"No. Not spells."

She nodded once, mostly to herself. Her private voice, Owen noticed, was not warmer than her public one. It was only less defended. He could hear the small repairs in it where she chose the truer sentence over the cleaner one.

"If we're doing this," she said, "I need to know whether that was one lucky page or whether the whole thing's like that. And whether it always does this to you. I'm not fussing. I just need the shape of it."

He heard the if and the need and the not fussing. None of it was dramatic. She had only looked at the page, then at his hand, and made room for both facts at once. She had earned the exactness.

The manuscript lay on the bench beside his satchel, wrapped leaf over wrapped leaf inside the spare shirt. He could put it away now. He had the proof he needed. More than proof: a heading, a method, another mind capable of seeing what the pages wanted. But Prue had already seen the page come up under the winter sky and the burn answer it. Sending her back to her tower charts and school issues and platform sandwiches now would mean pretending those were separate things. They weren't.

His hand hurt. The lamp guttered once and recovered. Somewhere outside, very far off, a door slammed in the castle proper and the sound came through stone and glass as a dull, ordinary fact.

"All right," he said.

Prue waited.

He reached into the satchel, drew out the wrapped bundle, and untied the shirt completely this time instead of teasing out a single leaf. The Grinlow Leaves settled on the bench between the dead labels and the lamp base with the weight of something both lighter and more serious than any schoolbook had a right to be.

Prue looked at the bundle, then at him.

"You brought all of it."

"Yes." He swallowed. The answer felt less like generosity than stepping off a curb in fog and discovering the road was already under him. "If you're staying, you may as well see how it starts."

For the first time that evening she seemed to lose a word, though only one. Then she pulled the notebook and the November sheet nearer, turned to a fresh page, and said, very carefully, "All right, then. Show me from the shelf mark."

He did. By the time the cold finally drove them out, her cramped star numbers had started appearing in the margins beside his Grinlow lines, and the whole thing had stopped being his alone.


# Chapter 6

The next evening at supper, with one of Prue's star numbers half copied into the margin beside a line of Grinlow symbols, the spoons began at the Ravenclaw table.

At first it sounded like somebody dropping cutlery one piece at a time: a light dry knock, then another, then a run of them. Owen looked up and saw every spoon on the table rise an inch from the wood. They turned together, bowls gleaming with gravy and lamplight, and pointed straight at the High Table.

For one stupid second the Hall held still around it. Steam lifted from potatoes. A first-year at the end of Hufflepuff froze with custard on her chin and her mouth open. Then the spoons started hammering themselves against the rims of the serving bowls, not random, not noisy enough to be accidental. Three beats, pause. Three beats, pause. They all faced the teachers while they did it, as if a hundred bits of silver had developed opinions.

Amycus Carrow came up out of his chair so hard the bench scraped stone.

"Enough of that," he barked, and half the spoons dropped dead at once. The other half kept going for one more beat, stubborn as teeth chattering in a skull. Amycus drew his wand. The doors of the Hall slammed shut on the bang of it.

Owen's first thought was not fear. It was annoyance, pure and mean. He had one good hour before curfew. He had a copied line in the margin of his Charms essay, a prepared chalk stroke wrapped in paper inside his sleeve, and a decent chance of finding out whether the new surfaced table meant contact in the physical sense or only in the legal one. Somebody had decided to be brave with spoons.

Around him, benches scraped. Conversation died in clumps. At the far end of the Hufflepuff table Martha had already shoved the last of the treacle tart toward a second-year whose nose was red with cold and whose left sleeve still bulged with the mitten she kept checking for as if its pair might come back by magic. Martha stayed half turned toward her even when Amycus started down from the dais, one hand still on the custard jug, plait loosening down her back.

Alecto Carrow moved more slowly, her heavier heel striking crooked on the flagstones. "Wands on the tables," she said. "Bags open. Everybody stays put till we know which little shit thought that was funny."

A groan went round the Hall, quickly smothered. Nobody wanted to be the one heard reacting.

Owen slid his parchment flat over the copied figures. The margin still looked like homework if you did not know what you were seeing. That was the useful thing about Prue's hand; even her dangerous notes looked schoolish at a glance, all tidy fractions and fussy corrections. He folded the sheet once, then again, and tucked it inside the back cover of *Defensive Theory for Beginners*. The paper inside his sleeve felt damp already with sweat.

Amycus had made it to the middle of the Hall. He was thick through the shoulders and flushed at the cheeks, the colour climbing faster as he got angrier, and there was a thread hanging from one undone bootlace that kept flicking against the stone when he walked. He looked, Owen thought irrelevantly, like a man too lazy to tie his own shoe but very willing to break your fingers for noticing.

"Up," Amycus said.

Nobody moved quickly enough for him. He jabbed his wand at the nearest table and every goblet rattled. "Up means up."

The Hall lurched into motion. Students stood. Benches shoved back with a long wooden scream. The smells of supper came out stronger when everyone moved at once: cabbage water, damp robes, the sweet thick skin on stewed fruit, soot from the torches. Owen stood with the others and slid his wand onto the table. He left the book where it was and put his hands where Alecto could see them.

Martha edged in on his left as the table compressed. "You know anything about this?"

"About spoons?" he said.

She turned and stared at him, not fooled and not interested in the literal answer. Her cuff had come unstitched at the wrist and someone, probably her, had repaired it with green thread that did not match a thing on her robe. "Jesus Christ, Owen."

"I don't know who did it."

"That's not what I asked."

Alecto began the searches at the end of Slytherin, slow and performative. Students had to empty pockets onto the wood. Quills. Crumbs wrapped in napkins. Contraband sugar. A pocketknife from someone who ought to have known better. She handled every object like it had personally insulted her.

Amycus reached Ravenclaw and started pulling satchels apart. He tore through them without care. Ink bottles broke. A textbook hit the floor hard enough for the spine to split. The first-year with the custard chin began crying soundlessly, her face folding in on itself while she tried not to make a noise.

Martha saw her and hissed, "For fuck's sake," under her breath, though whether at the girl or at the Carrows Owen could not tell.

"If you've got something," she said to him without looking over, "anything at all that isn't just skulking and notes and whatever you've been doing, this would be a useful moment to stop being mysterious."

He kept his eyes on Amycus. "It isn't ready."

"Ready for what?"

"For this."

She laughed once, a horrible little breath with no humour in it. "Everything's for this now."

Owen did not answer because there was not an answer that did not turn into the whole impossible thing at once. Because if he tried to explain that he had been working on conditions and not protections, on categories and not shields, she would hear, correctly, that he had been using occupied school nights to tinker with a manuscript while she ran messages through a regime that beat children for spoons. He knew that. Knowing it did not help.

Amycus had reached Hufflepuff. A fourth-year emptied his pockets too slowly and got backhanded across the mouth for it. The sound cracked through the Hall. The boy sat down by accident and then sprang upright again, blood on his lower lip, while Amycus laughed.

Martha's head came up. "It was cutlery," she said, not loud at first.

Alecto looked over. Amycus did too.

Martha repeated it, louder. "It was cutlery. You're acting like somebody blew a wall out."

The silence after that felt crowded. Owen could hear the first-year breathing through snot. He could hear, somewhere further off, a Ravenclaw girl's teeth clicking because she was cold or scared enough not to manage stillness.

Amycus smiled.

The expression spread slowly, the way spilled drink spreads through cloth.

"That's nice," he said. "I always like the volunteer commentary."

He moved toward their table.

Students tightened without meaning to. Martha had already set her shoulders for the thing she was going to say next. Owen knew the look: not courage exactly, not even recklessness, but the flat irritation she got when somebody bigger than her insisted on being stupid in a space everybody else had to live in. He also knew, with the clean mechanical certainty he hated about himself, that if Amycus reached the table and searched sleeves, the paper-wrapped chalk stroke would not survive the next ten seconds.

There were three possible decent actions in the room and he chose the fourth: he left Martha standing there while he crouched.

He let her face Amycus alone, kept his hands to himself, and left the stunt on her.

Instead he bent as if his wand had rolled under the bench. His fingers found the chalk packet inside his sleeve, thumb already whitening with powder. In one movement, shielded by table edge and bodies, he stripped the paper away and dragged the prepared line hard along the underside of the bench's front lip, burying it in the carved badger scrollwork where shadow ate the white. It was only the priming stroke he'd copied out and shaved earlier to a precise width: enough, if he had read the damned page right, to wake on the first hard contact in front of the bench and spoil it. After that it would need wording or go broad and stupid. The chalk mix carried a flake from the old burn skin at the heel of his palm because the Leaves had answered better that way. He pushed the crumpled wrapping deeper into a crack in the wood and straightened.

Amycus was in front of them.

"Well?" he said to Martha.

She lifted her chin. "Well what?"

The Hall let out its breath all at once, but too quietly to count as sound.

Amycus's smile widened. "There we are."

Owen still had chalk under his thumbnail.

* * *

Amycus did not ask for names.

That was the first bad sign. Names meant process, or the pretence of it. Names could be denied, traded, lied about. Amycus looked from Martha to the rest of the table and seemed to decide, there and then, that explanation would only waste everybody's evening.

"Out," he said, jerking his wand toward the centre aisle.

Martha did not move.

Amycus caught a fistful of her robe at the shoulder and yanked. The green-thread repair at her cuff snapped with a sound as small as a twig. She stumbled over the bench leg, hit the table with her thigh, and went because the alternative was being dragged face-first.

The first-year beside her made a noise, not quite a sob. Martha threw her a look over her shoulder that was almost annoyed, as if apologising for causing trouble was one more thing she had not got time for tonight.

Amycus hauled her into the open space between the house tables and turned to the room as if beginning a practical lesson.

"Since one of you thought it'd be clever to dress supper up with jokes," he said. "Fine. We'll do it this way, then. Useful way."

He kept one hand bunched in the back of Martha's robe while he spoke. The grip was intimate in the ugliest way. His fingertips worked into the cloth, finding purchase, enjoying it. He gave her a short shake. "First thing. Room gets ideas, you don't waste your arm on all of it. Waste. You pick one. One with friends. One the rest'll feel."

Scattered ugly laughter came from the Slytherin end, the sort students made when they wanted the adults to hear them being safe.

Martha twisted, trying to get her balance. "You sad bastard."

Amycus smacked her across the face.

The blow went wrong exactly the way the priming stroke was meant to make it go wrong. Owen saw the skin of her cheek flatten under the hand and then refuse the clean landing. Amycus's palm slid. It took her mouth sideways, split the edge of her lip against a tooth, and then skidded off in a way that made his arm overtravel. Martha staggered two steps rather than one. Amycus blinked, more irritated than surprised.

Under the table, the hidden chalk line had gone warm.

The sensation reached Owen through the soles of his shoes first, a dry friction in the stone, as if the bench were being very slowly dragged across grit. The old burn in his right palm tightened. So the copied note had been right about that much. The priming stroke was enough to wake the rule. Without the rest of the instruction, every hard contact in front of the bench would come in crooked and keep going. He'd told himself he was only refusing to lose the line. That story lasted about as long as it took Amycus to turn back and take hold of Martha by the jaw.

"Look," Amycus told the Hall. His thumb pressed into the cut he had just made. "Mouth won't answer, it can do this. Bleed. Cry. Shut the room up."

Martha jerked away from him and he lost the grip, fingers smearing blood and spit across her chin instead of pinning it. The line was taking more than blows now. It had started taking holds as well. Anything trying to arrive square in that strip of floor started slipping its shape. Amycus only looked annoyed, which meant the next thing would be rougher.

He had no time for clean wording or a safe radius. He had only the bench, the line under it, and the exact wrong demonstration in front of him.

He set his left hand flat on the table where anyone could see it. With the right he took up his wand as if on instinct. The wood bit his skin at once. He kept the movement small, hiding the tip against the bench edge, and let the phrase he had spent two evenings not understanding settle into the back of his mouth. The line was already awake. All the wand phrase could do now was tell it how to keep misbehaving.

A complete loss of contact would be absurd, he thought. She'd fall through her own shoes. He needed something short of landing: a skid, a glance, a pass.

Amycus shoved Martha backward and sent a stinging hex after her before she had recovered the step.

The curse hit the front of her robe, spread blue across the damp wool in a bright branching smear, and shot off sideways into the stone by the Ravenclaw table. Chips spat from the flagstone. Martha cried out anyway because the edge of it had caught her shoulder, enough to burn through cloth and leave a black crescent, but it was not the full-body wrench Amycus had meant.

A murmur ran through the Hall before anybody could stop it.

Amycus turned his head. "Quiet."

He spoke without taking his eyes off Martha. He had seen it, even if he had not understood it, and Alecto had too. She had gone still with somebody's confiscated pocketknife in her hand.

Martha breathed twice through her nose, sharp and deliberate, and spat blood onto the floor. "That all?"

Owen almost lost the line laughing, less because it was funny than because it was so completely her to answer pain with household contempt, as if Amycus had served burnt potatoes.

Amycus smiled again, thinner this time. "No," he said. "Not near."

He flicked Levicorpus at her.

The curse took at the edges. Her feet ripped off the stone, but instead of hauling her cleanly upside down it caught more in robe than body, wrenching her sideways. She rose with one leg kicked up and one shoulder dragged high, spun half round, and slammed into the end of a bench hard enough to make the wood jump. The back of her head missed the corner by an inch. Her forearm did not; Owen heard the crack of bone or bench and could not tell which.

His palm seemed to open under the burn, an inner split under the scar rather than any visible wound. The wand recoiled so violently it struck his knuckles against the table edge. Light flashed in his hand. He tasted old pennies and lamp smoke.

Around Martha, smaller things had started to go wrong. Her shoe skidded when she tried to stand. Amycus's grip failed twice in a row, fingers slipping from fabric that should have held. A bowl somebody had not cleared from the end of the table slid away from a reaching hand and smashed on the floor because friction in that patch of stone was now wrong, or thinner, or answering to a different rule.

Alecto frowned. "Amycus."

"What?"

"Something's off."

"Yes," Amycus said. "Her mouth."

He seized Martha by the plait this time. The twist held better than cloth. He dragged her close enough that Owen could see the watered red in her eyes and the raw place where the stinging hex had burned through robe to skin. Amycus put his mouth almost to her ear, speaking for the room anyway.

"And if that doesn't take-" He dragged her by the plait till her face tipped up. "you stop being nice. You go lower."

He drove a knee into her stomach.

The contact broke wrong there too. The blow slid off the angle of her hip and sent her sprawling rather than folding. She hit the stone on both palms and one knee with a skin-tearing slap. It spared her ribs. It took her skin instead. She gasped and went white.

Owen held the phrase. The line had answered a new question now, and the answer was filthy. The hurt kept landing; it simply slid off centre. Miss the ribs, take the palms. Miss the stomach, split the knee. Amycus could keep changing angle all night and the line would keep finding some other place for the blow to land.

Martha pushed herself up on shaking hands. Amycus kicked one arm out from under her and she hit the floor again, this time cheek first. Blood printed on the flagstone, a small mark and enough.

Owen heard himself say, very softly, "Stop changing target."

The words slipped out before he could keep them in. Martha's head snapped toward the Hufflepuff table. Amycus followed the line of it.

"You saying something, Phipps?"

Every part of Owen wanted the room smaller. He could feel the chalk line humming through the bench front like a held wire. "No, sir."

"Thought not."

Amycus raised his wand toward Martha again, then paused. His head tilted, listening to something mean in himself. When he spoke next his voice went almost convivial, which was worse.

"Actually," he said, "let's make this educational."

He pointed at a seventh-year Slytherin near the dais, a tall girl with a bitten thumbnail stained blue by leaking ink. "You. Show them a stinging hex. Properly this time. Not that nancy classroom rubbish."

The girl went grey but stepped out because not stepping out was another form of volunteering. She held her wand like somebody trying not to touch a dead rat.

"Go on," Amycus said.

She cast. The hex flew green-white, shaky and fast.

It clipped Martha's upper arm, sheared off into her robe seam, and tore the fabric open from shoulder to elbow in a line of sparks. The skin beneath blistered in three separate patches instead of one continuous lash. Martha bit down on the scream, failed, and let it out anyway. The sound had nothing brave in it. Owen was grateful for that. Brave sounds turned into stories too quickly. This was only pain.

Amycus barked a laugh. "See? Better."

The Slytherin girl looked as if she might be sick.

Owen's wand jumped again, jerking in his grip like a live thing trying to get away from him. The bone ache ran up his forearm. The next problem was speed. The line was no longer taking single orders from him; every new touch in that strip of Hall yanked a fresh decision through his hand. Cloth. Skin. Stone. Spit. Boot leather. The questions were arriving faster than he could narrow them, and his arm was starting to fail before the chalk did.

Amycus turned abruptly and fired at Hufflepuff.

At first it was just a savage sweep, a warning shot at the table that had looked too horrified to his liking. Students ducked. Owen, because he had the line under his hand and nowhere to hide inside himself, took it in the shoulder, though the line stripped some of the force sideways before it finished arriving.

The curse struck his robe, tore heat across the collarbone, and skidded off into the bench beside him, blasting a hand-span trench out of the wood. Pain came a second late, hot and granular, like somebody grinding salt into skin with a thumb.

He made a sound he hated and clamped his mouth shut.

Amycus stared at him.

That was the bad moment. The hit barely mattered beside being noticed.

"Funny," Amycus said.

Owen could not tell whether Amycus meant the odd angle of the deflection or only the fact Owen was still upright. Around him students were looking everywhere except directly at him, which was its own form of looking. Martha was on one elbow in the aisle, breathing hard, and she was staring straight at his wand hand.

"Maybe," Amycus said softly, "you'd like a turn instead."

Owen's first stupid hope was that he meant some minor hex, another stinger he might survive with the line still holding. Then Amycus's face changed again, all the loose amusement burned out of it, and he said, "Crucio."

The curse was red at the core and colourless at the edge.

Crucio was too big for the line to spoil neatly. Martha still took it; the body-knowledge of it found her at once, back bowing, hands clawing, mouth opening on a raw sound that ripped the Hall apart. But the curse would not stay in her. It crawled over robe cloth and flagstone joints in crackling threads, leapt from her sleeve to the floor, snapped blue-white along the seam between stones, and grounded itself somewhere under the Ravenclaw table with a sound like a lamp exploding.

The whole Hall flashed.

Candles guttered blue. Goblets rang. The house-point hourglasses at the wall sang one thin note and then were silent.

Martha collapsed, shaking and still fully inside the pain, but conscious enough to roll onto her side and cough blood-flecked spit onto the floor instead of lying there empty.

Owen lost the line.

The line tore loose from him. His wand punched backward so hard it flew from his hand, struck the table, and bounced to the floor. The shock up his arm felt like a bruise being hammered into place from the inside. He grabbed the table edge with his left hand to stay vertical. Under the bench, the hidden chalk mark had burned through wood and into stone. He could smell it now, a sour mineral hotness that did not belong in the Great Hall.

Nobody moved.

Amycus looked at the blackened seam in the floor and then at Owen and then at Martha. He lacked the wit to understand what he had seen. He still understood enough to know the room had misbehaved around the Hufflepuff boy and the girl in the aisle.

Alecto came down two steps from the dais. "Amycus."

This time her voice carried something besides annoyance. The candles were still burning blue.

Amycus spat on the floor beside Martha's hand. "Fine," he said, breathing hard through his nose. "That's enough. That's what clever little shits get in here. Pain. Everybody watching. No clean bit of it. Think you're special, do you? I take my time with that. Right there. Let everybody look."

He jabbed his wand toward the doors. "Take her to the hospital wing. If she can stand, she stands. If she can't, drag her. And if I hear another spoon in this castle so much as twitch, I start with the first-years. First-years first, then the rest of you."

The doors banged open.

Sound came back in a rush. Benches scraped. Somebody began crying properly now, with hiccups. The Slytherin girl with the ink-stained thumb backed away as if Martha's skin might stain her. Hufflepuffs surged into the aisle. Amycus stepped over Owen's wand where it lay on the floor, noticed, and ground the heel of his boot down beside it without quite touching.

"Phipps," he said, not looking at him. "You're odd."

Then he walked out, bootlace still trailing.

Martha tried to stand on her own and nearly went over. Two girls got hold of her elbows. She shook one of them off long enough to look at Owen.

Pain and furious, ordinary clarity filled the look. She was adding up what she had just seen and noticing what had already been in his sleeve.

Owen stooped for his wand because if he looked at her properly he might answer. The wood was hot. It jerked in his hand as soon as his fingers closed round it.

"What was that?" she said.

"Not here," he said.

"You did something."

"I don't know exactly what I did."

Her eyes dropped to his hand, to the chalk he had missed under the thumbnail. "But you had it ready."

He took too long to answer.

Her laugh turned into a cough. "Yeah. Course you did."

Then the girls on either side got her moving, and Owen went after them because standing still in the Hall with his wand in his hand felt worse.

The passage to the hospital wing clogged at once. Someone behind him was crying with hiccups now. Somebody else kept saying make room and not making any. Martha stumbled at the turn and slapped a hand against the wall. Owen reached without thinking. The older girl on her left knocked his hand away before he touched cloth.

"Leave it, Phipps."

"I was only-"

"Don't," Martha said.

She did not turn round. Her voice came out rough with blood in it and thinner than he had ever heard it. "Don't come in there now and do the helpful bit."

The group started again. One of the younger Hufflepuffs looked back at him with open hatred and then away because there were more immediate jobs. Owen stopped in the middle of the corridor while they took Martha through the hospital-wing doors. They stood open, bright, and might as well have been locked.

* * *

He should have gone in anyway.

That stayed true for maybe thirty feet.
