He backed down the corridor with the hospital wing still open behind him and tried the smallest spell he could think of, as if a decent bit of school magic might prove he was still the sort of person who could walk back through those doors. He tried only Lumos, under his breath, because the stair off the Hall was dim and his shoulder felt wet where the curse had torn robe and skin together.

The wand kicked like a mule. Light flared at the wrong end, burst white against his knuckles, and died. Pain ran up his arm to the elbow in a deep thudding stripe.

"Fuck," he said, and leaned his forehead briefly against the wall.

The stone gave him a faint stored vibration instead of cold, a wrongness in it, as if somewhere inside the castle a bell had been struck and the sound had not yet decided to become audible. He could feel it through forehead bone and teeth. When he lifted his head the portraits along the passage were muttering in their frames, not awake enough to complain, only uneasy.

He could have turned back then. The hospital wing was still behind him, bright at the end of the passage, and somebody in there would have said what he deserved to hear. He kept going the other way, down toward the kitchens because he needed distance from bodies and because the service passages there were often empty after supper if you kept out of the elves' way.

His wand hurt to hold. That was new. This was actual recoil, material and repeated, beyond the vague unreliability after the greenhouse or a misfire now and then, as if the wood objected to him on contact. He shifted it to his left hand and the right palm answered with a fresh tightening under the old coffer burn. The scar there had darkened from angry red to a strange bruised violet at the edges.

At the bottom of the stair by the kitchens, heat and noise should have met him. Pots knocked in the scullery. Water ran. The ordinary wet labour of the castle fed itself there. Instead the passage was quieter than usual, held down rather than empty.

Tallow stood on a stool by the scullery door, sorting bent ladles into two stacks with the concentrated misery of a man balancing accounts nobody else respected. He was small even for an elf, one ear singed to a notch, and he kept testing each ladle by tapping it once against his wrist before deciding which pile it belonged in. When Owen came down the passage, Tallow looked up and all the skin around his eyes pulled tight.

"No," he said immediately.

Owen stopped. "No what?"

Tallow set one bent ladle aside without looking. "No to whatever you've just been doing to the house."

Owen's first instinct was denial. His second was the literal answer. "I wasn't doing anything to the house."

Tallow gave him a look that would have been familiar on Martha and was somehow worse on a creature three feet tall with wet hands and soot on his cuffs. "Young sir, the plates are shivering in the rack and the south wall's making a singing fuss of itself. Tallow is not taking that for weather."

He hopped down from the stool, came two steps nearer, and then stopped as if an invisible draught had met him. His nose wrinkled. House-elves, Owen remembered from half things overheard in Hufflepuff, did not like some old parts of the castle. He had never paid attention because most old parts of Hogwarts were only old stone and school history and had nothing to do with him. That category had been shrinking all autumn.

Tallow pressed a damp hand flat against the passage wall, snatched it back, and glared. "There. Hear that?"

Owen listened.

At first he heard only kitchen noise at a distance: boil, clatter, a muffled shout. Then, under it, the note came through, finer than draught through a keyhole and steadier than plumbing, a held hum in the stone.

"It'll stop," Owen said, because he wanted it to.

"Maybe," Tallow said. "Maybe not. Old work doesn't always know when to mind itself once struck."

The phrase hit Owen in the chest harder than the corridor curse had. "What old work?"

Tallow's face closed at once. He reached for the nearest ladle and began rubbing at a stain that was not there. "Kitchen work is for kitchens. Upper-school foolishness is for upper school. Tallow's not talking beyond his broom. Not about pipes, not about coal, and not about what lies lower than both."

Owen stepped closer. The wand in his hand twitched. "If you know something-"

"Everybody who works in this house knows something," Tallow snapped, then repaired himself into a softer tone out of old habit. "Meaning no offence, young sir. Only you have a look about you tonight that says you've put your hand where the house keeps its age."

He glanced at Owen's palm and then away too quickly, not from politeness. From dislike, or fear.

"Go lie down if you've got any sense," Tallow said. "And if you haven't, don't lean on any wall that answers back."

Owen wanted to ask five more questions. He asked none because the hum in the passage had changed while they spoke. It had grown directional instead of louder. It seemed to pull, very slightly, back toward the Great Hall.

He left Tallow among the ladles and climbed again.

By the time he reached the Hall, the corridor outside it was empty. The doors stood half closed. Supper mess remained inside in the ugly aftermath way only a public room could manage: plates abandoned crooked, a goblet overturned, one shoeprint through spilled custard near the Ravenclaw benches. Torchlight moved gently over the long tables. Everything looked ordinary. The feel of it had gone entirely wrong.

Owen crossed to the Hufflepuff bench where he had hidden the line.

The wood under the carved badgers had split along the stroke of it. A white mineral seam had pushed up through the grain from inside, pale and dry and impossible to wipe away. He touched it with one finger. The bench gave back a shiver that travelled straight up his arm.

He crouched and looked along the floor.

There it was.

From the bench front to the centre aisle, nearly invisible until the light caught it, ran the ward scar: a hair-thin whitened line sunk into the flagstones, not painted on, not surface chalk, but part of the stone now, following no joint Owen could see. It forked once where the stinging hex had gone wrong. It thickened in the place where the Cruciatus had skated into the floor. Near the centre, where Martha had hit blood first, the line widened to the size of a thumbnail and then narrowed again, like a pulse trapped underground.

Owen sat back on his heels. The Hall felt bigger in attention than in distance, as if the room were no longer merely containing what had happened but recording it.

He drew his wand and held the tip two inches over the scar.

The wand flew backward out of his hand.

The recoil barely tossed it clear, but it cleared far enough. It cracked across his knuckles and the wand spun once in the air before he caught it against his chest. In the same instant the hum in the stone resolved.

A single note gathered itself.

The note reached him through his teeth, through the tender place under the old burn, through the bruised stripe along his arm. The Hall answered his proximity like metal answering metal. The hourglasses at the wall rang very softly. Far above, somewhere in the stacked dark of the castle, another answering vibration came back through the stone a heartbeat later.

That was when the real fear arrived.

The real fear came from somewhere colder than Amycus; Amycus could beat students and miss the structure of a room entirely. Fear of being caught with the manuscript remained practical enough, but this was narrower and worse: the sense of having made a disturbance in a system he barely understood, and of that disturbance travelling along routes older minds might already know how to read.

Snape, Owen thought.

He had not seen him. The fear was simpler than that: a headmaster lived in the listening parts of a castle whether he wanted to or not. Some forms of school authority were built out of notice before they were built out of punishment. If the Hall had just rung, somebody higher in the stone might have felt the note.

Owen forced himself up, legs stiff from crouching. He put the wand away. It hurt less when he was not trying to use it, though even in the pocket it seemed to sit wrong, heavier at one end than the other.

At the door he stopped and looked back once.

The scar held its place in the floor, faint as a crack seen through milk. Water and house-elf vinegar would never scrub it out. It sat there as evidence.

He left without going near the hospital wing.

He got to the cellar with nobody seeing him, or nobody stopping him, which was not the same thing. The dormitory was mostly empty. Hufflepuffs were still below, or at Pomfrey's, or carrying the story of the Hall from bench to bench. Owen did not take the notes out. He sat on the end of his bed with the robe stuck to the burn on his shoulder and his right palm giving little dry pulses under the old scar.

Every time he shut his eyes he saw the white seam in the flagstones and Martha looking at the chalk under his nail, and each time the old scar in his palm gave a dry answering ring. The two things sat together in his head and would not sort into anything useful. He listened to footsteps go past in the corridor outside, each one sending a faint answer through the stone, and kept not knowing whether he wanted hers to stop at the door or keep going.


# Chapter 7

The Grinlow Leaves did not change all at once. Sleep did not help. For three nights after Amycus Carrow had turned the punishment dais into a lesson in the Hall and Owen had answered with the condition of contact instead of any proper spell, the corridor working kept ringing in his palm whenever he shut his eyes. On the third, a page near the centre of the manuscript began to take ink. At first he thought it was soot from the common-room fire. Then the marks persisted when he shifted the book into candlelight, pale brown lines rising through the paper as if they had been waiting lower down and his stupidity in the corridor had driven them upward. By supper the page held a route under the kitchens, a cluster of founder marks he could now half follow, and one repeated instruction that his mind kept translating into the same plain phrase: ward scar sufficient. East Undercroft answering.

He shut the book twice and opened it again. His right palm, the one the coffer had branded in September, ached against the cloth cover. His wand had been unreliable since the greenhouse and worse since Martha, if he was honest, since Amycus had made her stand there and he had let the whole room watch him fail in public. The castle had answered too. For hours after the punishment the walls had seemed to ring through his feet. Even now, in certain corridors, a dull pressure climbed through the bones behind his face as if somebody far below was tapping stone with a metal spoon.

By half eight he gave up pretending he could wait for Prue or think sensibly alone. The page wanted a place, and the only person who had ever hinted that the kitchens ran deeper than pipes and coal was Tallow.

The kitchens were nearly finished for the night. Heat breathed out of them in thick wet gusts that smelled of onions, soap, singed pastry, and the heavier stew intended for tomorrow's lunch. Tallow stood at the scrub table beneath the hanging copper pans, polishing pudding spoons one by one with a square of old flannel and rejecting any that showed the smallest twist. He put the bent ones to his left in a separate little line, as if they had disappointed him personally.

Owen waited until the nearest pair of elves disappeared into the scullery with a cauldron between them. "Tallow."

Tallow did not look up. "If young sir wants treacle crusts, there are two under the blue cloth and he may take only one, because greed makes crumbs."

"I don't want crusts."

"That is already an alarming start."

Owen set the wrapped manuscript on the table between the straight spoons and the bent ones. "I need the east-side maintenance run. The older one. Under this level."

At that Tallow's hand stopped. The flannel stayed clamped round the spoon bowl. He lifted his eyes at last, and the skin around them tightened before anything else in his face did. "No," he said. "Young sir needs sleep, and perhaps better judgement if there is any left."

"It's in the Leaves." Owen pulled the cloth back just enough to show the new page. "This wasn't visible before. Not until after the corridor."

Tallow stared without touching. His damp hands hovered an inch above the paper and then retreated into the flannel. "Yes," he said softly. "The house heard about the corridor. Every knife in the wash-rack sang. The tureens knocked their lids off. That doesn't make it a place for students."

"It means the site answered."

"It means you rang something old and stupidly loud."

Owen felt the correction arrive in him like a click. "Not loud, exactly. Specific. I think the scar matched whatever threshold this uses. It says sufficient." He tapped the founder marks with one ink-stained finger. "Or near enough. I need to know whether the larger claim is real."

Tallow set the spoon down with deliberate care. "Young sir keeps saying larger claim as if that is tidier than saying what he means. The old work hurts my kind. The deeper routes hurt worse. There are passages house-elves use and passages we know better than to use. This is one of the second sort."

"I know."

"No, you don't. Knowing a word for pain isn't the same as having it behind the eyes."

Owen nearly answered too quickly and stopped himself. "Fair. Sorry. I know I don't know it the same way. But the page changed now, not in September, not after the greenhouse. Now. If the disturbance has opened the undercroft, then either the manuscript's true and there's something under the castle worth hiding, or I've burnt my hand and half broken my wand for a very elaborate lie. I need to see which."

Tallow made a small sound in his throat that might have been contempt or worry. He looked past Owen at the banked ovens, at the stacked plates, at the ceiling as if the school above them had become heavy enough to notice. "There are rules," he said.

Owen waited.

"There's no wandlight in the run. The old seams don't care for school spells. There's no touching side marks unless I say. If the house gives warning through the floor, we stop. If I say back, we go back. You won't argue just because boys who love books think pain is an opinion."

"I won't."

Tallow's mouth pulled sideways. "That answer would be prettier if I believed it." He folded the flannel, slid it into his apron, and swept the rejected spoons into a drawer with more irritation than force. "Come on, then. Before I remember sense."

He led Owen past the main ovens, through the buttery, and into a root store where sacks of potatoes leaned against the wall in soft collapsing towers. Tallow paused long enough to straighten a crate of onions whose corner had tipped into damp. Only after he had wedged a folded rag under it and nodded to himself did he tug aside an empty flour bin and uncover a squat iron door low in the stone.

The hinge made no sound when he opened it. Cold came out instead, thick and mineral. Owen crouched, then had to fold lower still as the passage dropped away under a barrel curve of stone no full-grown adult wizard would have chosen willingly. The grease lamp Tallow carried threw a short amber circle that moved over wet mortar, old pipe clamps, and occasional founder marks cut into the wall like maintenance instructions left for hands smaller than his. The air narrowed as they went. Kitchen noise sank into the stone behind them until all that remained was their own movement, Owen's sleeves brushing grit, Tallow's bare feet placing and replacing themselves with careful precision.

After twenty yards the pressure started in earnest. The roots of Owen's teeth hummed with the same thin metallic misery as biting hard on tinfoil and never being allowed to let go. His right palm grew hot through the bandage. He kept one hand on the floor for balance and felt, through cold slime and grit, a faint recurrent thud as if something far off were hitting the castle with the side of a fist.

Tallow slowed. Owen heard his breathing change.

"Are you all right?"

"Tallow is walking," he said, which was not an answer. Then, plainer, "Don't ask again unless you mean to turn round for the answer."

The passage bent east. Twice it narrowed enough that Owen had to put the manuscript ahead of him and worm after it on one shoulder. Dry heat ran through the wall here, locked in the stone and almost feverish under the damp. At one bend he saw old broom wear on the floor, centuries of slight curved scratches where something had once been dragged regularly through this route. Secrecy was only the by-product, he thought. The route existed for service and upkeep. Whatever lay below had needed mops and buckets and hands.

That was what steadied him more than any amount of diagram. Hidden things in stories were always altars, tombs, prophecies. This place smelled of wet lime and old soot and of things kept working by people no one had thanked properly in several hundred years.

The passage opened just enough for him to straighten at the waist. Ahead, the lamp found a wall that was too smooth to be natural and too featureless to be a door unless one already knew it was there. The founder marks on his page matched the shallow cuts round its edge exactly. At the center sat a thumb-deep hollow lined with the same dull white metal that threaded certain pages of the Leaves when the light caught them wrong.

Tallow had stopped several feet back. His singed ears were pressed flat. "Quickly, if it's going to open."

Owen wedged the grease lamp on a stone nub and peeled the cloth off his right hand. The old burn across his palm had faded from blister-red to a stubborn brown gloss, but near the life line a fresher mark pulsed in sensation alone, the place where the corridor working had rung back through him. When he pressed his hand into the hollow the metal went hot at once, a hard warning heat that stopped short of pain and still told him he had been expected.

Something shifted inside the wall. Pins withdrew one after another with small dense knocks. The seam breathed out dust and colder air. Then the slab rolled inward on hidden weight.

The East Undercroft met him as a service room built for weight. The chamber ran roughly circular and high enough to lose its ceiling in soot-dark shadow. Drains and service channels divided the floor into walkable ribs. Benches of black-green stone stood under two walls with bowls, clamps, and dead lamps left in deliberate rows. Iron rings had been sunk into one section of floor for hauling or bracing. Shelves, long emptied, still held clean tool-shapes in the dust. The place smelled of cold metal, old lime, and labour gone dormant rather than forgotten.

Owen moved toward the sloped gleam on the far wall.

Tallow caught his sleeve. "Dry stone only. Step where I step." He pointed with two cramped fingers toward the channels cut through the floor. "If those start silvering, we're finished. And stone first. The house tells on the stone before it tells on the chart."

Owen bit back the impatient answer and followed the line Tallow marked. At the centre rose the anchor stone.

It came out of the floor to just below Owen's chest, a faceted mass of green-black rock the size of a coal cart and too exact in its geometry to belong to any quarry he knew. Four broader bands entered the floor channels like buried conduits and ran under the walls toward the cardinal directions. Finger-width veins of silver and the same dull white metal threaded the block in ruled circuits, dipping below the surface and surfacing again. One face carried two generations of repair: old dark staples buried under a later skin of leaded sealant, founder cuts recarved over the top where a crack had split the first geometry. Apparatus, then. Damaged, patched, still carrying load.

Sweat had darkened the soot at Tallow's temples. "East face," he said. "There. That's where your corridor note came through."

Owen crouched. A section no bigger than a school slate had opened into hairline fractures across the white metal. Founder scoring veered round the damage with the ugly stubbornness of later repair. He put a finger out on instinct.

Tallow's hand snapped between him and the crack. "Look with your eyes."

"I'm only looking."

"Do more of it."

The rebuke steadied him better than agreement would have done. He circled the stone more carefully, reading what he could. Some facets carried conditions he recognized from the Leaves: masking load, transfer limits, thresholds for signal suppression. Others packed themselves too densely for him to parse, but even those read as work rather than ornament. At the damaged eastern facet the truth of the corridor settled in his stomach with a physical drop. He had struck an actual piece of the system.

The far wall carried the table the manuscript had failed to render until now. A shallow bronze slab jutted from the masonry on thick fixed supports, sloped for standing work. Silver points and threaded arcs crossed its surface around a pale central disc washed to the colour of bone seen through water. Tiny black inserts ringed the outer edge. Everything about it belonged to calibration: sight-lines, pressures, reference points, the marks left by people keeping a burden in trim.

Owen stepped toward the disc.

Tallow made a raw little sound. "Edge, young sir. Use the lip. The middle wakes hot."

When Owen laid his unbandaged hand against the bronze rim, the silver inlays sharpened under the skin of the metal. A cluster northwest of the central disc brightened into the same geometry as the cracked facet behind him. Hogwarts sat there by angle and repeated founder mark. More points answered in a broken ring, most burning low, two guttering almost out. For one foolish second he thought he was looking at Britain drawn badly. Then one silver arc ran cleanly off the right edge of the slab and picked itself up again on the lower curve with the same notch mark, the sort of cheat only round things needed when forced flat.

He dragged the Leaves half out of his pocket with his free hand and nearly dropped them. The newly surfaced gloss page carried the same paired sign at four quarter marks around a wash of ink the page had labelled world-cover rather than local load. He looked back to the table. Two faint scored bands crossed the pale disc north to south and east to west, rubbed almost out by old use.

He heard himself laugh once under his breath, not from amusement but because his body had chosen the wrong sound for shock. "It's true."

Tallow looked up sharply. "Which part?"

"Hogwarts," Owen said, touching the bright cluster with one knuckle. "And others. Not county by county. It's wrapping the whole thing."

"Whole what?"

Owen swallowed. "The planet, I think. Or near enough that arguing about the word would be stupid."

Tallow looked from the stone to the table with the expression of a cook discovering that the cracked range under his hands also held up the whole house. "Literal is often the rude option."

Along the lower lip ran three neat columns of marks, finer than the load notations and grouped in repeated sets. Owen knew some from the newly surfaced gloss pages: capacity, interval, transit, after-remnant load. He read them once and got stores. Crates, perhaps. Replacement stock. Tools meant for the benches and clamps behind him.

"No," Tallow said from the doorway, squinting. "Out-marks. Same thinking as stores and laundry lifts. Send, count what's left, seal what's empty."

Owen read them again and disliked how quickly that corrected him. One recurring sign he had taken for vessel sat beside the same tally stroke the kitchens used for heads at table. Not crates. Occupants. He tried to make the stepping numbers into rationing, plague loss, anything messier and less deliberate than departure. The entries refused him. Each reduction paired with the outward mark and a maintenance note about masking load after transfer. The last lines were cleaner than the rest: closure, decommission, planned departure. The kind of ugly practical record left when a household moved and meant the old rooms to stay shut behind it.

He flattened his fingertips to the metal and forced himself slower. "They left on purpose," he said. "The Meridian Commonwealth. They shifted the population and left the ward running. They meant to go."

"Left what behind?" Tallow asked.

Owen turned his head but not enough to lose the lines. "Us, apparently."

The lamp flame bent sideways though there was no draft. Along the nearest drain a pale thread appeared and crept through the cut stone bright as molten pewter. Tallow saw it and went still.

"Back," he said.

Owen lifted his hand from the bronze. Then the black inserts at the rim answered one by one, not all of them, only the ones aligned with the bright Hogwarts cluster and two guttering points farther round the disc. The manuscript page in his pocket gave a hot twitch against his ribs. He stopped halfway to the door and turned back.

"Young sir."

"Wait."

He dragged the Leaves out fully and laid them against the lip. The first margin line nearly sent him the wrong way. Recurrent convergence after high discharge could have meant weather, recoil, ordinary load seeking balance. Then the next cluster repeated a hunter's hook from a damaged gloss he had only half understood, used there for pursuit conditions rather than repair. Not convergence, then. Predatory convergence. Reacquisition risk.

The coldness of it frightened him more than any prophecy would have done. Beyond the world disc the silver arcs frayed and gathered at the same worn places, metal thinned almost white where attention kept finding brightness. Whatever noticed those flares did not need a face or a creed. Pattern was enough. Motion was enough.

"If I'm reading it right," Owen said, not looking away, "a flare doesn't just leak out. Something finds it again."

Above them, thickened by stone, something heavy struck in the castle proper. A door slammed, or Amycus hit a boy into a wall, or a suit of armour toppled in one of the corridors. The undercroft returned it as a body-blow through the floor. Owen flinched. Tallow closed his eyes for one second too long.

"School again," he said.

The word pulled the school back into Owen whole: Martha swallowing blood because Amycus was watching, Neville counting who still stood, boys learning to keep eating through a scream two passages away. None of that had eased. Above them somebody was still being hurt, and if the school ever lit up properly the table was saying hurt would not stay local.

The pale thread in the drain split and ran down a second channel. Tallow's free hand clamped the jamb so hard the knuckles blanched under soot.

"Back," he said again, harsher now.

This time Owen obeyed. He scooped the Leaves to his chest, but not before fixing the outer ring in place: three whitening marks, one of them Hogwarts, and the warning written like maintenance, not fear.

He stepped away from the table and back toward the centre stone. Up close, on the cracked eastern facet, he could see where some older repair had failed and been reworked by less skilled hands. A smear of founder-era tool scoring wandered off true by less than the width of a quill. The whole planet might presently depend on margins like that.

He rested his burned palm lightly against the anchor stone. Heat answered from deep inside it, stored strain climbing his arm to the shoulder socket. He didn't know how to repair it yet. He barely knew how to read it. But disbelief had ended here. The damage was real, the work older than ministers and Dark Lords, and somewhere above them Amycus was still teaching children with pain.

When he finally turned away, he took the image of the table with him so hard it hurt: the world at the centre of a maintenance chart, the dim nodes, the whitening marks outside the mask where notice gathered whenever magic flared too brightly. The castle overhead groaned once more, ordinary violence or ordinary panic carried down into the stone, and he carried both sounds with him.

Tallow sealed the door behind them with both hands and a muttered string of house marks Owen could not follow. By the time they had crawled halfway back to the kitchens, the pressure behind Owen's eyes had eased just enough for thought. He knew he should be sick with fear. Instead he felt the colder, steadier thing that had replaced fear whenever the manuscript stopped teasing and started proving.

At the flour-bin entrance Tallow paused to set the iron panel back into its slot and wipe his palms on his apron as if he could remove the undercroft by ordinary kitchen means. "You won't bring a crowd next time," he said.

"There won't be a crowd."

"No. I thought not."

"Young sir," Tallow said, quieter now, "whatever that stone is, the house has been carrying it a very long time. Mind how you use what it gives you."

Owen nodded. Beyond the root store the kitchen clocks ticked, pans cooled, and somebody in the scullery dropped a ladle with a familiar domestic clang. Then, from higher in the passage, came the blunt scrape of feet on stone and a boy's voice saying, too fast, "Hospital wing. Move."

Owen looked up. The sound held there for a second, plain as an opened door.

He set the wrapped manuscript on the flour-bin lid instead, pulled a nub of pencil from his pocket, and took the few seconds needed to sketch the outer ring before the whitening points could blur.


# Chapter 8

Owen left the Great Hall as soon as he decently could. Christmas had thinned Hogwarts without making it kind. The portraits on the way out watched from their gilded frames like informers, and a laugh from two landings up came down the stone stripped of any warmth it might once have had. Even the Hall, with its bewitched candles and the paper chains somebody had bullied into service again, looked less like a feast than a room trying to remember what feast meant. After the East Undercroft and the shout for the hospital wing scraping down the passage above it, he had wanted one room in the castle that admitted, without theory, what people were for.

He took the service stair toward the kitchens. The air changed halfway down. Cold stone gave way to yeast, onions, damp wool steaming itself dry, and the sweet metallic tang of washed copper. By the time he ducked through the painting and into the kitchens proper his glasses would have fogged, if he had worn any. The room hit him with light and condensation. Huge pans hung over the ranges, every polished curve blotched white with steam. Holly had been jammed into cracks in the beams where it would do no structural harm. Someone had stuck cloves into the peels of two oranges and set them by the big proving drawer, so the whole place smelled faintly festive and mostly of soup.

Tallow stood on an upturned crate at the nearest stove, dipping a ladle, watching the soup sheet back into the pot, and muttering at an open cutlery drawer. He had tucked a blue pencil behind one singed ear and left a greasy thumbprint on the side of his own cheek. On the crate beside him lay three bent dessert spoons and two more in a neat accusing row, as if he meant to hold a hearing later.

"No, young sir," he said before turning around. "Don't stand there trailing corridor cold over the tiles. Sit down. Soup's right now and if it gets any thicker it'll have to be sliced."

He turned then, saw Owen properly, and clicked his tongue at the look of him.

"You're pale in a peevish way," Tallow said. "That means hungry. Sit."

Owen sat. The table was scrubbed pine with knife-nicks all along one side where years of hurried slicing had gone through bread and into wood. It looked ordinary enough to hurt a little. Somewhere behind him, out of sight, another elf laughed. Something went into a hot pan with a violent hiss.

"What's wrong with the spoons?" Owen asked.

Tallow shot the drawer a black look. "What's wrong with the spoons is that students think cutlery is portable by divine license. Five dessert spoons gone in three days and one soup ladle singed black. No house can keep standards if every pudding is attended by theft."

He stirred, tasted again, then added a cap of cream with the solemnity of a man correcting a legal injustice.

"If the school gets much quieter," he said, "the missing spoons will start accusing one another just to hear company."

"Then interrogate the ugly ones first," Prue said from the next table. "They're the likeliest to break."

Owen had not seen her at once. She was half-hidden behind a stack of plates, tall even sitting down, her dark curls pushed back with a ribbon that might once have been navy and now had the colourless defeat of too many washings. One hinge of her spectacles had been repaired with a twist of copper wire, and she kept nudging them up her nose with the heel of her hand so she would not get grease on the lenses. There was ink on both cuffs. There was always ink on both cuffs.

Tallow sniffed. "Miss Hollis speaks as if the handsome spoons are any more loyal."

"I don't trust pretty silver," Prue said. "It's vain metal."

That got a brief snort from Owen before the kitchen door banged again and Martha came in sideways, shoulder first, with her scarf half off and her plait loosening down her back. She had a heel of bread in one hand already, which meant Tallow had either given up on stopping her years ago or never tried in earnest. She shut the door with her boot and pointed the bread at the pot.

"If that's the parsnip one, I want the thick bit," she said.

"Miss Sorrell wants the thick bit of every situation," Tallow said, but there was more approval than complaint in it. "Sit before you eat the tablecloth as well."

Martha flung herself onto the bench opposite Owen. She was still compact even in all the winter layers, broad through the shoulders, face pink with cold and temper. One cuff had split and been mended in green thread that did not remotely match the jumper. She saw him looking at it and tucked her wrist under the table without comment.

"You escaped the Hall early," she said.

"So did you."

"Amycus was trying to sing. I drew a line."

"Sensibly," Prue said.

Tallow brought the pot over himself rather than trusting any of them with it. The soup came out thick and gold-brown, steaming hard enough to blur the scratched windows over the sink. He followed it with a plate of bread, a dish of butter gone soft at the edges, and a smaller plate on which sat four mince pies with badly pinched lids.

"Those are for later," he said at once, because Martha had looked at them.

"I know what 'later' means."

"You know what it means when someone else says it. That's not the same."

Martha rolled her eyes and tore bread anyway. Prue took one corner of a slice and set the rest back down with exactness. Owen wrapped both hands around his bowl. The heat went through his fingers and found the burn in his palm at once. The scar did not like sudden warmth. It brightened under the skin with a sensation that was not pain exactly, more a private wrongness, as if the hand remembered the coffer and mistrusted all containers since.

For a little while they ate like people who had not come to accomplish anything. Martha blew on each spoonful too hard and splashed the heel of her thumb, swore, licked it clean, and kept going. Prue scraped cream from the rim with a piece of bread because she said skin was only acceptable if attached to a person. Tallow, passing between counters, kept glancing over as if he expected one of them to insult the meal by turning solemn in its presence.

It was Martha who preserved the ordinariness first.

"Dad does bubble and squeak on Boxing Day whether anyone wants it or not," she said into her bowl. "He says if you've made it through Christmas you owe the leftovers proper respect."

"Your father sounds doctrinaire," Prue said.

"He's about frying. Not about anything useful. He does it in the big iron pan because he says the little one lies about heat. Burns the first side every year and calls it flavour."

Tallow made a scandalised noise from the sink.

"That's not flavour," he said. "That's impatience with grease attached."

"Tastes good, though." Martha tore another piece of bread. "He puts in too many sprouts on purpose because he knows my aunt will pick them out and complain and then he can complain back. Whole afternoon sorted."

"That does sound useful," Prue said.

Owen said, before he could stop himself, "Does it actually squeak, or is that just the name?"

Martha looked at him over the rim of her spoon, then laughed, properly laughed, the sound bright enough to make the room larger.

"You poor sheltered thing. Yes. A bit. If the potato's wrong."

"Or if the cook is wrong," Tallow muttered.

"The cook is always wrong, according to someone," Prue said.

"According to evidence," Tallow said.

The talk moved sideways the way talk did when nobody was trying to win it. Martha described her father's habit of cooking in one sock and one slipper because he never noticed when laundry baskets ate the other pair. Prue said her mother overboiled every green vegetable in existence and had therefore ruined saltless food for her on principle. Tallow said a school full of adolescents could empty six bread bins in an evening and still look as if it had been starved by policy. Martha asked if there was any crackling left from upstairs. Tallow said there had been and now there was not and stared at her until she stopped smiling quite so hard.

Owen let the voices pass over him. He answered when spoken to. He ate more than he had meant to. He watched steam run up the windows and vanish. The kitchen had its own weather and its own law. Under the table his shoes were damp from the thaw coming off them. Somewhere below the flags, and too near now that he knew the way, the anchor stone sat in its chamber.

Knowing that had altered the room. Every scrape of bench and clink of spoon seemed to happen over something buried and waiting. He kept having to drag his attention back up to the soup, the clove-stuck oranges, Tallow muttering over the cutlery as if that were the whole problem in the world.

Tallow came back with the mince pies after all, one on each chipped plate, and set them down without admitting that he had planned to do so.

"There," he said. "If anyone says the kitchens are ungenerous, Tallow will require names and dates."

Martha bit into hers at once and hissed through her teeth when the filling burned her mouth.

"Serves you right," Prue said.

"I'm hungry, not strategic."

"A distinction many movements fail to maintain."

"Listen to yourself," Martha said. "You've spent too long with catalogues."

Prue smiled into the pastry. The smile lasted half a second.

"Did Miri come down last week?" Martha asked Tallow abruptly. "The little Ravenclaw one. The one who used to nick the sugared orange peel from the tray when she thought you weren't looking."

The change in the room was small enough that no sound announced it. Tallow's hand stopped on the back of Martha's bench. Prue lowered her eyes to her plate.

"No," Tallow said after a moment. "Miss Kell had not been in the kitchens since the train came back."

"She's not in her dorm either," Prue said. Her voice went flat; she set each word down separately. "Her bed's stripped. Her trunk's gone."

Martha swallowed, too fast this time. "Did someone fetch her?"

Prue gave a little helpless lift of one shoulder. "If they did, they didn't sign the night ledger."

Tallow resumed moving. He picked up the empty soup bowls one by one though nobody had quite finished.

"The little miss left two gloves in the warming cupboard in November," he said. "Green ones with the thumbs stretched. Tallow kept them back in case. They're still there."

No one said anything to that. Across the room the other elves kept working. A pan clattered. Somebody laughed again, not having heard this end of the table or having heard and declined to carry it.

Martha worried at the crust of her pie until it broke apart under her fingers.

"Hester's brother didn't come back either," she said. "At least, not after the first week. They told the table he'd got dragon pox, which was stupid because Hester said her family doesn't even keep a kettle hot unless they have visitors. Nobody with dragon pox in that house would survive long enough to infect anyone."

Prue made a noise that might have been a laugh in another month. This time it snagged in her throat.

"That's very nearly medical reasoning," she said.

"It's kitchen reasoning. Better science."

Owen looked from one of them to the other. The names arrived without enough purchase. He could summon scraps: a small Ravenclaw girl with sugar on her mouth once, maybe Miri, and some boy Martha had pointed out in the corridor for keeping contraband Gobstones in his sock. Not enough to say anything that would not sound borrowed. Silence lengthened. He reached for the plainest fact he had and heard too late how badly it would fit.

"A lot of people go home over Christmas," he said, because silence had begun to seem like an accusation.

He heard the inadequacy of it before the others did. Or perhaps at the same time.

Martha stared. "Owen."

"I only mean we don't know. Some of that's ordinary. The trains, and families, and-"

Prue set her spoon down with care. "That wasn't the thing she meant. And no, before you start narrowing it, I heard you."

He looked at her.

"I know," he said. "I just meant it isn't necessarily all the same thing."

"You're still doing it," Prue said. "Taking the worst part and making it paperwork."

"People aren't categories," Martha said. It came out tired rather than fierce.

Tallow gathered the plates with rigid care, setting one atop the next so softly the china barely clicked. His ears had flattened back a little.

"Young sir," he said without looking at Owen, "there's room in a house for uncertainty and still no room at table."

That ought to have embarrassed Owen into silence. Instead it made him want to defend the principle, which was worse.

"I'm not saying it doesn't matter."

"All right," Martha said at once. "Don't."

She bent over her ruined pie and ate the rest in two practical bites as if the conversation had not happened. Prue took off her spectacles and wiped them with the least greasy corner of her napkin, though there was nothing on the lenses Owen could see. Tallow carried dishes away and began, with unnecessary energy, to inspect the cutlery drawer again.

The meal went on, which felt stranger than a row would have. Martha asked whether anyone had seen snow settle on the greenhouses. Prue said only on the north side and only badly. Tallow complained that students tracked salt over the flagstones and then blamed the floor for being gritty. Owen answered a question about whether the fifth-years were still being made to polish trophy cases with toothbrushes. He said yes. Martha said bastards. Prue said yes, that too.

After that, each new subject came with a beat's delay, as if somebody had to lift it over the thing he'd dropped in the middle of the table.

When they were done, or near enough, Prue folded her napkin around the remains of her pie crust and stood.

"I've got to get back before my dorm decides I've defected to domestic service," she said.

"Leave the plate," Tallow told her. "If anyone is defecting it won't be with crockery."

"I make no promises."

She slid her spectacles back on, gathered her scarf, and looked at Owen directly.

"If we talk tomorrow, don't do that again," she said. "Don't make it sound tidy just because it helps you think."

Owen opened his mouth.

"No," Prue said. "I know it helps you think. I'm saying I can't listen to it when it's people."

"Good night," she said.

"Night," Owen said.

Martha said, "See you tomorrow?"

"Probably. Unless the library collapses from sentiment."

It was a decent joke. On another evening Martha would have thrown something small at her for it. Tonight she only nodded. Prue left. Cold knifed in with the opening door and was gone again.

Martha stayed seated another half minute, rolling the stem of an empty spoon between her fingers.

"Walk up with me," she said at last.

Owen said yes before he considered whether he wanted to.

Tallow handed Martha a square of paper with a mince pie in it and, after a moment's thought, handed Owen one as well.

"Take your scarves," he said. "And if either of you pockets so much as a teaspoon, Tallow will know."

"You always say that."

"And I'm always right eventually."

* * *

They climbed out of the kitchens into the narrower passage where the heat gave up quickly. The stone there held yesterday's cold and intended to keep it. Orange peel and roasting fat fell away behind them. Ahead lay the dark service corridor toward the Hufflepuff cellar, lit every twenty feet by lamps with mean little blue flames. Martha wrapped the pie in both hands for warmth before taking a bite.

For a while they just walked. Their footsteps had a different sound out here, thinner and more solitary than the kitchen clatter. From somewhere above came a burst of song from the Great Hall, late and ragged and overforced. It sounded like people trying to prove to one another that the season still existed.

"He's right about the sprouts," Martha said with her mouth half full. "My dad puts too many in."

"Apparently your whole family cooks by provocation."

"Yes." She swallowed. "Works well till it doesn't."

They turned into the lower corridor where narrow windows looked out at black grounds dusted grey with old snow. The glass had filmed over at the corners. Someone had scratched a star into one pane and then tried to rub it out again.

Martha slowed. "I got a letter this morning," she said.

Owen looked at her. "From home?"

"Obviously from home. Nobody else sends letters that smell of paraffin and dog."

"Right."

She picked pastry off the paper with her thumbnail.

"Dad wrote about the weather for most of it," she said. "The canal freezing at the edges. Mrs Greeley's hens not laying. My cousin taking a shovel to the privy step because of the ice. Then two lines about me not to worry and to eat enough if the school was feeding us. Which sounds funny till you remember who's in charge of the school."

"Letters are being watched," Owen said. "He was probably being careful."

"I know he was being careful."

Martha let the words sit between them. Her jaw worked once. She kept her eyes on the window as they passed, her own reflection gliding beside them in warped black patches.

"That's not the difficult bit," she said. "The difficult bit is that I couldn't tell whether he was being careful because he's frightened or because he's always careful now. Those are different things and they look exactly the same on paper."

Owen looked down at the grease spotting the paper round his pie. His mind went at once to censors, delays, who handled which sack of post on what day. That was the sort of answer he knew how to build. It was no use to her at all, and he felt that before he opened his mouth.

He chose it anyway.

"You probably can't tell from one letter," he said. "Not on its own."

Martha laughed once, under her breath.

"See, there it is."

"What?"

"That." She turned to face him properly now, back against the cold wall. "I say I'm scared and you start trimming the edges off it till it'll fit in a notebook."

"I didn't mean-"

"I know you didn't mean anything by it. That's the problem."

The lamp nearest them guttered blue. Owen could hear the faint hiss from it. At the far end of the corridor the barrel-door entrance to Hufflepuff sat in shadow, iron hoops catching a little light.

"I'm only saying one letter isn't enough to conclude-" he started.

"Conclude?" Martha said. "For fuck's sake."

She pushed away from the wall, then stopped herself from walking off and came back a pace as if sheer fairness compelled her.

"I'm not asking you to conclude anything," she said. "I'm asking whether it gets to you. Properly. The missing people, the letters, the way everyone keeps talking lower every week like if we whisper enough none of it will be true."

Owen looked at her. There were flour smudges on her sleeve from the kitchen table. One bit of pastry clung to the scarf at her throat. Her plait had come half undone, strands sticking to her cheek where the cold had dampened them. She looked fifteen and frightened and furious at herself for letting the frightened part show.

Of course it got to him. Amycus got to him. Martha nearly taking curses in November got to him. The undercroft got to him. It all got to him so steadily that he had built a habit of sorting it: what could be answered, what could only be endured, what had to be put aside till he could function again. The habit had become quicker than speech.

"It does," he said, and heard how bloodless that sounded. He tried again. "I just can't, if I let every- sorry. That's not what I mean. I mean if I start treating every gap and rumour as the whole shape of things, I can't think straight enough to be any use."

Martha closed her eyes.

"Any use to what?"

He should have lied. Not cleanly and not well, just enough. To us, perhaps. To the school. To you. Instead he hesitated. The hesitation itself answered.

When she opened her eyes again, whatever softness had been left in them had gone flat.

"Right," she said.

"Martha-"

"No, I heard it."

"You didn't, not exactly."

"Then exactly what did I hear?"

He had nothing fit for air. The corridor waited with her. The blue lamp hissed. From very far away, or very deep below, the castle gave one of its settling groans.

"There's something I'm trying to hold onto," he said at last. "And if I lose the thread of it because I'm- because everything here is loud all the time-"

"Everything here," Martha said slowly, "is loud because people are getting hurt."

"I know."

"Do you?"

He flinched, not in body so much as in attention. It felt like missing a stair in the dark and only learning it from the jolt in the knee. She saw that too.

"You know what's stupid?" Martha said. She looked away, out through the filmy window where the grounds were only a deeper black. "I keep thinking if they came for me I'd want thirty seconds to make the bed. Just that. Pull it straight. Hide the socks. I know that's idiotic. I know if they come they're not going to stand there while I tuck corners. But I keep thinking it anyway."

She rubbed the side of her thumb over the paper that had held the pie until it tore. Tiny flakes drifted down to the flags.

"And when Hester's bed got stripped and Miri's trunk vanished and my dad writes about hens as if that's enough information for a person to live on, I think maybe the whole world is turning into that. Beds made for nobody. Meals for whoever's left. I just wanted-" She stopped and shook her head. "I wanted you not to answer it like a timetable."

She said it without looking at him. Owen stared at the shredded paper in her hand.

He could have said he was sorry. He was sorry. But even now the apology kept trying to become a clarification: sorry, but there are other pressures; sorry, but if you could see; sorry, but. Every version betrayed itself before it reached his mouth.

He managed, "I don't know how to do both."

Martha let out a breath that clouded in front of her.

"That's at least honest," she said. "Miserable, but honest."

They stood in silence. In the kitchens below, someone dragged a crate across stone. The sound came up through the floor faint and grating. Owen thought absurdly of Tallow lining up spoons in their drawer, counting what the room still possessed.

"That wasn't just the letter," Martha said.

He looked up. "What?"

"In there as well. You heard Prue." She made a small, irritated motion around his face, his shoulders, perhaps his whole person. "You stand there and you are here, technically, and then after about ten seconds you go somewhere else. Not physically. Worse."

He wanted to deny that. He wanted, more secretly, to ask whether Prue had said anything specific. Both impulses were mean.

"I've just been tired."

Martha gave him such a flat look that the lie died where it was.

"Fine," she said. "Be tired, then."

She folded the torn paper into a square so small it disappeared in her fist.

"I keep bringing the wrong things to you," she said. "That's my mistake."

"Martha, that's not-"

"No. Leave it."

She tucked her scarf up under her chin with two hard little jerks, the way she did when both hands wanted a job. Then she stepped around him and started down the corridor toward the barrel door.

Owen moved after her once, then stopped. "Will I see you in the morning?"

She did not turn round.

"You'll see me somewhere," she said.

She gave him only a small lift of the hand, already half on her way to the barrel. At the Hufflepuff door she knocked the right pattern and slipped through when the wood swung aside. The door shut behind her with its usual thick, stupid warmth.

Owen stood in the blue lamp light with the untouched mince pie cooling in his hand.

The corridor smelled of old stone, paraffin, and the spiced fruit Tallow had sent upstairs in paper. Grease bled through the wrapping into his palm until the burn began to throb. One word kept coming back in Martha's voice. Timetable. He looked at the shut barrel door and thought, stupidly, that he could still knock.

He stayed where he was. By the time he made himself turn away, the paper had gone translucent in his fist and the burn in his palm was throbbing under it, a hard little rhythm easier to follow than the thought of knocking.


# Chapter 9

The same three crooked marks in Owen's notebook had started to look like an insult. He kept curling his toes inside his socks, trying to trick some warmth back into them, while he sat at the end of a scrubbed kitchen table with his ash-linen notebook under one hand and a heel of bread under the other, pretending to copy Herbology notes and staring at the same margin for the fourth time. The kitchens were an easy place to disappear into noise and steam, but February had turned the castle's cold into one more school habit: it found the wet band above a sock, the strip of wrist between cuff and skin, the spot at the back of the knee where stone got in and stayed. Even here there was no proper defeating it, only heat from ovens on one side and the draught from the service arches on the other, as if Hogwarts meant to keep every comfort half-argued.

The marks had come out of the Grinlow Leaves that morning only after he pressed his burned palm flat across the margin and waited through the hot thread the page always drew up into his wrist. South warming passage, fourth hinge from scullery arch. The English gloss in his own notebook looked childish beside the older notation, but he had not found a better one.

Tallow was on a stool by the nearest range, sorting spoons into piles with the concentration of a man handling bones. He muttered numbers under his breath, stopped to sniff one of the spoons in suspicion, then polished it on his apron anyway. The kitchens smelled of onions, wet stone, yeast, and a pan of something sweet catching at the edge.

Martha came in through the fruit portrait with sleet in her hair and a tea towel over one shoulder. Her dark plait had half come loose. One of her knuckles was split and freshly mended, the skin still shiny. She had a crate of potatoes braced against one hip as if she had forgotten it was there.

"You're hiding in plain sight again," she said.

Owen looked up. "I'm in the kitchens."

"Yes. Exactly." She set the crate down by Tallow's stool. "Have you eaten?"

"Some."

She pushed the bread heel nearer him with two fingers. "That doesn't count."

Tallow clicked his tongue without looking round. "Miss Sorrell is correct, and Tallow dislikes repeating an obvious fact in his own kitchen."

Martha pulled a face at the range, affectionate enough that Tallow allowed it. Then she turned back to Owen, and the quick ordinary irritation in her expression thinned into purpose that left no room for the thing still wrong between them. "Need you tonight." She said it in the tone that meant the job came first and other damage could wait.

He glanced at the notebook before he meant to. Martha noticed everything practical and most things that were not. Her eyes went there and back.

"For what?"

"Lower west corridor. After curfew." She hooked the tea towel into her waistband. "We've got two first-years stowed off the staff lavatory, and Hester attached to the business because she won't stop volunteering. They bolted after Dark Arts this afternoon and somebody needs them out before Amycus does his late sweep. Neville's putting a noise together at one end, we move them through at the other, and then everybody gets downstairs before anyone important starts feeling clever."

Owen kept his face still. So it was lower west. The service line in the margin note had to be part of that run. The burn in his right palm gave one small hot throb, enough to make him close his hand.

"Why me?"

Martha stared at him as if the question itself were rude. "Because you know the kitchen side better than half the people doing this. Because you can get through a narrow gap without sounding like a wardrobe falling over. Because there are three twelve-year-olds in a room that smells of bleach and mop water and fear, and I'm short a person." She wiped her wet hand on the towel. "And because I asked you, Owen. That one was meant to matter a bit."

He took a bite of the bread to buy time. It was still warm in the middle. Across the table Tallow lifted a spoon, frowned at a bent handle, and put it in a different pile as if the castle's fate might yet be decided by cutlery.

"What sort of noise?" Owen said.

"The ordinary sort. Armor, lavatory, corridor mess, a few things where they shouldn't be." Martha leaned against the table. "Nothing grand. We don't need grand. We need four minutes and nobody fainting."

"If it's only that, why short a person?"

"Because people keep getting caught, or hexed, or moved to detentions, or sent home in bits." She said it flatly, not for effect. "Because one of the sixth-years from Ravenclaw can't put weight on his foot after Tuesday. Because normal reasons."

Owen nodded. He could already see the corridor in the notebook's margin, not the real one but the older line beneath it, founder stone dressed over and over under newer doors. If he went with Martha, he could test the warming passage without having to invent a reason for being out after curfew. That was the gamble: he would be using a rescue as cover for old work in a corridor made of doors, stalls, latches, and one service press the castle might read as a single run.

Martha was still watching him, though not hard, not yet. There was more hope in it than she would have liked him to notice.

"All right," he said.

Her shoulders changed first, dropping by about half an inch. Then the rest of her face followed. "Right. Good."

"I'm not promising I'm useful."

"No, I've met you before." For a second she nearly smiled. "Just turn up and do what you're told. That'll already be an improvement."

Tallow slid off the stool and crossed to a shelf without wasting movement. He handed Martha a folded cloth parcel that had not been there a moment ago, or at least not where Owen had seen it. The parcel smelled of cheese and pepper.

"For the little ones," he said. "And if anyone puts muddy feet on the west passage wall again, Tallow will know."

"They won't." Martha took the parcel with more care than she had shown the potatoes. "Thanks."

Tallow gave Owen a look from under singed brows. "The drain by the fourth arch is backing up. Mind where you stand if you don't want the castle on your stockings."

The fourth arch.

Owen said, too quickly, "Which fourth arch?"

Martha's eyes narrowed a fraction. Tallow only sniffed.

"From the scullery side," the elf said. "The one that always smells of cold iron when the weather turns. If you had been listening to the house instead of your notebook, you would know it."

"I was listening."

"Mm."

Martha picked up the potato crate again. "Second bell after lights-out. Old Charms storeroom, the one off lower west. Don't be late."

She started for the portrait, then stopped and looked back. Her voice, when she spoke, was lighter on purpose. "And do try not to bring that face with you."

"What face?"

"That one. The one that says every other person's emergency is interrupting a timetable only you can see."

He opened his mouth, shut it again, and Martha went through the portrait before he found an answer that was not either defensive or true.

Tallow resumed sorting spoons. The kitchen noise filled back in around the gap she had left: pans shifting, water hissing on hot iron, some distant rattle from the scullery where another elf was losing patience with a tray.

Owen put his hand over the notebook until the page stopped looking exposed. The bread had gone cooler. The line in the margin had not changed. South warming passage, fourth hinge from scullery arch.

He told himself he could do both and shut the notebook as if a shut cover counted as discipline.

* * *

The old Charms storeroom had not held charms equipment in years. It held broken desks, two boxes of warped copybooks, a coat stand missing three hooks, and eight students trying not to breathe loudly.

Neville stood in the middle of them with a bit of chalk and the back of a cabinet door laid across two chairs. He had the corridor drawn on it in blunt white lines: the stair turn at one end, the staff lavatory and first classroom opposite it, the fruit portrait halfway down in its recess, the second classroom facing the portrait, then the service arches and press at the scullery end. He looked more tired every time Owen saw him now. Lost sleep had pulled some of the solidity out of his face, and the bruise along his jaw had gone the stale yellow of an injury nobody had time to let fade.

"Again," Neville said as Owen came in. "We're not here to win a war. We're here to get the two first-years out of the lavatory classroom before Amycus finds them there. If anything goes wrong, the younger ones go first and the rest of us become scenery."

One of the younger ones, a second-year girl with one mitten safety-pinned to her sleeve so she would not lose it, raised her hand without waiting to be asked. "What if the scenery's already on fire?"

Martha, crouched by a canvas bag of junk at the wall, said, "Then run round it, Hester."

Hester nodded as if this was useful and final.

Beside the cabinet, the two third-years waiting to relay the younger ones stood with a folded blanket each and contraband bread tucked into a flour sack. One had flour on his sleeve from the kitchens. The other was barefoot and holding a tin of dittany against his chest like a rescued pet.

Len, lanky and hollow-eyed, kept winding and unwinding a bit of string round his wrist until the skin went pink. By the door Jo worried at the sticking-plaster round both thumbs with her teeth, then caught herself and shoved her hands into her sleeves.

Owen had not expected blankets and bread. That, more than anything, made the thing feel like Martha's work as much as Neville's.

Neville tapped the chalk map. "Len, stop worrying that string and trip the armor by the stair when Amycus comes round the turn. Hester, you pull the spoon from the cistern brace only when Martha says, not before. Don't improve on the plan because you suddenly feel inspired. Jo, keep those bandaged thumbs out of sight and stay on the portrait. You two wait in the second classroom opposite her till Martha brings the little ones across, then straight through the fruit passage and down. Nobody bunches in the doorway. Once they're through, everyone peels off separate."

He looked at Owen.

"East end," Neville said. "You keep the service press under the fourth arch clear and the scullery side dark. If somebody comes up from the service arches early, you cough twice and we all pretend we're idiots for another reason."

"Right," Owen said.

Neville held his stare for another beat, checking for whatever it was he had wanted from Owen back in the Room and had still not found. Then he went back to the cabinet door.

Martha lifted things out of the canvas bag and handed them round as she talked: a spoon bent nearly in half, string, a bottle of ink, three Filibuster scraps no bigger than cigarettes, a rag that smelled of vinegar, two rusted screws, one lump of soap shaved into slivers. It was school sabotage: cupboard scrap, cleaning bits, and the mean little ingenuity of children who knew exactly what the castle left lying about.

"The armor only needs to start moving," she said. "It doesn't need to kill anybody, so don't overdo it. The ink goes under the lavatory seat, not in the bowl. If it goes in the bowl, it just looks sad. Jo's got the portrait. The relay room stays shut till I've got the little ones over to it. Neville's got the stair. Owen's got the east end." She glanced up at him. "Have you got the east end?"

"Yes."

"Good."

The wrongness began before he left the storeroom. It was not much, only a tug through the burn in his palm and a low tremor coming up through the soles of his shoes. The lower west corridor was old enough, then. It was old in the useful way.

When they filed out two at a time and flattened themselves into the stone shadows, the corridor felt shorter than it had on Neville's cabinet door and far more crowded. The walls sweated faintly. Six doors crowded thirty feet of passage if he counted the lavatory stalls. One suit of armor stood by the lavatory corner with a dent in its breastplate and a halberd none of the original owners could have meant it to keep. The other stood opposite the service press at Owen's end, smaller and more decorative, its visor wired on years ago and badly polished.

The fourth arch from the scullery side smelled, exactly as Tallow had said, of cold iron. It was exactly the fourth arch. Owen stopped under it as if he were only checking sightlines. The service press set into the wall there was newer oak on older stone, the frame built into a seam that went down farther than the joinery had any right to know. Someone had patched the lower hinge in brass. The patch sat over scratch marks that were not accident.

His palm burn woke all the way.

Neville had the stair. Len crouched by the large armor at the lavatory corner, string ready in both hands. Hester was on one knee by the cistern brace outside the first classroom door. Jo had flattened herself into the portrait recess. Across from her, behind the second classroom door, the relay third-years waited with their blankets and bread. Martha stood between the two rooms, ready to pull the first-years from the first and hand them across to the second. Everyone else's attention was fixed where it ought to have been.

Owen took the notebook from inside his jumper and opened it against the wall. The corridor torchlight was bad for the older marks, but he had already copied what he needed. Three small signs, a narrower joining line, then the human note beside them so he would not lie to himself later about what he had intended: admit on first pressure, suppress complaint. He hesitated just long enough to know exactly what he was choosing. Then he rubbed soot and tallow together with spit on the pad of his thumb, pressed the paste into the groove beneath the brass hinge-plate, and traced the signs no bigger than nail crescents. The stone answered at once, too quickly, with that deep unpleasant sense of a room taking notice.

He froze.

Nothing else happened yet.

From the far end Martha gave him the smallest possible jerk of the chin, telling him to get into position.

Owen slid the notebook away and put his fingers on the service press handle. It loosened under his hand so beautifully that for a fraction of a second he was stupid enough to feel pleased. There was no squeal and no resistance. The latch yielded like cloth.

Then, three doors down, the first classroom latch clicked.

He looked up.

The disused classroom opposite the lavatory opened itself an inch.

At the lavatory the nearest stall banged back hard enough to hit tile.

The little decorative armor at his shoulder lifted its visor on a wire snap that sounded exactly like a mouse trap going off.

Owen took his hand off the press. "Oh, for fuck's sake," he whispered.

* * *

The corridor started coming apart in small ugly stages.

The first classroom door that had opened an inch flung itself full wide. A mop bucket inside toppled over and rolled into the passage. Hester jerked round toward the sound, lost hold of the spoon brace in her other hand, and the lavatory cistern gave a liquid cough before she had touched it properly. At Owen's end, the service press swung open to blackness. The small armor suit, visor still jerked up on its wire, took one mincing step forward and discharged the Dungbombs wired into its greave directly into its own feet.

The smell hit half a second later.

"No," Owen said, already moving.

He reached for the first classroom door. It shut under his hand, then sprang back open again as soon as he let go. The pressure in the stone thickened, spreading under the flags and up the frames. The press, the stalls, the armor visor, even the cistern had taken the same instruction. He hadn't marked a hinge. He'd marked the whole passage.

At the lavatory end someone yelped. It was the younger ones. Owen turned just in time to see the first classroom where Martha had hidden them spit its door open so hard the handle cracked the plaster. The boy with the thread-mended spectacles stumbled out clutching his sandwich. Behind him the boy in the too-big jumper slid in spilled water and nearly lost both shoes.

"Move," Martha snapped. "No, this way. Toward the portrait. Don't stand there looking at it."

Neville came off the stair in three strides. "Kids first. Go."

Then the toilets joined in.

Every cistern along the lavatory wall gave up on containment in the same ugly instant. Chains rattled against porcelain. Lids jumped. Brown-grey water surged over the back lip of the nearest one and slapped across the floor, carrying ink from the rigged seat with it in long purple-black fans. The stall doors slammed open and shut, open and shut, never settling. Somewhere behind tile a pipe knocked hard enough to shake grit out of the mortar.

The large armor suit by the corner woke under Len's string before Len had pulled it, because the changed corridor was already treating any suggestion of passage as enough. It lurched forward with murderous dignity, halberd lowering, and walked straight into the lavatory doorframe. The blow tore the halberd free, spun the suit sideways, and sent the cistern brace Hester had been meant to use skittering under Neville's feet.

For one impossible second the whole business was merely humiliating: filth, ink, armor, children in wet socks, doors refusing to stay where doors belonged. One of the first-years laughed in pure fright and then burst into tears in the same breath. Hester, still on her knees, shouted, "I didn't pull it yet," still trying to make the point matter.

Then the second classroom opposite the portrait flew open and exposed the rest of the route.

The two third-years who had been waiting there with blankets and contraband bread stared into the corridor, stunned, their cover gone. One of them had flour on his sleeve from the kitchens. The other was barefoot, holding a tin of dittany tight against his chest. Any hope of keeping the operation small vanished with the open doorway.

"Shut them," Neville said.

Owen had his wand out already. "Colloportus."

The spell came off the wand sideways, a thin spray of red sparks that stitched harmlessly across the opposite wall and knocked a framed fire notice crooked. His ordinary magic never failed in large dignified ways. It spat sideways and left him kneeling in dirty water while the corridor got worse.

"Owen," Martha snapped.

"I know."

He dropped to one knee in the dirty water and went for the stone with his bare hand instead. A countermark might have worked if he had had a clear surface, if the corridor had been still, if First Art respected panic less than it did. The flagstones throbbed under his palm with the same sick answering pressure he had felt in the greenhouse and under the undercroft arch. There was too much motion and too many interrupted lines. His own pulse kept slipping into the shape.

He scratched the first half of a narrowing sign with his thumbnail. The service press beside him answered by swinging wider, and a rush of colder air breathed out of the dark beyond it. For a bright contemptible instant he could smell deep stone and old damp below the kitchens. The route had opened.

Martha had one arm round the crying first-year and the other shoved against the first classroom door, trying to force it closed by body weight alone. "Neville, take Hester. Go. Go now."

Neville did not waste words on objection. He caught Hester by the sleeve, snatched the sandwich boy with his other hand, and drove them toward the fruit portrait and the blown-open relay room opposite it. Len, swearing, wrestled the big armor suit by the halberd stump long enough to send it crashing into the opposite wall. The smaller decorative suit took another two short furious steps and planted both feet in Dungbomb sludge, where it stayed shuddering.

The corridor might still have been saved into something only ridiculous if Amycus had taken even thirty seconds longer.

He did not.

His boots hit the stair turn with speed enough to show he had been running. He came in half belted, robe collar crooked, one bootlace tucked into the top of his boot instead of tied. There were tiny shaving cuts on his jaw and the sweet rotten smell of drink under whatever mint he had sucked on to hide it. From the stair he had the whole run at once: wrecked lavatory on one side, the second classroom blown open opposite the fruit portrait halfway down, and at the far scullery end Owen on one knee beside the service press with cold air still breathing from it.

The corridor looked back at him in sewage, ink, and cheap sabotage.

"What the fuck," he said, almost admiringly.

Then he lifted his wand and blasted the large armor suit apart.

Steel rang off stone. A greave skimmed past Owen's shoulder and hit the wall hard enough to take plaster with it. The first-year with the spectacles screamed. Martha shoved him bodily toward the portrait and turned back because she was Martha and there were still too many bodies in the corridor.

"Run," she said to the third-years in the exposed room. "Now. Move."

Amycus saw her say it. That was enough.

He crossed the distance in four heavy strides and caught the back of her robe before she could turn. The cloth bit into her throat. She went backward, boots skidding in lavatory water, and hit the tiles on one hip so hard Owen's own stomach clenched.

"You," Amycus said, delighted now. "There you are."

Martha twisted, got one foot under herself, and nearly made it. Amycus kicked the back of her knee. She went down again. The corridor seemed to recoil around the impact, doors still banging and water still spilling and all the while the actual human damage narrowing to a single body on stone.

Owen pushed up from the floor, filthy hand slipping on the flag. He could say it. He could say enough. Not all of it, not the Leaves, not the undercroft, but enough to pull Amycus's attention off her and onto the thing he had actually done.

He opened his mouth.

Amycus dragged Martha upright by her plait.

Owen did not speak.

* * *

Amycus held her there long enough for the corridor to quiet by degrees around them. One stall door still knocked. Water kept running somewhere behind the wall. From the other end came the muffled slap of the fruit portrait closing and the hard-breathed retreat of the children Neville had got out. The operation had shrunk to aftermath and selection, to deciding who would be left with the blame and who would not.

Martha's face had gone white under the wet. Strands of hair had pulled free from the plait and stuck across her mouth. She spat one away and looked straight at Amycus, breathing through her nose in small controlled pulls.

"Funny little mess," Amycus said. He was breathing hard through his nose. "Funny little rebels. Doors opening all over, piss everywhere, children out after curfew. You people do like making work."

He wrenched her head back by the hair and looked past her at the corridor as if the answer might be written on the wall. His gaze snagged on Owen.

"Phipps."

Owen did not move.

"Still here," Amycus said. "That was my first surprise tonight. Come on then. Use your eyes for once. Which one set it off?"

Owen's notebook felt enormous inside his jumper. The chalk paste under his nails had gone gritty with dirty water. He could still smell the colder air from the opened service press behind him, faint now under sewage and scorch. The route was still there. If he gave Amycus the whole thing, the route would go with it. The manuscript would go with it. The hidden work below the kitchens would go with it. Everything that mattered at the scale he had finally begun to understand would go with it.

Everything that mattered at the scale in front of him was in Amycus's fist.

"I don't know," Owen said.

Amycus barked a laugh. "You don't know. You were standing in the middle of it."

"I saw the armor go first."

It was the most literal answer. It was also true.

Amycus's mouth curled. He hated students who thought a tidy true sentence might count as obedience. He let go of Martha's hair only to hit her across the mouth with the back of his hand. Her head snapped sideways. Blood appeared immediately, thin and startlingly bright against all the dirty water.

"Wrong answer," he said.

Owen flinched half a step without meaning to. Amycus saw and enjoyed it.

"Ah," he said softly. "So you do know something."

Martha made a short rough sound that might have been a laugh if it had not come through blood. "He doesn't know his own timetable half the time."

Amycus turned and drove her down to her knees with a hand on the shoulder. Broken porcelain from one of the cistern lids crackled under her robes. She sucked air so sharply her shoulders jumped but she did not cry out. He set his boot on her left hand and fed weight into it a little at a time. Broken porcelain clicked under her palm. He was showing her exactly where the break would happen.

"Let's try the girl," he said. "Who else is here?"

"No one."

He put more weight on the hand. Owen heard the tiny ugly grind of skin against porcelain before he heard Martha's breath go wrong.

"Who else."

"No one."

Amycus looked up at Owen again as if inviting him into the lesson. The corridor light shone wetly on his shaving cuts. "See, Phipps, this is why everybody hates group work. One stupid child decides to decorate the school and then another one has to explain it with her fingers."

Owen said nothing.

He could feel the pressure of the old line under the flags, still faintly active, still waiting for a cleaner command. If he dropped to the stone again and tried to change it, he would do it under Amycus's eyes with no preparation and no guarantee except that the castle was already wounded enough to answer too loudly.
