If he confessed, Amycus would have the corridor and then, very quickly, anything else Owen had touched. If he did neither, Martha would be the one taken.

He chose neither loudly enough for anyone to mistake it. He simply stayed where he was.

Martha's eyes found his for one second. There was pain in them, and fury, and an exhausted disbelief that was somehow worse than either. Then Amycus hauled her up again and the look broke.

"Right," Amycus said. "You can be tomorrow's example. We've all earned that, haven't we."

He shoved her toward the stair. She caught herself on the wall with her good hand, smearing blood and dirty water over the stone. Amycus yanked her onward by the arm.

"Anybody I find out after curfew from this minute," he called over his shoulder, "can join her and bring their own teeth in a bag."

The threat ran down the corridor into the walls. Then his boots and Martha's stumbling steps receded toward the turn and were gone.

For a moment nobody moved.

The little armor suit finally settled with a metallic sigh. Water dripped from the broken cistern in fat slow taps. Owen could hear people breathing in the relay classroom, behind the wall, inside the whole damaged hush of the thing.

Neville came back first from the portrait passage, wet to the knees and breathing through his mouth. He took in the corridor in one sweep, ending on Owen. Len appeared behind him carrying the tin of dittany that had survived somehow. Hester, pale and furious at herself, clutched her pinned mitten with the free hand like it might keep the rest of her attached.

"The little ones are down," Neville said. He looked at the blood on the wall, then at Owen again. "Was that you?"

Owen knew better than to ask which part.

"Part of it," he said.

Len swore under his breath.

Neville's face did not change much. That was the worst of it. If he had shouted, Owen might have answered back, or at least known where the line was. Instead Neville nodded once and filed the fact away with the rest of the damage.

"For what?" Neville asked.

Owen said, "I was opening the press."

"For us."

The opened service press behind him breathed cold from the dark.

Owen did not answer in time.

Neville looked away first. "Right."

That single word carried more rearrangement than a speech would have. He crouched by the broken cistern, snapped a bit of plaster off the floor, and pressed it to the running crack because damage still deserved practical attention even in the middle of betrayal.

"Len, get the wall. Hester, help him. Jo can come back up for the bucket. Nobody says press, sabotage, corridor, any of it if they're spoken to." He stood and faced Owen again. "And you don't come near another job of mine if you can't tell which side of it you're on."

Martha's blood was drying already in a crooked handprint where she had caught herself.

Owen said, because it was weak and because it was true, "I didn't mean this."

Neville's mouth went tight with something flatter than anger. "No. That's the problem."

He turned away and started giving people tasks. Hester fetched rags from the classroom. Len scraped soot and dung off the armor's decorative foot with two fingers, his mouth turned away from the smell. Jo slipped back through the portrait with a bucket and would not meet Owen's eye. The corridor filled with work, quick and low-voiced and competent.

No one asked Owen to do any of it.

He stood by the still-open press with dirty water drying on his socks and the notebook heavy under his jumper while the others mopped, lifted, wiped, hid, and repaired around him. The cold from the service dark kept touching his ankle. Somewhere down the stair Amycus shouted, and farther off than that, almost below hearing, Martha made no sound at all.

By the time the last of them went back down to the kitchens in twos and threes, nobody had asked Owen to carry so much as a bucket, and the omission already felt like the start of a rule.


# Chapter 10

The scullery off the kitchens smelled of wet stone, old onions, and lamp oil going sharp in the heat. Midnight had come and gone, and Owen still had the Grinlow Leaves spread on an upturned flour bin because it was flatter than the table, and flatter mattered. The page he wanted would hold for perhaps six breaths if his burned right palm pinned the lower margin and the lamp sat exactly level with the nick in the opposite wall. On the seventh breath the ink went pale and the lines slid back under the vellum as if the manuscript had thought better of showing him anything.

He had been stuck there three nights, which was another way of saying that since the press business nobody downstairs had found a better use for him.

His ash-linen notebook lay open beside it, dense with cramped tries and cross-outs. He had written that the quarter mark repeated at ninety degrees and that the thing answered to stars the way library shelves answered to numbers. He had written that much four times in four different places as if changing where it sat might make it truer.

The kitchens beyond the scullery still muttered at low volume: pan bottoms ringing softly, water thrown into a basin, one of the younger elves laughing and then stopping at once when an older voice shushed him. The ordinary work of the castle went on under the occupation because somebody had to scrub the roasting tins and bank the ovens even when Amycus Carrow was upstairs teaching children to scream properly.

Footsteps came along the service passage, cautious and tired, too heavy for Tallow's quick soft slap.

Prue came in sideways through the narrow door with a roll of transparencies under one arm and a folded bundle of charts clamped to her chest. She had not bothered with gloves. She never did when paper was involved; wool caught on thin pages and drove her mad. Her fingers were pink with cold and already marked with blue ink near the nails. Over her school jumper she wore an old bottle-green cardigan with one wooden replacement button at the throat, and she worried that button between two fingers while she looked at him and the book and did not yet put anything down.

"Neville said you wanted the clear ones," she said.

"I did."

"That was his charitable summary."

Owen nodded once. "I couldn't get the projection to stay put."

She set the bundle on the bin beside the manuscript without sitting. Her spectacles were smudged at one corner. "Martha's still limping."

Owen looked at the page because the page at least obeyed some kind of rule.

"I know," he said.

"Do you?"

"Yes."

She let out a breath through her nose. "Right."

Neither of them said sorry. It would have been too early for a useful apology and too late for a good one.

Tallow slipped in behind her carrying a tray with a dented kettle, three thick cups, and a plate with two heels of bread that had been toasted too long on one side. He had wrapped kitchen string around the handle of the kettle because it had cracked in January and he refused to throw it away on principle. He turned each cup so its chip faced the wall before setting it down, a small private courtesy to damaged things.

"No ink on the bread," he said. "If there is ink on the bread, Tallow is not pretending not to notice."

"Wouldn't dream of it," Prue said.

Tallow gave her a look that suggested her dreams could not be trusted around paper. His singed ear twitched. "Old stone is sharp tonight. If you two are going down, you will step where the light steps, and if one of you falls and cracks a tooth Tallow is not fetching Madam Pomfrey to explain why."

Prue turned to Owen. "Going down where?"

He had meant to say it more carefully. He had meant to ease her to the edge of it, perhaps show her the anchor page first and only then admit that the page now wanted the undercroft more than it wanted the scullery. Instead he heard himself answer in the most literal shape available.

"Below," he said. "The page won't stabilize properly anywhere younger than founder stone."

She watched him for a moment. "And this is the first time you thought to mention there was a below?"

"It's not safe in the sense people usually mean."

"Mm. Helpful distinction."

He nearly started explaining what the distinction actually was, then stopped because that was exactly the sort of answer that made her face go flat like this. Tallow poured tea to spare them from themselves. It was over-brewed and black as old nails.

"Drink first," the elf said. "Thinking goes rotten faster in the cold."

Prue took the nearest cup and sipped. Her whole mouth tightened. "This is vile."

"It is hot," Tallow said. "Not every useful thing needs charm."

That almost got a smile out of her. Almost.

Owen drank because it was there. The tea scalded the back of his throat and made his empty stomach wake up. He had not realized until then that he was shaking a little. Lack of sleep or too much of the book, impossible to tell.

Prue unrolled her transparencies across the flour bin. Thin sheets of smoked glass and marked parchment clicked softly together. She had copied sky grids, declination lines, and an old equatorial projection in a hand so neat it looked engraved until one noticed the cramped corrections at the corners. On top of that she laid a folded tracing of the world, oceans and coastlines reduced to the minimum a pattern needed. She smoothed it flat with the side of her wrist to avoid leaving fingerprints.

"You said projection," she said. "Show me exactly where it slips."

He put his burned palm to the lower margin. The skin there was always hotter than the rest of him now, as if the coffer had left a coal under it. Lines came up through the vellum in a slow black seep: circles within circles, radial marks, four larger sigils that refused at first to mean anything except repetition. Prue leaned in until her shoulder brushed his and held one smoked sheet above the page. The ink steadied. He felt her go still beside him, caught by the sudden technical greed of seeing a system begin to answer.

"There," he said quietly. "Treat the third ring as sky. The geometry starts making sense after that."

"It's sky laid over something else," she said at once. "Hang on."

She shifted the transparency left, then back, then rotated it by the width of two fingernails. The marks on the page snapped into a different relation. What had looked like decorative hatching at the outer rim became latitude bands. The inner knots opened into four anchor points set around a sphere that had not been drawn as a sphere because the manuscript assumed the reader already knew how to unfold it.

Owen had known, roughly, that the table was large. He had not known it was world-sized until Prue's hand made the page admit it.

"Oh," she said.

Her "Oh" came out thin and careful, like a foot finding air where a stair ought to have been.

The ink went pale again. Owen swore under his breath.

"No, wait," Prue said. "Again."

They did it again, and again. Each time the image held a little longer, enough for her to mark one relation on the tracing, enough for him to copy terms that he could half-parse and half only feel in the drag of his burned palm against the vellum: holdfast, veil hinge, southern return, school not by name but by category, schola, rendered through the manuscript's logic as a place where repetition and concentration had been built to stack.

By the fifth attempt his wrist ached and Prue's curls had escaped their tie and were catching lamp glow at the edge of his sight. She smelled of cold wool, library dust, and a cheap soap that left almost no scent at all. Their shoulders stayed pressed together over the bin. The lamp made one small warm pocket in the room, and the work kept drawing them back into it.

"This isn't enough," Owen said. "It keeps degrading."

Prue did not look up from the page. "Then perhaps your mysterious below stops being mysterious now."

Tallow had been standing with his tray like a witness called to the wrong hearing. At that he clicked his tongue, gathered the kettle, and jerked his head toward the dark service slit behind the scullery shelves.

"Bring the lamp," he said. "And don't touch the wall on the narrow turn unless you enjoy blisters in odd shapes."

The passage was too small for dignity. Owen went first with the lamp, the Grinlow Leaves wrapped in oilcloth under his arm and his notebook tucked into his waistband. Prue came after, carrying the transparencies high and flat as if she were transporting surgical instruments through a drain. Tallow led them far enough to show the safe places and then stopped at the last low arch where the air changed.

It always changed there. The warmth of the kitchens fell off, and the old work began. The burn across Owen's right palm tightened as if the skin were shrinking around the scar. The stone seemed to thicken the sound of their breathing until each inhale came back at them from the walls.

Tallow set the kettle down at his feet and rubbed his damp hands hard on his apron. "Further is yours," he said. The formality had come into his voice, which meant he was frightened enough to use manners as a shield. "Tallow will wait where the house still feels like itself."

Prue looked from him to the dark stair beyond. "Does it hurt you?"

He gave a little offended shrug. "Everything hurts somebody if it gets old enough."

Then, less tidily: "It is worse for elves. Mind the third step. It is hollow in the middle."

That, more than any warning, convinced her. She nodded once. "All right."

Owen took her down the stair.

The route curled through the castle's forgotten structure rather than its planned one: under a laundry flue warm as a throat, along a ledge where the wall sweated black, through a slit door whose hinges had fused long ago and now opened only because he had learned where the founder stone had cracked around the pin. Prue did not complain. She touched the places he told her to touch and ducked where he ducked. Once, when the ceiling dropped lower than he remembered, she caught the back of his robe to stop him from moving on too fast.

"You could have mentioned the crawling," she said.

"I forgot that bit."

"That's not reassuring."

The undercroft opened abruptly after all that constriction. After the passage, even this chamber felt like a lung. Damp mineral cold came off the floor. At the center, in its shallow setting, sat the anchor stone: a broad disk of dark founder stone with lines incised so fine they vanished until light hit sideways. The star-table around it had been cut directly into the floor, rings and spokes and notation dimmed by age alone, because almost nobody had stood here for centuries.

Prue stopped just inside the threshold.

The old-work pressure reached her then. He saw it in the minute way her mouth changed shape behind her hand, in the swallow she had to force.

"Is it meant to feel like that?" she asked.

"Yes."

"Good. I hate surprises when they're physical."

He set the lamp on a dry shelf of stone and knelt by the anchor. The chamber answered the manuscript before he touched it. The oilcloth twitched once in his hands, eager and repellent at the same time.

"Don't stand directly on the inner ring," he said. "Stand there and the work starts sliding out from under you."

"That sounds very much like danger in a cardigan."

"I know what I mean."

"I know you do."

This time the flatness in her voice had edges.

He unwrapped the Leaves and laid the page across the anchor stone. The effect was immediate. Ink rose black and sure through the vellum, not seeping now but taking its proper lines as if it had been waiting all evening to stop pretending. Prue let out a quiet breath and dropped to her knees opposite him, too interested to bother staying offended for one second longer than interest required.

They worked.

The room narrowed to nib-scratch, lamp hiss, and the steady ache building through Owen's palm. He held the page and translated what Meridian notation he could. Prue tested each relation against her grids, her world tracing, and the old astronomy tables she had copied so many times at school that she now trusted the shapes more than she trusted most people. When one approach failed, she pushed her spectacles up with the back of her wrist, crossed out the line, and tried the next premise. Owen kept narrowing his claims in the notebook, then crossing out the narrowing because the stone made it obvious when he was being cowardly with a conclusion.

The four anchor points came first as pattern, then as place, but not cleanly.

Prue laid the world sheet over the rings and caught the first knot under the wrong sea.

"Hang on," she said at once. "The chart came later than the prime line."

She slid the tracing west by less than the width of her nail and held one smoked sheet above it. The knot dropped under Britain. At the same moment a cramped marginal tag Owen had been treating as a unit mark opened into category instead: school holdfast, northwestern division. The burn in his right palm kicked so hard he nearly lost the page.

"That's us," Prue said. For once she had forgotten to keep her voice level. She looked up at the ceiling as if all that stone might turn transparent out of courtesy. "Not as a symbol. Literally us."

Owen had known the anchor under Hogwarts was real. Seeing it sit in relation to the rest of the world was different. It took the school out of its own story and nailed it into something older and much less interested in children.

The second knot refused longer. He called it ocean because the tracing put it in open blue. Prue said, "No, look at the bands," and swapped in the stripped chart, the one with only the meanest coastlines left on it. She rotated the glass a hair. The bands that had looked decorative deepened into heat marks around a vast inland red.

Prue sat back so abruptly her heel squealed on the stone. "That's half the planet away."

"More," Owen said, because the outer ring had finally stopped being coy about scale.

She laughed once under her breath, without any humor in it, and leaned in again. "Fine. Again."

The western point came from a misread. Owen took the note for tide bite and started drawing coast. Prue caught his wrist with two ink-cold fingers.

"Air," she said. "It says air bit water into stone. Not tide."

She dragged the lamp closer, bent until her curls brushed the vellum, and turned the projection on its side. The supposed coast reared up into a long mountain spine far to the west, high enough that even the manuscript bothered to mention water freezing where it fell.

After that the fourth anchor gave faster. By then they knew what sort of evasions the table liked. A salt plateau in the great interior of Asia, all dryness and glare even in the reduced language of the map.

Prue traced each point onto her world sheet with ink made thin by cold. Her hand steadied only once the last dot was down. "Four," she said, mostly to herself. "Not a web. More like a tent with the whole planet hanging in it."

"A quadrant hold," Owen said. "Or that's the nearest English to it."

"Nearest isn't the same as right."

"No."

"Still. Tent will do for tonight."

He liked that she could be exact without pretending exactness was free.

When they had the points, the stress marks began to make sense. The outer rings logged load: recurrence intervals, surge tolerances, places where the field had brightened and then failed to settle. Prue found the rhythm first because she had spent half her life making dots and arcs mean time.

"Look," she said, tapping a cluster of fine slashes around the Hogwarts point. "This isn't random deterioration. It jumps. Holds. Jumps again. Closer together later."

Owen bent over the page until his nose almost touched it. The chamber smelled of old mineral water and hot oil from the lamp. He could hear the castle above only as occasional thick thuds through many yards of stone, like furniture being shoved in another house.

"Those are local events," he said. "High-force loads."

"Meaning what, exactly?"

"Curses. Punishment magic. Anything heavy enough to strike the old structure rather than just the rooms built on top of it. Repeatedly."

Prue's finger moved to a darkened notch deeper than the rest. "And this."

He knew that one. It marked late November, the punishment scene when Amycus threw force across a row of students while Owen, desperate and half-ill, changed contact just enough to make curses skid. He had felt the castle answer then like a bell hit in the middle.

"That was me as well," he said.

Her head came up. "As well."

"Not the punishment. The amendment. It leaves a scar. Every successful one does."

"You have been cutting the same place you're trying to mend."

"Shallowly."

"That's not very comforting, Owen."

"I didn't say it to be comforting."

She looked at him for a long second, then went back to the page because the page was, for the moment, the only thing either of them could make move. They marked the other nodes. The western spine showed long old damage and recent brightening. The red inland anchor pulsed weakly but steady, a bad sign because weak and steady suggested attrition rather than a single wound. The salt plateau had a fracture pattern like dried glaze.

Hogwarts was worse than any of them.

The point burned hottest and shivered under the vellum. Each fresh load sent hairlines spidering outward from it, then drew back. It was a site being prodded awake by brutality, a school turned signal flare by a regime too stupid to know what its own violence was doing to the stone under its boots.

Prue sat back on her heels and scrubbed her hands over her face. There was ink on the side of her wrist now and a black half-moon near her jaw where she had touched herself without noticing.

"They're doing this," she said quietly.

"Yes."

"All year."

"Yes."

Another thud came through the chamber, heavier than the others. A shout followed, too muffled to make words of, only human shape and distress. On the page the Hogwarts point darkened and threw out one more fine line.

Prue stared at it. "That just answered something upstairs. Somebody got flung into a wall or a desk hard enough for this thing to feel it."

"Probably."

"Probably."

Owen looked at the mark. "Yes."

She laughed once, without humor. "You only go vague when it's awful."

He almost corrected the statement because it was not always true. Then he let it pass because it was true often enough to count.

They went on. He needed the local geometry now, not merely the fact of four anchors. The table around the Hogwarts point carried a secondary run of notation that did not recur at the other anchors. Prue saw it at the same moment and shifted closer to catch the lamplight better. Their knees knocked. Neither moved away. Her left shoulder settled against his for balance while she copied the signs into the margin of his notebook, quick and narrow and exact. The contact was practical and not practical. It warmed a strip of him he had not known was cold.

"This one again," she murmured. "Every time the load jumps. Only here."

Owen traced the phrase with his thumbnail. "Institutional distinction. No, that's too tidy. Directed force inside bound school."

"School, specifically?"

"Yes. The Hogwarts node keeps a local set the others don't."

"Meaning what, in practical terms?"

He should have said he needed another night with it. Instead the half-visible answer, the sleeplessness, and the relief of finally not working alone made him answer too soon.

"Meaning there may be ways to work on this place as a place," he said. "Not just the whole field. I can't tell yet if it's access or warning or some sort of local correction."

She stopped writing.

"Local enough to help people here."

"Maybe. Later. If I can read it properly."

"And if later turns up while Carrow's still upstairs making an example of somebody?"

He disliked how quickly the line had found its real shape. "That depends what it costs."

Her shoulder came off his. The warmth went with it.

"Right. So that's it, then."

"If it draws from the same purchase as the repair—"

"No. Don't start there. I asked whether you'd use it."

"I know, but it's the condition."

"It's always the condition with you. If this, if that."

He put his left hand flat on the stone. The burn across his right palm kicked once, hot enough to make the skin tighten. "There are four anchors left doing the work. Hogwarts is the one getting hit over and over. If I spend leverage here because it's nearer, and that opens the wider failure, then I've done something immediate and still made it worse."

"Nearer," she said. "You say that and skip the rest."

"You know what I mean."

"I know. I know, and I'm still arguing with you."

The chamber gave another blunt thud from somewhere above, thicker than sound. The Hogwarts line on the page darkened under Prue's hand.

She looked at it, then at him. "That moved because somebody upstairs got hit again. Hard."

He hated that she was right in the form she meant and wrong in the larger one, and that both truths sat in him at once. "If using a half-read local condition breaks the field, then no. I can't promise I'd use it."

Prue took off her spectacles and rubbed the mark they had left on the bridge of her nose. Without them her face looked younger and much more tired. "You hear yourself, don't you? No, that's wrong. You hear the version with the person rubbed out. Somebody upstairs just hit a wall hard enough for the stone to jump, and you go straight back to conditions."

"That's not fair. I'm in that room as well."

"That isn't the point."

"I'm telling you the scale of it," he said.

She let out a flat little laugh. "Stop saying scale. I know it's bigger than one room. I know. But that noise upstairs lands in somebody's ribs or their teeth."

He wanted to tell her she was flattening the logic into something sentimental and unusable. He wanted to tell her that if the school failed, the room upstairs did not get safer, only smaller. What came out instead was, "What do you want me to say?"

Prue set her spectacles back on. Her fingers shook once, from cold or anger or both. "Start there, for once. With whoever just hit that wall. Then tell me the price if you have to."

He had nothing he could say that did not either lie or make the same argument again.

After a moment Prue pulled the notebook back between them. "Move your hand a bit left. The mark's going."

He obeyed.

What followed never quite settled into routine. The castle kept interrupting. Three times something heavy hit or struck overhead hard enough to travel through the stone, and each time the Hogwarts line on the tracing thickened before settling back. Prue marked every change without comment. She had stopped giving him the benefit of broad claims. If he translated a sign too widely she said, "No, smaller," and waited until he narrowed it.

By the time the lamp was burning low they had one usable sheet: the four anchors fixed in black, the Hogwarts point furred with fresh stress, and the school-specific run copied into Owen's notebook with more question marks than certainty. The page on the stone had already started giving itself back to secrecy. Dawn or exhaustion; he could not tell.

Owen flexed his right hand. The burn answered at once.

Prue stacked the transparencies one by one. Her movements were neat from habit, not calm. At the threshold she glanced back toward the route they had come by, fixing it in memory.

"I can get back to the kitchens."

"You haven't done it before."

"I've just done half of it," she said. "I'll manage."

He stood, slower than he meant to. "Prue."

She waited with the lamp in her hand. He had three possible sentences and none of them were any use: that the danger was real, that he was not being cruel for sport, that he knew exactly how he had sounded. By the time he found breath for any of them, the moment had gone.

Prue turned and went into the passage.

He stayed only long enough to wrap the Leaves, slide the tracing and the ash-linen notebook into his satchel, and make sure the copied line would still be there in daylight. Then he followed the last of the lamp-smell back toward the kitchens and the service stair, where the first grey of dawn had started finding the upper cracks, with her flat silence ahead of him all the way up.


# Chapter 11

By breakfast, when the actual parchment finally reached him, Owen had already been told twice that the Headmaster wanted him.

The first warning came from a narrow portrait on the second-floor landing, a witch in tarnished blue silk whose painted pearl earring had been scratched off years ago so that one bare ear looked oddly accusatory. She had been darning a stocking in her frame with short offended jabs. As Owen came up from the cellar after dawn, satchel dragging at his shoulder, lamp-smell still in his clothes and the whole night with Prue still caked under his eyes, she looked up and said, "Mr Phipps. Do stop behaving as if staircases can protect you. The Headmaster has sent for you."

He had kept walking because portraits repeated things for pleasure and because he still had the Grinlow Leaves in his satchel, wrapped under his spare shirt beside the ash-linen notebook. He had not trusted the manuscript to the dormitory after the night's work, not with the map of four failing anchors still half alive in his head and Prue's last, flat silence following him down the corridor more cleanly than anything she might have said.

The second warning came outside the Great Hall. Edith Wren, a Hufflepuff sixth-year with a voice made papery by a winter cough, caught his sleeve as he went in. Her badge had been sewn back on with blue thread because she lost pins constantly and refused to admit it.

"Filch was asking after you before breakfast," she said. "And one of the Slytherin prefects. Proper asking, not corridor gossip."

"Right," Owen said.

Edith let go of his sleeve and studied his face for a second too long. "You look dreadful."

"Didn't sleep."

"That'll help."

She moved on, coughing into her fist. Owen stood one beat longer in the doorway while the Hall noise struck him full on: plates, chairs, the soft public thunder of a few hundred people pretending school still had a shape. Then he went to the Hufflepuff table because not going would only have made him more visible.

The parchment waited under his spoon.

It was folded once, no seal, only his name written in a severe hand that managed to look disappointed by the existence of ink. His bowl of porridge steamed around it. Somebody had placed it there after he sat down or before he arrived; either possibility was bad in a different direction.

Across from him, Martha looked from his face to the note without pausing in the work of buttering bread. Two of her knuckles were split and yellowed with ointment. She had started favoring her left leg after the corridor fiasco. Politeness let people ignore it; anyone who knew her old stride saw it at once.

"Open it," she said.

Owen unfolded the parchment.

`Mr Phipps will attend the Headmaster immediately.`

Nothing else. No threat, no explanation, only the command and the signature below it: `S. Snape`.

Martha tore her bread in half with more force than necessary. "Well."

"Yes," Owen said.

"If you're going to say you don't know what it's about, don't. Nobody's that unlucky by accident."

"I don't know which thing it's about," he said before he could stop himself.

That brought her head up. Disgust showed fast; he had managed to confirm there were multiple candidates. Three places down the bench a fourth-year stopped talking to his friend and glanced over. At the Ravenclaw table Prue was bent over her tea with the concentration she usually reserved for classification. One lens of her spectacles was clouded where she had wiped it badly on her cuff. She did not look up.

Owen folded the note smaller and slid it into his pocket. His satchel strap had wound itself round his ankle under the bench. He thought, briefly and very stupidly, of leaving it there. But the table was full, people moving in and out, Amycus somewhere in the middle aisle with his mug and his appetite for interruption. Every route back to the cellar crossed other eyes first. The manuscript was safer on him than off him, merely the less stupid option. Safety had dropped out of the calculation entirely.

Martha had already started on her porridge. "Are you going to eat?"

"Not now."

"Fine."

She did not tell him good luck. He did not wait for her to.

The walk to the office should have been long enough to think. It was not. Everything in the castle seemed arranged to remove thought and leave only awareness.

Two suits of armour stood outside the Hall doors. Usually they watched the room with the bored vacancy of school fixtures. This morning one of them had its visor angled fractionally down, enough that Owen felt inspected while he passed. On the third-floor landing a pair of Slytherin girls broke off talking when they saw him and stepped aside too quickly, leaving a clear strip of corridor between themselves and him. A staircase that normally preferred dithering students over punctuality settled under his feet at once and carried him straight upward without the usual malicious pause. He was so tired that each small convenience felt worse than obstruction would have done.

His right palm had been hot since dawn. Newer cross-lines had risen over the clean old coffer burn after the corridor work and the undercroft strain. He kept the hand in his pocket and the pocket clenched.

At the headmaster's staircase the stone guardian admitted him before he had fully stopped. The concealed door folded back on a draft of colder air, and the spiral stair began to turn with the faint grinding sound of old machinery deciding it had waited long enough.

Owen put one hand on the rail and rode upward with the satchel pulling his shoulder crooked. He had imagined, in the worst moments since breakfast, that he might be sick on the stair. Instead he was too frightened for that. Fear had gone dry on him somewhere between the portrait and the Hall. It sat now as a series of practical recognitions. The manuscript was in the bag. His wand was unreliable. If Snape searched him, the rest became fast. If Snape did not search him, that might be worse.

At the top, the office door stood open.

He had never been inside under Snape. The room still had the circular authority of the place under Dumbledore, but whatever softness had once kept it from feeling administrative had been pared away. The windows let in a washed grey morning. Most of the silver instruments on the side tables had gone still, their chatter tamped down for the morning. Several portraits of dead headmasters appeared to be sleeping with theatrical innocence. Two others were plainly listening. The air smelled of beeswax, cold ash, and old cloth that had been shut away from daylight too often.

Snape stood beside the desk, one hand on a stack of parchments he was not reading. He looked more tired than Owen had ever seen him at a distance, which was to say he looked exactly as severe as usual with a little more of the skull showing through. His black hair hung straight and damp at the temples, giving him the look of a man who had come in from weather only he could detect.

"Close the door, Mr Phipps."

Owen did.

"Put your satchel on the chair."

The command came flat and final. Owen obeyed; hesitation would only have worsened it. He set the bag on the hard wooden chair opposite the desk and sat nowhere near it.

Snape watched the movement. "You may stop looking at it. If confiscation had been my immediate object, you would not still be carrying it."

The line went through Owen like a nail drawn down glass. He kept his face as blank as he could manage. Tiredness kept putting cracks in it.

Snape went on with the dry patience he used for correcting posture. "Within the last several months this school has acquired a greenhouse with impaired ideas about closure, a punishment platform that briefly mistook contact for permission, and fresh stress marks in wards old enough to predate standardized wand instruction. Your own spellwork has degraded in parallel. Shall we continue pretending these are separate accidents."

The words came as summary, not inquiry. Owen answered anyway.

"Things have gone wrong," he said.

"Yes." Snape's mouth moved by less than a smile. "That is what I have just summarized."

Owen looked at the desk because looking at Snape made him too aware of the satchel at his side. The desk surface was almost bare. A school ledger, a quill, two sealed memoranda, and a fine grey film of dust in the carved groove at the back where even house-elves could not be bothered with ceremonial furniture this year.

"I haven't done anything to the office," he said, and heard at once how childish it sounded.

Snape's eyes narrowed in concentration, the look of a man finding a smaller and more exact disappointment than the one he had expected. "No. Fortunately for both of us, you have so far confined yourself to less politically complicated sections of the building."

He came round the side of the desk, not quickly. Owen was suddenly aware of how little the room offered in the way of useful movement. The portraits, the door, the stair below it, the still instruments, all of it had the same quality as the summons had carried up the corridor: surveillance that no longer needed to hide it was surveillance.

"Your right hand," Snape said.

Owen did not move.

"Mr Phipps."

He took the hand out of his pocket and laid it palm up on his knee. The old coffer burn crossed the center in its dark glossy ribbon. Over it and off it ran the newer red-brown branches, finer and less regular, as if some second hand had tried to write through the first in a language skin was unsuited to.

Snape did not touch. He bent slightly, studying the palm with the same expression he might have given a contaminated potion sample.

"A primary brand," he said after a moment, mostly to himself. "Then load response. Crude, but not random." He straightened. "What you have been using is older than wand discipline and less forgiving. Older scholarship usually calls it amendment."

The word hit harder than any shouted accusation could have done. Owen had not realized, not really, how much of his courage depended on the possibility that no one else in Hogwarts possessed even the right nouns.

"I don't know what you mean," he said.

Snape looked at him with frank contempt. "Then your standards for lying have worsened along with your wandwork."

Owen shut his hand again.

Snape returned to the desk but did not sit. "Let me save us both time. I do not know which manuscript, table, ruin, or founder-era leftover you have put your hands on. If I did, this conversation would be shorter and your morning markedly worse. I do know the aftermath. A greenhouse latch that spent three days disagreeing with the word shut. A punishment platform that read contact as leave. A corridor threshold that kept returning children to the same patch of flooded floor after your diversion ought to have finished. Those are not separate accidents. They carry the same handprint."

Owen's mouth had gone dry enough that swallowing felt public. "You've seen this before."

"I have read around it," Snape said. "Enough to know what happens when some fool leaves old work in the walls and assumes it will stay obedient. The descriptions tend to end with missing pieces. Which should not reassure you."

The room seemed to contract by a measurable amount. One of the supposedly sleeping portraits shifted under its painted blankets and then went still again.

Snape's voice lost a degree of its classroom edge and became colder for that loss. "You keep treating this as a matter of wrong wand movements. Whatever you touched does not stay where you meant it to."

Owen heard the greenhouse latch, the punishment dais, the stupid comic burst of doors and toilets and armor in the corridor sabotage, all of them suddenly classified inside a vocabulary that was not his alone.

"I wasn't trying to do any of that," he said.

"No," Snape said. "You were trying to make one narrow exception. Old work is not interested in the size of your intention."

Owen looked up despite himself. "If you know this much, why haven't you taken it."

Snape's face altered by almost nothing. It was enough.

"An admirably direct question," he said. "For you." He turned one parchment over, though there was nothing on the back worth seeing. "Because the Carrows notice broken children, missing food, and overt insubordination. They do not notice stress in old ward seams unless somebody educates them. I prefer to preserve that ignorance."

The answer showed strategy and hid the rest. Owen tried another angle.

"So you want me to stop."

"I want you," Snape said, "not to make the school structurally more ridiculous than it already is."

For a second his breath caught on the edge of a laugh and died there. Snape had shoved the whole mess back down to corridors, doors, and children tripping over consequences. Owen hated how nearly that helped.

"I can't promise that," Owen said before good sense caught up.

Snape's head tilted. "No. I had not expected promise from you. Only information, which I am also apparently not to have."

That was a mistake, Owen knew it while making it, but fear had gone on hardening and had taken some of the ordinary manners with it.

"If you already know the traces," he said, "then you know enough without the source."

One of the portraits gave a tiny sound that could have been a smothered cough or a laugh. Snape did not take his eyes off Owen.

"Do not mistake my current ignorance for your safety," he said.

"I'm not."

"Good."

He moved to the window and looked out over the grounds, though the tone of the room did not suggest scenery had much to do with it. From where Owen sat he could see only a slice of pale lawn and the black lines of wet branches.

"There is another matter," Snape said.

Owen waited.

"Hogwarts is not inert stone. The founders wrote conditions into it and the conditions persist. The staircase brought you straight here. The guardian admitted you before you had properly stopped. The armour outside the Hall has been turning its visor at children it means to watch. Little corrections, so far." He turned back. "If you keep worrying the same old seams, the castle will stop waiting for instructions. It will begin answering on its own terms."

The old burn drew tight across Owen's palm. Under his shoes he could almost feel that remembered metal tapping from below, the building all at once less like masonry than machinery.

"What's that mean."

"It means you stop being the only fool in the exchange," Snape said. "A door may refuse you. A room may decide it understood more than you said. That is enough meaning. Do not ask a building full of founder logic to help you improvise."

The warning went through Owen more coldly than the threat had. For the first time since he entered the office he forgot the satchel for a full second.

"You know about the undercroft," he said.

Snape's expression shut at once. "I know enough to recognize when the school has acquired a new source of strain below the public level. Do not flatter yourself by assuming omniscience where attention suffices."

There it was again, the gap. He knew traces, not site. He knew tradition, not scale. He had vocabulary, not the whole map. Owen did not have the luxury of relief; even partial knowledge in Snape's hands felt like standing near a blade while someone else tested its weight.

"Who else have you told," Snape said.

Owen thought of Prue's face over the table, ink on her cuffs, the hurt in her silence when he would not promise human use for human danger. He thought of Martha. Neville. The Room in September. He answered the most literal version available.

"No one who can do anything with it without me."

Snape's gaze sharpened. "That does nothing to reassure me either."

"It wasn't meant to be."

For the first time something almost like genuine irritation crossed Snape's face. "No. I see you have reached the age at which boys confuse exclusivity with competence."

He came back to the desk and finally sat, which somehow increased rather than reduced the pressure in the room. Seated, he looked less like an administrator and more like a knife somebody had remembered to fold.

"Listen carefully," he said. "If Amycus or his sister develop curiosity about your hand, your wand, or the bits of corridor damage that coincide suspiciously with your movements, your life will become shorter and louder. If you are foolish enough to continue this work, keep it off public routes. Keep it off moving architecture. Keep it away from children who have already got sufficient cause to scream. And if the building starts answering before you have finished asking, you will stop. Immediately. I will know the difference."

Owen's throat felt lined with grit. "How."

Snape's look suggested the question had failed before it was fully set down. "Because I am Headmaster," he said. "Because every damaged ward in this place eventually reports as inconvenience to someone. Because there are very few categories of disaster left to surprise me."

The last sentence sat there as fact, flatter than the rest and wholly self-contained, a hard little object nobody touched.

Owen heard himself ask, "Are you going to tell anyone."

Snape's answer came without delay. "No useful person."

Again Snape gave him one of those slant answers built to survive the whole year. Owen had no way to test it and no power to require a straighter version.

Snape picked up one of the sealed memoranda and set it down again. It was dismissal, almost.

"You may go, Mr Phipps."

Owen stood.

"And Mr Phipps."

He stopped with the satchel strap in his hand.

"Whatever you are carrying," Snape said, "the fact that it has chosen a fifteen-year-old boy as its current custodian should not be taken as endorsement by history, providence, or this office."

Any reply would have sounded stupid or adolescent in a way Owen could not afford just then. He lifted the satchel, felt its familiar bad weight drag at his shoulder, and left.

The spiral stair carried him down too smoothly. By the time he reached the corridor his knees had started shaking with a delay that felt insulting.

He had walked into the office with the manuscript, the notebook, and every obvious fear laid out in advance. He had walked out with the manuscript and the notebook still in his possession. The fear had altered shape instead of lessening. It had picked up detail.

* * *

By supper the meeting had gone common.

Nobody came up and said so. That would have required a level of leisure the year did not provide. But the knowledge moved through the school in the way useful dirt always did: under doors, through lines, across tables, inside jobs people were already doing with their hands.

A second-year Ravenclaw who normally borrowed ink from anyone looked at Owen's bottle in Charms and asked the person two seats over instead. In the library Madam Pince took back a stack of indexes from him without comment and then, after a beat, locked the damaged north bay with a key rather than the usual charm. That one hurt for reasons too particular to explain.

At the Hufflepuff table a hand-drawn map of corridor turns lay open between the salt cellar and a third-year boy's elbow, grease-soft at the folds from too many hands. The boy had a patched sleeve and a habit of chewing the inside of his cheek. A first-year girl arrived at the bench with two folded slips pinched in one fist.

"Martha said one goes to the kitchens and one goes to the west stair," she said, already angling them toward Owen out of old habit.

"Not him," the third-year said.

He did not say it cruelly. He said it the way a timetable was corrected.

The girl's face went bright red. Martha sat farther down with two younger girls and a loaf she was slicing too thin because that was the only way there would be enough. She did not look up. "Elsie can take the kitchens one," she said. "Ben gets the west stair after pudding."

The first-year nodded, pulled the slips back to her chest, and moved away. The third-year folded the map once and kept his palm over it until Owen had sat down.

Prue was absent entirely. Owen found himself checking the Ravenclaw table three times anyway.

Halfway through the meal a first-year Gryffindor stopped beside him with a folded note. She had sewn stars in white thread onto the frayed edge of her cuff, uneven little five-pointed things that made the school robe look briefly like something she had chosen.

"Neville says after," she muttered.

"Where."

"Barnabas tapestry."

She put the note down and went before he could ask anything else. The paper was blank inside; words would only have slowed it down.

He knew, walking there after supper, that accusation would be the easy part. He could answer accusation, even badly. What waited at the end of the corridor was arithmetic.

Neville had chosen a disused classroom off the seventh-floor turn, not the Room itself. That was sensible. The corridor smelled of damp stone and cabbage from somewhere below. When Owen pushed the door open, Neville was inside with a crate of pilfered supplies on the desk: bandages, soap, three apples, and a jar of peppermints that had probably been taken from some office meant to keep them for guests. Martha stood by the windowsill tearing an old sheet into strips with both hands and her teeth when the fabric refused the line. She kept working when he came in.

Neville shut the door behind him.

"How long were you with him," he said.

Neville skipped greeting and theatrics alike. Owen almost preferred that.

"I didn't time it."

"Long enough that people saw you go in and come out."

"I was summoned."

"Yes," Neville said. "I know how offices work."

Martha ripped another strip and wound it into a roll. "Shut up and answer him properly."

Owen looked from one to the other. Martha's plait had come half out and been redone in a hurry. There was dried soap at one wrist where she had clearly tried to wash something off and been called away before finishing. Neville wore the month on his face: sleep taken in ten-minute scraps, a bruise worked out along his jaw in yellow and green. He still stood with the steadiness of somebody forcing the room to borrow it from him.

"He'd noticed damage in the wards," Owen said. "And my hand."

Neville held out a hand. "Let's see."

Owen hesitated, then showed him. Neville did not touch either. He just looked, jaw tightening once.

"That from Snape."

"No."

"From your private project."

"Yes."

Martha snorted softly without amusement. "At least we're saying it plain now."

Neville's eyes stayed on the hand a second longer, then moved to Owen's face. "What did he want."

There it was, the question with no good answer.

"He knows some of it isn't ordinary magic," Owen said. "Old stuff. Older than wands, or older than how we use them. He knows enough vocabulary to recognize traces."

"What traces."

"Stress marks in the castle wards. Places things went wrong."

Martha dropped a bandage roll into the crate. "Places you made them go wrong."

"Not always on purpose."

"I know," she said. "That's one of the less comforting bits."

Neville took that in and let it sit. "Did you tell him anything he didn't know already."

Owen thought of the undercroft, the anchor stone, the four dim nodes on the table. Thought of Snape saying manuscript and not book, old work and not system, enough but not all. He answered carefully.

"Not much."

"Not much isn't a measure."

"No locations. No other names. Nothing that lets him use it."

Neville's face did not change. "Use what."

Owen knew at once he had stepped in it.

"The work," he said. "Whatever the old system is."

"Right." Neville rested both hands on the desk. "So here's the problem. You've got some hidden bit of magic you've kept from everyone. It marks you. It marks the building. Snape notices, calls you in private, and you come out with your bag still on your shoulder and your face still in one piece. Then you say he can't use it. So don't stand there and act baffled. You can hear how that sounds."

"I didn't say don't worry."

"You didn't need to."

There was a short silence. From somewhere down the corridor came running feet, then laughter cut off too quickly. Martha kept tearing sheet into lengths and winding them, tear, pull, fold, wind, as if the work licensed her not to look directly at him.

Owen tried again because the alternative was standing there and watching the arithmetic close.

"He warned me about the castle," he said. "Said if this old work gets pushed too hard the place starts answering back. Doors, rooms. Rules going wrong. He knows that part."

Neville's expression stayed flat, but something in it cooled another degree. "And you thought that helped."

"It means he's dangerous."

"He was dangerous before breakfast."

"Not like this."

"Fine." Neville shoved one of the apples aside so Martha's bandage rolls had room in the crate. His voice sharpened for the first time, not louder, just cleaner. "Then he's dangerous in one more direction. Does that change where I send people tonight. Does it change who carries messages. If not, what am I meant to do with it."

Owen opened his mouth and had nothing except the wrong scale again, the same failure as the Room months earlier, only now with worse evidence and less trust.

Martha supplied it before he could.

"It sounds like one more thing wrong," she said. She still was not looking at him. She yanked the sheet and the tear went crooked. "No. Worse than that. One more thing we're meant to hold in our heads while we're doing this. Does that help."

"That isn't fair."

She turned then. "No. It isn't, Owen. None of this is."

The plainness of it shut him up better than shouting would have done.

Neville straightened from the desk. "Listen. I don't know whether Snape's using you, watching you, or just keeping you on a shelf for later. Maybe you don't know either. But I can't have someone in planning who goes in and out of his office and then gives me not much when I ask what happened."

"It wasn't private business. I was hauled in."

"That doesn't make it safer."

"I didn't choose it."

"You chose everything before it."

Owen drew breath and got almost none of it. The room seemed to contract around the desk, the torn sheets, Martha's hands. Any attempt to argue percentages now would turn him into the exact boy Neville already thought he was.

He heard the thin scrape of his own breath and hated it. "So what. You're throwing me out."

"I'm keeping you off routes, meetings, and message work. For now." Neville nodded once toward the satchel at Owen's side. "Till I know what that is. Till I know what Snape knows. I've already got too many people moving blind."

"He doesn't."

"You don't get to certify that."

Martha dropped the last roll of bandage into the crate and wiped her hands on her skirt. "You really don't."

Owen looked at her. "You think I told him."

"I think every time this thing kicks, somebody else gets left to clean it up," she said. Weariness had flattened her voice. That hurt more than anger. "Me limping round the stairs. Him sorting routes. First-years running notes because you're off with that bloody bag. Then you come out of Snape's office carrying the same bag you went in with. From where anybody else is standing, that looks filthy."

He wanted to say he had been protecting them by not naming Prue, by not naming the undercroft, by not dragging anyone else into Snape's vocabulary. He wanted to say the work under the school had become large enough to deform every ordinary decision around it. He wanted, stupidly, to say that Snape had not taken the manuscript, to present that as innocence instead of one more problem.

None of those sentences belonged in Neville's room, with the apples on the desk and the torn sheets and the smell of soap. They were all the wrong scale again.

So he said, "Right."

Neville rubbed a hand over his face. "If you need somewhere to sleep, you still know where the room is. If there's immediate danger to students and you know it, you tell me. Quick. Plain. Beyond that, stay out of the work."

"Your work," Owen said, and heard the pettiness too late.

Neville dropped his hand. "Yes. Mine for now. I've got to know who's on which stair and who hasn't come back by curfew. I'm not adding maybe-you to that list."

No one spoke for a moment after that.

Then Martha picked up the crate. "I've got to get these downstairs."

Neville moved to open the door for her. She went out without brushing past Owen, though the room was small enough that avoiding him took a visible angle.

At the threshold Neville paused and looked back once.

"This isn't a punishment," he said. "I just can't send people out while I'm still guessing what Snape got from you."

Then he left too.

Owen stood alone in the disused classroom with the torn-thread dust from the sheets still drifting in the late light. On the desk one peppermint had rolled out of the jar and come to rest against a crack in the wood. He stared at it for a second, stuck on the stupid sense that he could be left in charge of peppermints and still make a mess of it.

When he finally went downstairs to the Hufflepuff common room, the round room was full in the ordinary way: homework, wet shoes by the fire, somebody arguing over a Transfiguration essay, bread smell coming faintly through the stones from the kitchens. The scene should have been a relief. Instead it felt arranged.

At the long central table two third-years were bent over a hand-drawn map of corridor turns. One of them, the boy with the patched sleeve and the chewed cheek, saw Owen come in and put his hand over the paper at once. He did it efficiently, not guiltily.

The other boy folded the map and slid it under his Potions text.

Neither of them said anything.

Owen crossed the room to his usual chair and set the satchel down between his shoes. The manuscript was still there. The notebook was still there. So was everything Snape had not taken, everything Neville would not now let near his own work, everything large and hidden and structurally wrong.

Around him the common room kept being warm, round, and Hufflepuff in the old stubborn way. Somebody laughed by the hearth. A first-year came to the table, took a folded slip from under the third-year's wrist, and carried it toward the fire without looking at him.

By curfew Martha was nowhere in the common room. The map had come back out twice and gone to other hands both times without once touching his side of the table. When the room thinned enough that nobody would bother tracking him for one more absence, he took the ash-linen notebook out of the satchel and went up toward the wrecked corridor.


# Chapter 12

In the wrecked corridor, Martha hit the wall again. Wedged in the black slit of a service recess, Owen kept his notebook open on his thigh and listened.

The wrecked corridor still smelled faintly of burst cisterns and wet plaster from the sabotage, though the house-elves had got the worst of the flood out days ago. Damp had dried in dirty crescents on the skirting. A cracked suit of armor lay in sections along the flagstones where it had been pulled apart and never properly put back: gauntlets by the first window, breastplate by the third door, one leg propped against a stack of boxed school records that had gone soft at the corners. The far stair should have led down toward the moving portraits and then the turn for the Trophy Room. Instead the damaged thresholds folded the passage back into itself. Anyone who took the last door arrived again by the broken armor and the white scuff of Amycus's boot.

"Up," Amycus said. His voice carried lazily in the empty hour after curfew. "Don't lie there and make me fetch you. If I have to fetch you, I'll do it badly."

Metal grated on stone. Martha got both hands under the breastplate and dragged it in against her knees. Even from the recess Owen could hear her breath catching. She had lost her plait halfway through the detention. Dark hair stuck to her face and neck with sweat. One sleeve was wet through at the cuff where she had gone down in the old toilet water on her second pass. There was blood on one shin now, not much in amount but bright enough to look separate from everything else in the corridor.

"It weighs the same every time," Amycus said. "Thought Hufflepuffs were sturdy."

"Thought teachers could count," Martha said, and her voice came out rougher than usual but still hers, practical and annoyed before it was frightened. "You've had me carry it six times."

Amycus laughed through his nose. He had a habit of working at the side of one back tooth with his thumbnail when he was enjoying himself. Owen could see the glint of the nail as he leaned in the middle of the corridor with his wand tucked under one arm like a cane. He smelled of sweat, old wine, and whatever over-spiced meat the staff had eaten for supper. "Then you ought to be getting better at it."

He flicked his wand. The breastplate jumped in Martha's hands and slammed hard into her thigh. She swore, bent double, nearly lost hold of it, then straightened because he was waiting for her not to.

Owen looked down at the page under his left thumb.

He had spent three nights preparing it in smaller and smaller claims until the line would hold. It was not an escape charm or a rescue, nothing as clean as that. The First Art would not answer to a wish dressed up as certainty. This was a local amendment written over a copied threshold table from the lower castle, with soot and chalk and a sting of his own blood worked into the linen surface until the notation darkened and sat still. If he pressed the page to the third lintel, where the corridor's broken return had the oldest stone under it, and let his palm burn open against the lower hinge, the passage should answer to its shortest true course for perhaps forty seconds. It might hold a minute if the castle was merciful. That would be long enough for a body already pointed toward the stair to go down it instead of being spat back into Amycus's hands.

That was all.

That was not all.

The same page, spent on the lower maintenance door off the kitchen runs, might finally persuade the sealed turn beneath the East Undercroft to answer as a door again instead of a wall. He had the route, the copied fractions, the mortar seam, the half-legible margin from the Leaves that only made sense when laid against founder stone. He had the corridor above empty because Amycus had cleared everyone off for this little show. He had, for once, enough quiet to try the deeper work without tripping over prefect patrols or other students wanting warmth or bread or honesty he had no intention of giving them.

If he fired the amendment here, the ward scar would ring through two floors of castle. Snape would feel it if he was awake. Owen's wand would go wrong for hours afterward. The lower door would keep being a wall. The route would wait, yes, but routes did not merely wait. They got noticed. They got bricked. They got cleaned, catalogued, assigned some tedious school use that made secret work impossible. Old opportunities died by inches.

Martha lurched past the second window with the breastplate clanging against her shins.

Amycus had turned the corridor into a lesson. That was the ugliest part of him. He never wanted pain by itself if he could help it. Pain was better when it taught the room what he wanted the room to learn. Two fourth-year Slytherins had been made to watch the first half hour from beyond the rope at the stair mouth. One of them, a sandy boy with a runny winter nose and a cardigan sleeve unraveling at the wrist, had tried not to look and been struck so hard round the ear he fell into the wall. Amycus had sent them off only after he got bored with their faces and decided Martha alone would do.

Now he was making her carry the armor from one end of the broken corridor to the other, through a door that kept returning her, and every time the space cheated he acted as if she had failed some simple instruction. When she slowed he hit her calves with short blunt jinxes that made the muscles jump under the skin. When she slipped he made her start again from the armor's scattered heap instead of where she had fallen. Once, twenty minutes before, he had kicked the helmet at her and split her lip when she did not catch it properly.

Owen knew all this because he had been there for twenty minutes and had not left.

He could keep the facts narrow if he kept the language narrow. The page would not save Martha for good. At best it would get her one clean run at the stair. Amycus might catch her on the landing. She might not understand quickly enough what had changed. Snape might come. The corridor might over-answer and unfasten the wrong turn. Any of those things could happen, and if one of them did the only result would be more pain, more scrutiny, less chance of reaching the anchor before the castle's damage worsened again.

All of that stood up well enough in the mind.

It did not change the smaller, meaner fact that the page under his thumb would have helped her tonight.

Martha got to the far door. She shifted the weight of the breastplate, boot sliding on grit, and tried to shoulder it through with the last of her balance. The door took her. For one second she was gone from the corridor. Then the broken threshold returned her neatly to the starting stretch by the first window.

The breastplate slipped from her hands and crushed down across her foot. She made a short animal sound and bit it off.

Amycus actually clapped once. "There she is. We were losing our rhythm."

She bent to lift the thing again. One of her hands failed. He had not seen that happen before. Her right hand opened and closed uselessly over the rim as if the fingers belonged to somebody standing next to her. She stared at them, annoyed, then tucked the bad hand against the metal and levered the weight up against her hip with her forearm instead.

He could end this pass. He could do it now, while Amycus was still talking.

His palm itched under the old burn. His teeth hurt with the nearness of worked stone. The page was ready. The copied line had settled. He even had the third lintel chosen already, had measured the cracks around it with his eye when Amycus first hauled Martha into the corridor and slammed the warded door behind them.

Martha dragged the breastplate forward, limping properly now.

"Come on," Amycus said. "You've still got the stairs to miss."

She reached the place in the corridor where the service recess ran behind the wall in a slit too narrow for anyone but a child or a starved older student to use comfortably. Owen had his body pulled back from the gap, but not far enough. Her head turned on the sound of the armor scraping. Her eyes snagged on the black seam in the panelling and found him at once.

She did not stop. She did not call out. Her expression changed less than he would have thought possible. There was a flicker of recognition, then something flatter and much colder than surprise. She shifted the breastplate higher, took one more limping step, and looked away from him so she could see where she was putting her feet.

Amycus had not noticed. He was digging again at his molar with the nail of his thumb.

Owen could still have used the page.

He waited for the heat of shame or panic or decency to make the choice for him. Nothing obliged. There was only the corridor, the notation, the weight of the founder seam below the kitchens like a second pulse under the stone. He thought, with the ugly exactness that came to him when he wanted to feel less instead of more, that Martha was conscious, still upright, still capable of taking the stair if given it, and that a leg could be mended better than a lost route.

He folded the page once, very carefully, so the wet-dark line in the corner would not crack, and slid it back under the oilcloth.

Amycus said, "Again," and Martha, without looking toward the recess this time, bent for the helmet.

Owen backed out of the slit, turned into the service run, and went down.

The kitchen-side passages were warm only by comparison with the rest of the castle. Steam from the scullery pipes slicked the stone. Farther in, past the last place house-elves stacked fireblackened pans, the warmth died and old pressure took over. He moved with his lamp hooded and low, counting the turns he had already marked in pencil on the inside cover of his notebook. He took two lefts under the cask room, then one descent by the split step, and kept his hand off the west wall where the mortar sweated in cold weather and left salt in the skin. The work steadied him because it always did. The work asked for sequence and weight and what answered to what. No part of it required that he return to the corridor above and act like a person whose first duty was to the nearest body.

At the maintenance door below the East Undercroft he knelt again.

The door had no handle left, only the memory of one, a long vertical discoloration in the stone where metal had once fixed into a slot. He laid the prepared page against that mark and put his burned palm over the center. Pain arrived cleanly, as if the old coffer had woken under his skin. It was not heat this time but pressure, then a sharp inward pull that seemed to take the hinges through his wrist and up into the roots of his teeth. The copied line in the page went slick and alive beneath his hand.

He said the condition exactly as he had narrowed it, no bigger than necessary. The linen darkened. The stone gave a little under his palm.

Above him, somewhere very far away and still far too near, there came a muffled clang that might have been armor or a body or both.

He did not stop.

The seam of the door shivered. Dust dropped in a thread from the lintel. A strip of black opened, no wider than two fingers, and colder air licked across the back of his hand carrying lake damp and the old mineral smell from deeper under the castle.

For one second relief was stronger than anything else in him.

It was working. The page had held. The lower turn had been answering as wall for months and now, under the copied line and founder pressure, it was willing to remember otherwise. It was not open yet, not fully, but it was enough to prove the route real. It was enough to keep going.

He breathed once, twice, feeling the ache race up his arm. The castle rang around the amendment in a pitch only half sound. It was softer than the contact working in November had been, better controlled and lower down. He could tell himself that mattered.

By the time the linen page went slack and dead under his hand he had widened the gap to the width of his fist. Beyond it lay a descending dark and the cold wet smell of stone that had not seen ordinary school air in centuries.

He sat back on his heels with blood from the reopened burn creeping into his lifelines and thought, not for the first time and not for the last, that this was what justified everything.

When he climbed up an hour later, the corridor above was empty. Amycus had gone. The rope had been taken down. The armor lay in a different pattern now, as if the pieces had been kicked apart for sport. There was a smear of blood low on the wall by the third door where a hand had caught and slipped.

Owen did not touch it.

***

The kitchens had gone quiet by the time he found Martha.

Most nights there was always some clatter left in them: spoons in sinks, pans stacked to dry, the low collective muttering of house-elves who worked as if silence itself were a spill needing mopping. Tonight the big ovens only hissed. A skin had formed on the pan of onion broth nearest the banked fire. Tallow stood at the central table sorting bent forks into two neat piles, his singed left ear tied up with a strip of checked pudding cloth so it would not brush his shoulder. He looked up once when Owen came in and then looked down again at the cutlery as if deciding whether Owen belonged with the salvageable things or the scrap.

Martha sat on a flour bin by the wall with one leg stretched out on an overturned crate. Madam Pomfrey had done what she could and no more. The knee was bandaged thickly under a borrowed pair of cut-off pajama bottoms, but the joint beneath the wrapping was still grotesquely swollen. There were green-brown bruises already climbing her calf where Amycus's jinxes had landed. Somebody had cleaned the blood off her lip. Her hair had been gathered into the first failed stages of a plait and then abandoned, so half of it hung loose over one shoulder. She held a bowl in her left hand because the right was strapped from knuckle to wrist and did not want to close properly.

She was peering into the broth with faint irritation.

"It's got skin on it," she said to Tallow. "I know that's not your fault. I'm still saying it."

Tallow flicked one bent fork into the good pile. "Miss Sorrell is welcome to complain after she drinks it. Complaining before drinking it is vanity."

"It's not vanity if I'm correct."

"It often is."

She gave a short laugh that caught in the middle and turned into a wince. That hurt Owen more than the sight of the knee had done, not because the pain was worse but because the laugh was ordinary, and because she was still doing it.

Tallow took a second bowl from the hob with a folded cloth and set it on the far edge of the table without looking at Owen. "If Mr. Phipps has come to stand in a doorway till the draft does the rest, Tallow asks him to choose a hobby less damp."

"Thanks," Owen said.

Tallow made a small noise that might have meant nothing. He went on sorting forks.

Owen took the bowl and sat on the bench opposite Martha because there was nowhere else to sit that would not make a point of itself. The broth smelled of onion and pepper and a little too much bay. Heat rose into his face from it. He had not realized how cold the undercroft had left him until then.

Martha sipped carefully, swallowed, and only then looked at him.

"So," she said.

"So."

"How far did you get?"

He did not pretend not to understand her. "Past the maintenance door."

She nodded once, as if confirming a sum she had already done. "Right."

Tallow's hands did not stop moving over the bent cutlery, but the sound of metal on metal got very exact.

Owen put his bowl down. "Pomfrey said your knee should mend."

"That's nice of it."

"She said the hand's mostly swelling and strain."

"Amazing. Have you got any more medical miracles, or was that the lot?"

"Martha."

"No, go on. You seem to have come with notes."

He had, in a sense. He could hear himself choosing smaller claims before he made them. "I had a line ready."

"Yes," she said. "I saw."

That should not have surprised him, but it did. The cold in him moved lower.

Martha shifted the bowl from one palm to the other because the heat was getting into the bad hand through the wrapping. "I wasn't sure at first if it was you or just a shape in the wall. Then you moved your thumb. I know your stupid hands."

He looked at his fingers. Ink sat in the cuticles, dust ground into the creases, and a little dried blood marked the heel of the palm where the burn had split again.

"It wasn't certain," he said. "I could have straightened the corridor for a bit. Maybe. If the lintel answered properly. But if I'd done it there, with Amycus right on top of it, the scar would've rung half the floor. Snape's already watching. And the same line was the only stable version I had for the lower door. I needed-"

"I know what you needed."

"You don't know the whole-"

"I know the size of a corridor," she said, still very calm. "I know the size of my knee. I know you had something in your pocket that would've changed the room and you left it there because you wanted the other thing more. That's plenty, Owen."

He tried again because method was easier than silence. "If I'd spent it there, the route below would've stayed shut. I had the ratios from the table at last. I had the quiet for it. Amycus had cleared everyone off. I couldn't guarantee the corridor would've held long enough for you to get clean away, and if it went wrong you could've been hurt worse than-"

She laughed once, not because it was funny. "Worse than what, exactly?"

The question sat between them with the kitchen heat on it. He had already done the ugly arithmetic on the way down from the corridor: ward scar against lower door, forty seconds against a knee, one route against one body. It only passed for reason if he refused to picture the actual knee.

He looked at the swollen knee and failed to answer.

"No, really," she said. "I'm curious now. Worse than that? Worse than him dropping the breastplate on my foot because I couldn't feel my hand? Worse than being sent back through the same bloody door till I started knowing which patch of wall I was going to hit before I hit it? Give me the chart. I want to see where the red line is."

Tallow turned away to the oven, lifted the broth pan, and gave them privacy so pointedly that it was almost indecent. The kindness of it made Owen feel more looked at, not less.

Owen said, "I'm not saying it was fine."

"You are, a bit."

"I'm saying I couldn't do both."

"No." Martha put the bowl in her lap too fast, sucked air through her teeth when it jarred her knee, and held it there with both forearms because the bad hand would not trust itself. "Don't say both. It wasn't me on one side and the whole world on the other. It was me in that corridor with the breastplate grinding my knuckles and you thinking about the door under the kitchens."

He had meant it like that. Hearing it back in her voice, with the bowl in her lap and the knee thick under the bandage, made the trick in the wording look as cheap as it was.

"The work is bigger than-"

"Stop." The word came out flat; she shut her eyes for a second, then opened them again. "Don't make it sound clean. You don't get to keep saying bigger like it gets the blood off the wall. My knee's blown up, my hand's strapped, and you still sound like you're marking homework."

He stared at the steam coming off his untouched broth. The skin on it had started to wrinkle. Some mean, schoolboy bit of him still wanted a neat line where hurt became too much, so he could pretend he'd misjudged by inches instead of wanting the door more.

"I can hear it, all right," he said.

"I know how it is."

There was a long enough silence for the oven to give a settling tick and for a pan somewhere near the sinks to drip twice into another pan. Martha shifted her leg a fraction and shut her eyes against the pain before opening them again.

"If you'd frozen," she said, "that would've been one thing. No, not one thing. I'd still be furious. But I'd have known what I was looking at. If you'd legged it because you were scared, fine, I'd call you a coward and have done. If you hadn't been there at all, I could've told myself you never knew. But you were there. I could see your face in the crack by the panel, and you kept the page."

Frozen would've been kinder. Scared would've been kinder. Those versions left room for failure instead of choice, and he could feel himself reaching for them anyway.

"It wasn't later. It was immediately after."

"That's not helping."

He shut his mouth. That, at least, he could do without dressing it up as sacrifice.
