Martha drank more broth, because even now she was the sort of person who would finish hot food before it went to waste, and wiped her upper lip on the back of her wrist where the split had started bleeding again. "Do you know what I kept thinking in there?" she asked.

He shook his head. He knew before she said it that she'd been waiting for something smaller than rescue: one stupid technical interruption, one muttered sorry, any sign he still belonged to the room.

"I kept thinking you'd do something annoyingly technical and then apologize for interrupting me while I was being punished." Her mouth twitched once. "That sounds stupid when I say it out loud, but there it is."

"It's not stupid."

"No. It was stupid. That's on me." She adjusted the bowl again, thumb slipping on the hot rim. "I kept telling myself you were still just being difficult. No, not just. Hungry and weird and off with your notebook. That if I got you sat at our table and shoved enough food at you and made you do daft jobs with the rest of us, you'd go back to being annoying in the normal way. Stupid of me."

He felt the shape of the service recess again under his shoulders, the black slit in the wall, her eyes finding him and then going flat.

"Martha-"

"No, listen. Don't do that." She lifted the strapped hand an inch, winced, and let it drop back against the bowl. "Don't sort it into fear and timing and all your careful little bits. You saw this hand stop working. You saw me smash my knee up and keep hauling that breastplate anyway. You had the page and you kept it. That's it. That's tonight."

The words were plain. That made them worse. If she had shouted, if she had called him monstrous or useless or cruel, he could have argued with the shape of it in his own head. But this was smaller and more exact. This he could fit against what had happened and find no slack in.

"I was trying to get to something that might matter for more than one corridor," he said, hearing as he spoke how little the sentence had in it besides himself.

"And I was in one corridor." Martha leaned back against the wall, then hissed when the crate knocked her bad knee. "That one. Helmet by my foot. Your face in the wall. Now it's this table and this soup and my knee going thick under the bandage. That's the stuff you keep stepping past to get to the clever bit under the castle."

Tallow came back with a small plate of bread crusts rubbed with garlic and set it by Martha's good elbow. He did not set any by Owen. "Miss Sorrell needs salt," he said to the room at large. "Arguments make young creatures leak."

"Thanks," Martha muttered, and picked up a crust awkwardly between her left thumb and forefinger.

Owen almost said thank you too and stopped because the words would have sounded like theft.

Martha chewed, swallowed, and then said, more tiredly than before, "I can't keep doing this with you."

He knew what she meant and still asked, "Doing what?"

"Being the one who keeps making room for you. At our table, I mean." She looked at him straight on. There were shadows under her eyes that had not been there in September. "Shoving the potatoes over, telling people no, leave it, that's Owen's bowl, he'll get here. I'm not doing that anymore. I'm not your reminder to eat. I'm not your excuse for turning up in the kitchens when it suits you. I'm done hauling your sleeve while you stare through me at the bloody stone."

"You don't have to haul me anywhere."

"Good. Then we agree."

He felt the answer land before she spoke it.

"We're finished, then," she said. She was not dramatic or vicious about it. It sounded like the sort of sentence someone used when a chore had gone on too long and there was no point wasting more soap on it. "Properly. Next time you come down here, sit somewhere else. Not at my table. Work with whoever still wants to work with you. If Neville asks why I don't send for you anymore, I'll tell him myself."

He sat very still. The bench under him had a wobble in one leg. He had never noticed that before.

"I'm sorry," he said, and because even now part of him wanted precision, he added, "I know that isn't the useful bit."

Martha nodded. "No. It isn't."

She turned her attention back to the bread crust in her hand, then to the bowl, blowing the skin aside with one tired breath before taking another sip. She did not dismiss him. She did not need to. The whole kitchen had already adjusted around a fact that no longer required discussion. Tallow moved behind the table with his careful cutlery and did not once ask Owen if he wanted more broth. The ovens hissed. Somewhere in the far pantry a stack of plates settled against itself.

Owen looked at Martha's bowl, at the smear of butter on the crust she had not quite managed to spread evenly with one hand, and thought absurdly of all the small things she used to notice first: whether the porridge had caught at the bottom, whether somebody had left a scarf on a damp peg, whether he'd gone a whole day on tea and crusts and called it enough. She had always dragged the world back down to touch and food and bruises before it could float off into talk. He had liked that in her because it saved him trouble. He saw now what else it had been.

He stood.

Neither Martha nor Tallow looked up.

His own bowl sat full on the table, film forming over the broth. He left it there and went out through the scullery door toward the colder dark under the kitchens. Behind him, after the door had swung shut, the spoon in the untouched bowl knocked once against the crock and went still.


# Chapter 13

The damp got through his trousers before the kitchen warmth had fully left him. Kneeling by the plinth, Owen felt the old pressure build behind his molars until he either got the lines right or went back upstairs with a headache that made parchment shine. Above him the rest of Hogwarts still interrupted him like a school: bells, essay deadlines, Amycus roaring in a corridor, somebody at breakfast asking whether he meant to eat that bit of toast. By now the East Undercroft felt less like a discovery than a job he had not meant to take and could no longer leave half done. Martha had stopped speaking to him except when a doorway forced the issue. Prue still spoke, but not with any waste in it. Down here the stone did not ask what kind of person that made him. It only waited to see whether he had understood.

He had been wrong about the lower tables for nearly three weeks.

The mistake annoyed him in a practical way that was worse than embarrassment. He had copied the pages twice into the ash-linen notebook, once in his own cramped hand and once with the signs separated into columns the way Prue had suggested, and still he had kept treating the marked block beneath Hogwarts as if it were a repair rubric with half its instructions lost. The block was whole; repetition had hidden its purpose. The same cluster sat beneath all four anchors, shifted one place each time, so it governed relation rather than any local repair: held, thinned, under weight, in live water.

It described the condition under which one site answered to another. Spellwork sat further up the chain. This was site logic.

He put the notebook flat on the anchor stone and checked the copied marks again, rubbing the heel of his hand over the place where the page had softened from damp. The Hogwarts column did not end where he had thought it ended. It turned. The star-table cut around the plinth, which he had been using as a map, had a second use embedded inside the same geometry. The old builders had not bothered to make a separate door for departure any more than they would have made a separate alphabet. If you were permitted one, you were presumed able to read the other.

The thought made him sit back on his heels for a moment, cold seeping through the stone into his shins. He had spent hours tapping walls, measuring seams, tracing drafts like an idiot in a children's adventure. The chamber below arose from the undercroft itself. The undercroft was the anchor aspect of a structure whose other aspect lay farther down, toward the lake, and only existed as accessible space when the site was made to answer as transit instead of holding.

"Right," he said quietly, because hearing it aloud made it less likely to wriggle away again.

His own voice came back to him as a damp blur from the stone.

He unrolled the strip of thin paper he had been using as a protective overlay and laid it against the groove on the plinth's lakeward side. The groove looked decorative until you followed its returns with the burned centre of his right palm. Then the line answered with a faint heat unlike the cold around it, the way the manuscript sometimes answered by becoming marginally more itself under his hand. He took up the pencil and marked the three relation signs into the blank spaces he had left weeks ago, checking each one against the notebook, forcing himself not to hurry just because his pulse had finally started behaving as if he were right. The sequence stayed the same: held, thinned, under weight, in live water.

The last sign was the awkward one, all bent stems and a small cut across the curve. He had once taken it for damage from mineral drip. Prue had said damage did not usually repeat with such good manners. He wished, not for the first time, that he had let himself be grateful in a less useless way when she said things like that.

When the last mark sat properly in its groove, nothing happened for two whole breaths. Then the damp sheen on the wall opposite the plinth brightened as if a lamp had been turned behind the stone. Owen stood too quickly, banged his knee on the plinth edge, swore under his breath, and went still again.

The wall simply lost its certainty. The seam he had taken for a natural meeting of blocks thickened into a pale vertical band. Water threaded down it, silver and steady. The pressure in his ears rose, not sharply but insistently, like a hand cupping the back of his skull. A section of stone no wider than a wardrobe withdrew from the category of wall and stood there as depth.

Steps appeared inside it, worn into a narrow turning stair of old black stone. A bronze rail, green with age, ran down one side. Cold air came up from below with the mineral reek of flooded cellars and the stale-metal smell of something that had not moved in centuries until now.

He had wanted certainty about the outer wards. What he had now was a stair under the lake.

Owen picked up the notebook, tucked the paper overlay back into it, and put the pencil between his teeth so both hands were free. His wand sat heavy and temperamental in his sleeve. He did not trust it enough for anything beyond light, and even that might go strange if the site objected. After a moment's thought he took the candle stump from his pocket instead, struck it with a match cupped against the damp, and watched the flame crawl up crooked and yellow. The light made the new stair look narrower rather than safer.

He descended sideways.

Water had found the steps years ago and never stopped using them. It sheeted over the treads in a skin so thin it did not look dangerous until his sole shifted on the second turn and his shoulder hit the wall hard enough to send the candlelight jumping. The stone was colder than the winter flagstones in the Great Hall, colder than the metal taps in the Hufflepuff loos at dawn. It had the impersonal cold of depth, a temperature kept by weight rather than weather. By the fifth turn the cuffs of his trousers were soaked. By the eighth the skin along his burned palm had begun to sting as if the whole route knew exactly where he had been marked.

The sounds were wrong in a way the undercroft's sounds were already teaching him to expect. Above ground, a bang remained a bang and a shout remained a shout. Down here every noise arrived as pressure first. A distant shift in the lake came through the wall as a great padded thud. His own steps returned from below as if somebody larger and slower were climbing up to meet him. Twice he stopped dead because the hairs on his arms lifted under his sleeves and he was certain he had heard breathing, and twice it was only water taking a new line through stone.

The stair emptied into a passage just tall enough for him to stand straight if he kept his head clear of the dripping vault. The floor had once been level, perhaps polished even, but the lake had pushed through hairline cracks and laid a skin of pale grit over everything. His candle showed grooves worn in parallel along the middle of the floor. They were too even for erosion. Something wheeled had gone back and forth here many times, heavy enough to bite rock and regular enough to make the same journey matter.

That steadied him more than any prayer would have done. The grooves meant carts, weight, and repetition. Departure, then, had not been a legend whispered over firelight by people in robes wanting to sound older than they were. It had been traffic.

The passage bent, widened, and gave him the chamber.

For a moment he did not take in the lens at all. What he saw first were the benches.

They ran along the walls in two long ranks, their wooden seats long since rotted away so only the iron brackets remained, each pair bolted at the same careful height. Above them, shallow niches had been cut into the stone with little raised lips to keep objects from sliding. Most were empty. One still held a corroded buckle, green and blunt with age. Another contained what looked like the mineral ghost of some folded fabric that had turned, over a vast stretch of damp, into a crust the colour of old teeth. On the floor near the far wall lay the snapped frame of a trolley or barrow, not magical in any showy sense, just practical metal with its wheel gone. The chamber had the stripped, after-use look of a station closed in a hurry and never reopened, except that hurry had occurred at a civilizational scale.

He imagined, unwillingly and all at once, coats dark with lake weather, children made to wait quietly, somebody counting loads, somebody swearing because a fastening had jammed, and then he cut the picture off. The place did not need his invented ghosts to prove what it was.

The star-lens occupied the chamber's far end like an altar built by surveyors.

It stood in a cradle of bronze ribs as tall as the classroom windows on the third floor, tilted upward into the black curve of the vault. Its face was a dark mineral disc, not quite glass, with a white crack running from the lower rim toward the centre and stopping there in a crooked hook. Three outer rings surrounded it, each cut with notation so worn he could only read every fourth or fifth mark without touching. One ring sat misaligned on its bearing, shoved half an inch off true. Salt had bloomed along the base in thin furred ridges. The whole thing looked as if somebody had built a telescope with the assumptions of stonework instead of lenses: weight, angle, pressure, endurance.

Beside it, a waist-high desk of the same black stone carried more tables, these shallower and narrower than the anchor stone's, as if intended for monitoring rather than for real work. Owen set his candle in a dry pocket of rock and bent over them until the flame painted the grooves gold.

He knew enough now to stop hoping for easy prose. The notation was all function, no kindness. But the same joints kept returning from the plinth tables: site, load, visibility, threshold, answer. He opened the notebook to the four-anchor page and set it against the desk so he could check each guess before it hardened into fact.

His scalp prickled.

If he had the structure right, the lens did not show stars for their own sake. It read the ward skin from the inside and measured what answered beyond it when the skin brightened. The cracked disc made sense suddenly, and horribly. If this chamber had been part of the departure infrastructure, then the people who used it had wanted more than a route off Earth. They had wanted warning.

He set his notebook beside the desk and copied the nearest string into the margin with fingers that had begun, annoyingly, to shake. Fear was part of it, and so was the childish, nauseous excitement of finding that the impossible thing had a manual after all.

There was a receiving groove across the desk, empty except for grit. He cleaned it with the edge of his cuff, then laid his burned palm across it because nothing else in the room was likely to count as a valid key. The skin answered at once with the deep needle-hot ache he had learned meant yes. The skewed outer ring of the lens jerked. Metal grated against metal with a noise like a chair dragged slowly over stone. The disc darkened until it no longer reflected the candle.

He ought to have stepped back. Instead he leaned closer.

At first he thought the lens had failed. It showed only blackness, shallow and matte, like a slate rubbed with old grease. Then the black adjusted in layers. Faint points came through, hard as coordinates, each star stripped of twinkle and atmosphere. Curving across them, almost invisible until his eyes took the scale of it, ran a film of light so thin it might have been a flaw in the mineral itself.

The film enclosed.

Continents and oceans had no place in it. The lens showed relation. The film was the ward field, a skin of answer wrapped around Earth's magical signal, and at one point on that skin a line shone harder than the rest.

He checked the notebook, matched the segment's skew against the Hogwarts column, counted the offset twice because he did not trust the first count, and only then let himself name it. Hogwarts. The light there looked wrong: raw, rubbed thin, stripped of anything triumphant. The white hook of the crack in the lens aligned with it, and for a disgusting second he could not tell whether he was seeing damage in the instrument or damage in the thing it read.

He touched the first load-mark on the desk.

The bright line at Hogwarts skated half a degree across the stars and broke into four separate stress points, all of them white enough to turn his stomach. For one stupid second he thought the reader had just collapsed into nonsense: cracked lens, cracked world, no honest way to tell which damage belonged to which thing.

But the lower tables had warned him about compound reads. Site and load were sharing the same damaged bearing. He flattened the notebook with his wrist, followed the shifted anchor-order in his own cramped copy, and found the small correcting notch he had copied three times without ever understanding why it kept returning. When he pressed it with the heel of his hand, pain went bright through the old burn. The outer ring jerked back with a hard bronze scrape. The four points shifted. Three settled away into the distances of the field, stressed but low. The fourth returned to the same skew the Hogwarts anchor occupied on the plinth tables, raw and rubbed thin under the hooked crack.

He still did not trust it. Beside the correction sat a forked tick that paired elsewhere with sequence dividers. He tried that next, barely touching it. The white stress along the Hogwarts segment loosened into intervals: one broad old bruise, several smaller recurring thickenings, one recent ugly burr lying half under the main line as if it had been forced from inside.

Only then did he start making claims. The broadest pulse was winter-sized if the scale meant what he thought it meant; it almost had to be the corridor catastrophe. The recurring smaller strikes could be the Carrows' punishments, laid on month after month. The newest interference made his stomach drop because it sat under the skin the way his own work did. Hogwarts took impacts, held them, and kept the record in stress.

"All right," he whispered. "All right. Show me."

The next mark along the desk carried no language he knew, only the same load bracket bending outward at the end. The grouping implied a crowded burst of force at one node. More hands fed it than punishment usually took, all that force packed hard onto a single point, if he was reading honestly. He pressed it with two fingers.

The line at Hogwarts brightened until it ceased to look like a line. It opened into a white seam.

Something answered.

Storybooks would have given it a face, claws, a mouth, something with teeth you could point at and call danger. The lens gave him blackness changing shape. A spread of darkness that had seemed evenly empty resolved into structure by moving the wrong way. Several distant occlusions altered together. The whole region canted toward the seam with the horrible efficiency of one thing distributed over too much distance. The stars behind it dimmed by degrees under the weight of that attention.

Owen's heel slid in the lake grit. He caught himself on the stone desk with his left hand, pain ringing up his wrist. The lens went on showing him the answer without any interest in whether he was prepared to look at it.

Predation was too small a word, really. Foxes and hawks were individual animals with stomachs. This was the relation a leak had to whatever lived by finding leaks. The brightened seam advertised itself for use.

He forced himself to look back down at the marks instead of freezing there with his hand cramped against the desk edge. Beyond the concentrated-exchange mark, another bracket widened from the same sign family until it enclosed the whole node. That had to mean siege, or something close enough that the old builders had expected castle-scale violence and planned to measure it. If you built a school as a fortress and wired it into a planetary ward, you did not get to act surprised when war and infrastructure eventually touched.

He did not want to touch that mark. He touched it anyway.

The lens answered so fast it felt like a blow.

The Hogwarts seam whitened past legibility. A rent opened where the crack had been. The ward field around it flared outward in a branching glaze. Beyond it the dark cohered further, enough that the chamber seemed to acquire more cold from the sight alone. The distributed thing held to its course with the patience of certainty. Owen had the awful impression that if such a flare occurred in truth, there would be a period of minutes or hours in which the breach was still becoming real and the answer beyond it would already be on its way along some route that made air, broom, beast, and ship look like guesses from a smaller world.

He snatched his hand off the desk. The image thinned at once. The white rent fell back to a strained line, the dark beyond loosened into scattered absences again, and the chamber's ordinary cold came back as if someone had taken a blade from the back of his neck.

For a little while he could not do anything except stand there and listen to water working through the stone.

He had been right, and the fact did not feel triumphant. Some childish bit of him had still been reserving space for symbolism, old ward weather, the usual noble rubbish about balance and vigilance and legacy. No symbolism survived the lens. The thing outside existed on its own terms, and the school's violence was more than ugly local history inside a sealed world. If Hogwarts went to battle, the castle would show itself. Potter was still at large. Voldemort had made the school a fortress and a hostage both. Neville was already stockpiling people, food, exits, stubbornness. Battle here had been the shape the year was making all along.

His hand hurt. He looked down and found the groove on the desk had reopened the old burn in two fine splits across the centre. There was not much blood, but there was enough to bead. He wiped it on his handkerchief, opened the notebook to a clean page, and wrote work first. The anchor would have to be stabilised before any battle load hit the node. Tidying, study, admiration: none of that mattered. He wrote the word twice, once in English and once beneath it in the closest Meridian structure he could manage, because the second version carried load and sequence inside it. Then he listed what he had and what he did not: existing plinth responses; partial transit access; one functioning read instrument, damaged but legible; insufficient command of seam language; no margin for another local amendment used on corridors, punishments, or rescue. Beneath that he added what tomorrow now required: a better copy of the seam table, chalk that did not shed too much grit, salt, clean water, bandages, something thin enough to fit the fine grooves without snapping, a plausible reason to miss part of supper, an Arithmancy essay finished on time so nobody started counting his hours.

The secrecy problem clarified itself before the blood dried. If he told Prue, she would want to see the lens. Then she would want Neville told before supper, and Neville would quite reasonably want exits counted again, first-years shifted, food moved, some answer about how many nights they had left and whether the castle would feel it before the fighting started. Martha would hear battle and go straight to bodies, beds, who could still run. None of that would be foolish. That was the problem. Every sensible conversation would cost a night he did not have. The work that might keep the seam from going white would turn into demonstrations, meetings, questions, objections he would deserve. The thought should have shamed him more cleanly than it did. Instead it went into the list as another constraint. No demonstrations. No meetings. No time lost.

He kept writing until the candle shrank enough to spit tallow down the side. Only then did he check the lens once more without touching any load mark. The ward skin lay around the stars like breath on black glass. The stressed place at Hogwarts remained visible, hairline-thin and viciously bright. He took up the candle and climbed slower than he had descended because the cold had got properly into him now. Water had soaked through both socks. His fingers ached around the notebook. Twice he stopped to brace himself against the wall while the route's old pressure built in his ears and released again. When he reached the undercroft, the borrowed opening was already losing definition. The stair still existed, but it had resumed being reluctant. He stepped out onto the plinth-side stone just as the wall hardened back into itself and the pale line of damp sank to an ordinary seep.

For a moment he stood in the dark with the notebook clutched to his chest and listened to the castle above. Weight spoke first: a door somewhere overhead, a far-off run of pipes, one deep movement in the stone that might have been the lake or the school settling into night. He knew now what the lens would make of enough magic thrown through these foundations at once. The whole castle would become a flare. He pinched out the candle. The smoke smelled greasy and domestic, absurdly kitchen-like after the chamber below. By the time he reached the service stair and the first faint warmth from the kitchen runs, the whole calendar had reduced itself to that one requirement: get to the anchor before the war did. Breakfast, essays, and the rest of April would have to fit around it.


# Chapter 14

April arrived anyway. The greenhouses had started sweating before breakfast. Condensation ran down the panes in fat, slow tracks, and the first breath inside Greenhouse Three always hit like a hand over the mouth: wet soil, tomato leaf, damp rope, old compost warming up. Professor Sprout had propped two broken panes with mismatched bits of glass and a strip of tin, and new growth was already climbing over the repair as if damage were only another sort of frame.

Nobody spoke much at first. They had trowels in one hand and essays in the other. Someone had abandoned a Transfiguration parchment on the potting bench and pressed a crescent of dirt straight through the title. A pair of third-years on the far side were arguing in undertones about whether the vanishment chart was due Thursday or Friday, with the low, desperate seriousness of people to whom the answer mattered. It still mattered. The castle had not stopped producing deadlines because it was occupied. There was a two-foot essay due on controlled vanishment, and if the school cracked open over their heads the essay would probably still have been due on controlled vanishment.

Owen worked a tangle of seedling roots free of their tray and tried not to think in systems. Spring made systems too easy. Every bench was a proof of persistence. Nasturtiums had gone reckless over one corner, bright enough to look rude. Shrivelfig cuttings that had sat all winter like dead fingers were furred silver-green at the tips. Someone had left the greenhouse door open a fraction, and cold air kept moving across the backs of his wrists in little draughts that smelled of thawed mud and the lake.

Sprout clapped once. "Mind the roots, not the leaves. The leaves are showing off."

A few people laughed, brief and unwilling. It was enough.

Owen shifted another plug of soil into a pot and looked, against his better judgment, out through the sweating glass toward the lawns. The grounds were greening in uneven swathes. Black patches still held winter, but between them the first clean colour had come up in a hurry, so that the paths seemed to have been drawn through a place briefly willing to forget itself. Beyond the slope the lake moved under a hard white sky. Wind roughened it in dark strips. From here he could not see the chamber below it, the broken lens, the torn brightness in the old work. He could see only water, and the giant beech near the shore carrying buds at every branch tip like a promise made too early.

His right palm ached where the old burn crossed it, not from pain exactly but from recognition. Some days the hidden work sat in his body before he thought of it, a change in pressure behind the teeth, a hot awareness along scarred skin. Today even the little school magic around him seemed louder than it had a month ago. A girl at the next bench muttered a coaxing charm over a tray of knotgrass and the syllables rang against the greenhouse glass with a density that made him glance up. Nothing happened except the knotgrass straightening obediently in its damp earth. That was the problem. So much of the world still worked.

After Herbology the castle smelt of thawing stone and boiled cabbage. Students moved through the corridors with books clamped to jumpers, skirts damp at the hem from the grounds, ties loosened by degrees as the day warmed. Someone had opened a high window near the moving staircase and the whole landing was full of March-cold air, sharp enough to make noses run. A second-year went past carrying three library books and a loaf heel wrapped in a napkin. Two boys from Ravenclaw were having an earnest row over Arithmancy tables with such total focus that one of them walked into a suit of armour and apologised to it.

It would have been easier if the school had looked ruined all at once. Instead ink still dried on cuffs. People still nicked extra butter at breakfast. Peeves still tipped water out of a trophy cup onto anyone silly enough to run under him.

At lunch he took his tray to the Hufflepuff table and found space halfway down the bench. The soup was green enough to suggest cress and thin enough to suggest otherwise. Bread came round in torn chunks rather than slices. Somebody had salted the radishes too hard. Over at the Gryffindor table Neville was bent over a bit of parchment with two younger students crowded in, talking with his finger, not his voice, moving from line to line as if the plan would hold together better if he could pin it to the wood. He looked tired in the compact, competent way he had looked all year: collar not quite straight, bruise yellowing at the jaw, hands too rough now for a boy who had once mostly handled plants.

Martha crossed the aisle carrying a second bowl she clearly had no business carrying on her bad leg. She favoured the left side when she thought nobody was looking and then over-corrected when she caught someone noticing. Today she had her dark plait wound up in a knot that had already begun to collapse, and when she sat she tore the crust off her bread by habit and slid it into her pocket instead of eating it. Later, for somebody who missed supper or got shut in somewhere. Or just later. Owen knew the movement before he let himself know he was watching.

She did not look at him. That was not new. It still landed fresh.

Prue arrived late enough that most of the decent bread had gone. She dropped onto the Ravenclaw bench with a stack of books under one arm and pushed her spectacles back up by the bridge without using a hand, an old little trick of her face. Her cuffs were inked blue again. She carried pencils loose in her pocket and they always worked free and jabbed through the lining; once, waiting in the library, Owen had watched her pull out three snapped points and curse at them with extraordinary quiet intensity. Today she set a folded scrap of parchment beside her bowl, glanced once down the hall as if checking whether anyone was watching the wrong thing, and then looked across at him.

She did not smile. She tipped her head very slightly toward the doors.

Later, that meant. Walk, or talk, or both.

He looked down at his soup and nodded once.

The afternoon went on being itself. A corridor smelled of floor polish and wet wool. Somebody in Charms had managed to set a quill smoking and got sent out with black fingers. There was homework from Binns as if Binns still inhabited the same century as the rest of them. Owen copied half a page of notes in the library and none of the words sat properly because he kept hearing, behind the ordinary scrape of chairs and turning paper, the pressure change he had felt under the lake when the star-lens flared. Battle-scale magic. Brightness through a tear. An event already waiting somewhere in front of them, coming on whether the school was ready or not.

When the dinner bell went he nearly did not go. Then he went because not going would be visible, and visibility had its own costs now. By the time he came back down toward the cellars the kitchen heat had started to breathe through the stones. Bread, onions, something yeasty and sweet. The scent caught under the ribs with unreasonable force. For a second he wanted nothing clever at all, only the heat on his face and a torn heel in his hand.

Prue was waiting at the end of the corridor where the cellar passage turned out toward the yard door. She had traded the stack of books for a single notebook tied shut with string. One curl had worked itself free and was lifting in the draught by her cheek. When he came up she looked at him for a second as if measuring whether he would try to be difficult from the first word.

"You ate?" she said.

"Some of it."

"Good. Come outside before I change my mind."

He went.

* * *

The path above the lake was still half winter. Mud sucked at the edges, and the reeds at the shore lay in flattened brown mats where the ice had crushed them. But primroses had started up in the shelter of the wall and the willow by the boathouse was beginning to haze green. The evening light was thin and bright enough to make the black water look metallic. Somewhere higher up the grounds a group of students were shouting over a game with all the desperate normality of people making use of an hour that might later be taken away.

Prue sat on the low stone wall, gathered her skirt under her, and untied the string around the notebook only to wind it round her fingers again. She always needed one thing in hand when she was thinking. If there were no book she used string, and if there were no string she picked the paper label off her own bottle of ink in strips and regretted it later when the bottle went tacky.

For a minute they said nothing important.

"Did Sprout still have you all elbow-deep in compost?" she asked.

"Mostly seedlings. One tomato section and some shrivelfig cuttings."

"How pastoral."

"You say that as if you'd have preferred Binns."

"I would have preferred a fire and two uninterrupted hours, but the school remains vulgar about my preferences."

The corner of his mouth moved, barely, and she saw it. She looked away at the lake as if not to press too hard on the almost of a better evening.

"I had to spend forty minutes helping Idris Fawley understand why his chart kept slipping a column," she said after a while. "It was because he can't count, but delicately. There's only so gently you can phrase that."

"Did you phrase it gently?"

"No. I'm trying not to lie more than necessary."

The line sat between them longer than it should have. Owen felt the shift and hated himself for hearing it as structure before he heard it as warning.

He said, "Right."

Prue rubbed the notebook string between thumb and forefinger until it bit white into her skin. "I know something changed."

He kept looking at the lake. "A great deal keeps changing."

"Don't." Her voice did not rise. It thinned. "If I ask badly, answer the better version. I know something changed below."

Wind came off the water and found the gap at the back of his collar. He could have lied. Not cleanly, but enough to preserve the shape of the evening for ten more minutes. He had got good at answering the question nearest the question, at making timing sound like the problem when the problem was value.

"I learned some things about pressure," he said. "About what makes the anchor worse."

"And now you look as if you're already somewhere else even when you're sitting still."

"I'm here."

"You know that's not what I mean."

He dug his thumbnail into the old burn at his palm. The skin there had gone smooth and shiny months ago. It still felt, at certain moments, as if it had only just closed. "I'm trying to work out what's urgent."

Prue gave a short laugh with no amusement in it. "You've settled that already. It's the arithmetic after that you can't stand."

He turned then. "I'm trying to stop it getting worse."

Her spectacles had slid down her nose again. She pushed them back up and looked at him over the rims with that dry, almost embarrassed exactness she used when she was coming closest to something personal. Behind her the lake took the light and broke it into hard pieces. A gull cried once, ugly and ordinary.

"I'm not asking you to give it up," she said. "I know you won't. Fine. Leave that. I'm asking for... some order to it. That's all."

He said nothing.

"Listen." She unwound the string from her fingers and had to start over because it snagged at her knuckle. "The school's not going to stay like this. Something's coming. We both know that, so don't do the careful little dodge. If it goes bad here, properly bad, the cellar first."

"Here meaning what?"

"Here meaning Hufflepuff first-years, anyone stuck by the kitchen turn, whoever comes apart on the lower stairs because all of this has finally gone to hell," she said, sharper now. "You know that side of the school. Better than most people. If the upper corridors jam, you get those children and anyone else in the cellar traffic to Neville's lot, or the Room if that's where they've put them. Then you go below. Not before. Afterwards, if there's an afterwards, we go back to it. Together, if you want. I do mean that."

The tenderness of it made him feel briefly sick. She had made the ask small and human-sized, impossible to shove aside as naivety.

He looked down at her hand and the string cutting her fingers. He wanted, with a force that felt almost childish, to take it away from her and say yes. Cellar first. Then the undercroft. A promise small enough to survive saying aloud and large enough to bind him. He could see the life inside it whole. That was the worst part. He could see the life he was refusing.

"Prue," he said, and stopped.

She waited.

"If it starts, I've only got minutes."

Something in her face closed down. Recognition first, anger gathering behind it. "Of course that's what you've got."

"I mean it literally. I can't just leave it and hope it's still there when I get back. If the first discharge is big enough, I might have minutes. That's all."

"For the ward."

He said, after a moment, "Yes."

"Then spend those minutes on actual people," she said. "That's the smaller promise. The cellar first. The children first. Then you can go below. I'm not asking you to save everyone. I'm asking you to start with the ones in front of you. I'm trying to make it embarrassingly easy."

He opened his mouth, meaning to say something human, and heard himself miss it. "I can't be in two places, can I? If the condition shifts before the anchor's stabilised, that's it. I lose the way in."

Prue stared at him for a second, then made a short, unbelieving sound. "Do you hear yourself?"

"I'm trying to tell you what happens."

"I know. That's what you do. You get exact, and then... and then I'm meant to act as if that's the whole of it."

He had no answer that would not prove her right in a different register.

Below them a wave slapped against stone. Somewhere behind the greenhouses someone yelled with laughter and got shushed. The supper bell carried out from the castle, thin and cracked in the wind. Owen felt each sound arrive and fail to alter the arrangement between them.

Prue slipped off the wall and stood facing him. She was nearly his height on the path, the notebook tucked against her ribs like a shield she had not meant to use.

"All right," she said. "Then I need the honest version."

He forced himself to look at her.

"If it comes to it," she said, "if the school breaks open and you can't do both, who gets you first: the cellar children or the undercroft?"

The wind worried at the loose curl by her cheek. He could see the little smudge where ink had marked the side of her hand. There was a minute in which honesty and cruelty were, for practical purposes, the same object.

"I don't know," he said.

She breathed in once through her nose and shut her eyes. He saw the sentence hit her body like a heavy tray dropped into a pair of hands that had expected the weight and still did not quite absorb it cleanly.

"You do," she said. "You've known that for a while."

He said nothing.

"You know, and I know, and that's enough." She looked past him toward the castle. "I kept thinking this was fear, that's all. That if you cared too much about anything up here it'd get used on you. Which is awful, yes, but ordinary awful. You've built your own measure, and the rest of us keep sort of sliding off the edge of it."

"That's not fair."

"Stop saying that. Fairness isn't what I owe you right now."

He flinched. She saw and almost softened. Almost. It made things worse.

"I'm not telling Neville," she said after a moment. "Or Martha either. Don't look at me like that. I'm not doing it for you. The Carrows don't need the help, and panic would only make a bigger mess. If you need a practical assurance, there it is."

"Prue, I didn't ask you to keep anything."

"You don't have to ask. We do the sums anyway because you won't say it straight."

The notebook string had cut a red line around her finger. She unwound it at last and let it hang.

"I can still help with text," she said. "If you bring me notation, I'll still look at notation. I'm angry. I can still think. But this, whatever this nearly was, I can't do while you keep putting every breathing person beside you after something under the floor."

He could feel himself losing the only reply that mattered even as he reached for it. Sorry was true and useless. Trust me was impossible. It will be worth it sounded insane, and worse than insane, it sounded proud.

"I'm sorry," he said anyway.

Prue gave him a tired look. "I know. That's nearly worse."

She started back toward the castle. After three steps she stopped without turning.

"Try the cellar first, if you can manage it," she said. "That was the compromise. I meant it."

Then she went on, fast enough that he would have had to call after her to stop her, and he did not.

He stood on the path until the cold got through his socks and the primroses at the wall lost what little colour they had held in the dusk.

* * *

The kitchens were louder than the rest of the castle because they had to be. Pans struck stone. Water ran. Someone laughed outright at a joke Owen could not hear. Steam took the low ceiling and came back damp. After the lake the heat felt almost indecent. It smelt of onions, yeast, dishwater, and the singed sweetness of something caught at the edge and rescued in time.

Tallow was at the long scrubbed table by the ovens, sorting bent spoons into three neat rows by damage rather than size. His hands were wet to the wrist. One ear, already singed at the tip, twitched each time a bigger cauldron lid came down somewhere behind him. He had tucked a dish towel into his belt and forgotten it there.

Owen stopped just inside the arch. He had been coming to the kitchens all year and still had not learned how not to enter as if he were asking a favour of a room.

Tallow did not look up. "If you're after hot bread, Mr Phipps, you're late. The good loaves went upstairs. The rest are cooling and thinking bitter thoughts."

"I'm not after bread."

"A pity. Bread is easier to improve than boys."

Owen might once have smiled. Tonight the line only made the inside of his chest feel scraped clean.

He came closer. "I need a route."

That got Tallow's eyes on him at last. They were dark and bright and entirely unamused. "You do very often."

"No, right, sorry. I mean I need to know if there's a lower way from here to the undercroft if the main corridors are blocked."

Tallow picked up a spoon with one twisted tine, pressed the metal against the table edge with both thumbs, and straightened it slowly before answering. "Blocked how?"

"Crowded. Watched. Not usable."

"That is not an answer, young sir. That is three anxieties in a stack."

Owen heard the old habit in himself before he could stop it. "If there was fighting. Or enough people moving at once that the stairwells jammed. Or if the Carrows had half the doors sealed. Or if I had to get below quickly without being seen by anyone who'd think the wrong thing of it."

"Ah," Tallow said. "So. Only the ordinary cheerful possibilities."

He set the repaired spoon aside and reached for another. Around them the kitchen kept going. A tray of peeled carrots thudded down. Two younger elves hurried past carrying soup tureens between them, not glancing at Owen at all. Somewhere near the far hearth a pot boiled over and somebody swore in a mild, concentrated way.

Tallow said, "Miss Sorrell was in earlier. She carried the potato sacks badly because of that leg and then had the manners to look ashamed about it, which was foolish because potatoes have no pride. Miss Hollis put away six catalog ledgers for the library and stopped to dry her spectacles over the pastry oven because she won't admit they fog. Everybody brings something through the kitchen when the house is under strain."

Owen stayed very still.

"I know what you think of me," he said.

"Do you?" Tallow's tone remained light, which only made the rebuke more exact. "Because Tallow thinks many things of you, and some of them do move around."

He took up a spoon so bent it was nearly useless, considered it, and dropped it into a separate bowl.

"The house gets clever boys every few years," he said. "Clever girls too. The house sees plenty of those."

He bent another spoon against the table edge, frowned at the bend, and started again with a smaller one.

"What's scarce is children who can feel the weight of other children while they're being clever. No, feel is soft. Count it. Carry it. You came asking for routes when there were books. Then when there were old stones. Now because you think the castle may split and you want the quickest way under it. Fine. Useful. Tallow keeps useful on a different shelf from care."

The words landed harder than Prue's had because they were smaller. Less generous. Less interested in his soul. Tallow was discussing him as a maintenance problem, a load borne badly.

Owen looked past him at the ovens, at the dough waiting under cloths, at the rack of drying bowls. "If the castle may split," he said quietly, "then the route matters whether or not I deserve it."

Tallow nodded once. "Yes. Better. That is the first sensible thing you've said since coming down those steps."

He wiped his hands on the towel at his belt and hopped down from the stool. Up close the damp in his cuffs had gone grey with flour. He smelled faintly of smoke and washing soda.

"There's a service stair behind the scullery rack," he said. "Third rack. Left side. Boys always pull the first rack because boys are committed to obviousness. The hinge sticks in damp weather, so lift before you pull. It goes down past the hot-water mains and the old coal drop. At the bottom take the passage with the cracked blue tiles. If it stinks of lye, you've gone toward laundry and you are an idiot."

Despite himself, Owen said, "Cracked blue tiles. Away from lye."

"Then a narrow turn with a drain underfoot. Mind it. The stone sweats there and feet go from under people in a way that embarrasses everybody. You follow that until the wall starts sounding thick on the left and hollow on the right. Count seven iron brackets. At the seventh there is a hatch no taller than your shoulder. The latch is hidden under a lip of mortar because the founders had no trust in anybody's fingers. That gets you into the older run."

"While the house is being ordinary," he added. "If alarms go and the hot-water mains start knocking, the hinge screams on the way back up and the scullery uses that stair for kitchen traffic. Burns, kettles, children if it's truly bad. You'll get down quiet enough if you go early. You won't have it to yourself after."

"Toward the east side?"

"Toward where the house still remembers itself, yes."

Tallow's face altered a fraction on the last words, drawing tighter with tiredness.

"I won't take you past the mains," he said. "The lower dark bites now. More than it did in winter. It has an appetite on it."

"I wasn't asking you to."

"You seldom ask that particular thing. You wait. Somebody offers. Then when it costs them, you look surprised."

Owen shut his eyes for a second. The kitchen noise moved around him, all the labour of feeding a school pretending not to hear. When he opened them Tallow was already at a shelf, reaching up for something.

What he brought down was a heel of yesterday's loaf wrapped in cloth and a little paper twist of salt.

"For later," Tallow said. "You haven't earned cosseting. Boys below stairs forget they are animals and then fall over at important moments."

He put the bundle into Owen's hand without ceremony. It was still faintly warm at the centre where the loaf had held its heat.

"Thank you," Owen said.

Tallow gave him a look. "If you break crockery in that lower passage, leave it where it lies till morning. Tallow is not climbing after your guilt in the dark."

It was the nearest thing to kindness Owen had been offered in hours, and even that arrived shaped like a rule.

"Right," he said. "I won't."

Tallow turned back to the spoons. "See that you don't."

Owen stood there another moment, bread and salt in one hand, route beating itself into memory. Around him the kitchens carried on with their hot practical life, and he felt with a sudden, ugly clarity that he had been taking from rooms like this all year under the name of necessity. Food. Passage. Quiet. Interpretation. Warmth when it was offered. He called it necessity because the danger was real. The danger was real. The ledger still wasn't clean.

Nobody asked him to stay. Nobody told him to go.

When he left, the ovens were breathing and the spoons on the table made three tidy rows, each one bent to the degree the house could still justify keeping.

* * *

The Hufflepuff common room had gone soft with evening by the time he came back. Firelight, damp shoes by the hearth, the low continuous mutter of revision. Somebody near the far wall was reading notes aloud to himself in a hoarse whisper, getting stuck on the same line every time and beginning again with visible resentment. Two fourth-years were sharing a bowl of stolen treacle tart with the solemn concentration of priests. A pair of socks had been hung too near the fire and were beginning to smell singed.

Owen stood just inside the round doorway with his notebook under one arm and Tallow's wrapped bread in his pocket and let the room press against him. Eli Mercer asleep face-down over half-finished History of Magic notes. Mae Doran filing her broken nails with the edge of a ruler because she could not think with roughness catching on parchment. The Hufflepuff clock a minute slow as it had been since October, nobody fixing it because everybody had got used to leaning around the error together.

Mae looked up first and lifted the ruler at him like a summons. Two first-years were perched at her table with a rough map of the cellar between them, all circles and arrows and anxious crossings-out.

"Phipps. Good. Can you walk these two to the kitchen turn and back?" she asked. "They keep mixing up the bend by the scullery, and if the upper stairs ever jam they'll hare off the wrong way."

"Not now," he said.

Mae frowned. "It's two minutes."

"I know. Later."

The nearer first-year dropped his eyes at once, as if the trouble were asking.

If he crossed the room and sat, someone would make space. If he asked after the revised watch rota, somebody would answer. If he went looking properly, he could still find the Room of Requirement before curfew. He knew how to get to the Ravenclaw stair without being seen from the main landing.

He went to the dormitory instead.

The room smelled of wool blanket, soap gone flat in the dish, and the damp mineral note that came in from the lake through cellar stone. His trunk was under the bed. The boards complained when he knelt. He took out the ash-linen notebook, the spare pencil shaved with a knife because sharpeners were unreliable, two lengths of candle, the bit of chalk wrapped in rag, and the cleanest bandage he had left. He added Tallow's bread. Then he stopped with his hand on the latch and looked at what he had made.

It looked, offensively, like preparation: a kit laid out by someone who had already decided.

On the bed opposite, somebody had left a Charms textbook open face-down at a page on shielding practice. A sock hung from the bedpost by one damp toe. Beneath the window sat an abandoned mug with a skin forming over the tea. The room was full of boys expecting tomorrow. Even fear here kept the form of tomorrow: essays, punishments, breakfast, revision, maybe a better day if they were stupid enough or sane enough to keep imagining one.

He sat on the edge of his bed and took out a sheet of parchment. For a long time he did not write. Then, very small, he put down Prue's name.

He stared at it until the letters looked like a copied exercise.

What could he say that was not another demand dressed as disclosure? Warn them, but he could not explain. Trust me, when trust was exactly what he had failed to make possible. I am sorry, which she already knew. Martha's name came to him next without permission, along with the sight of her at lunch slipping bread crust into her pocket for later, saving for scarcity even in a room with food on the table. He thought of the way she had once, in winter, shoved a bowl at him and told him to stop acting like being hungry was a moral refinement. He thought of Prue winding string into her fingers on the lake wall until honesty had cut a mark into her skin.

He turned the parchment over. Blank on both sides now.

Down in the common room someone laughed hard enough to choke. Somebody else told him to shut up. The sound came through the floorboards warm and distorted, human beyond argument.

Owen put the parchment away unwritten.

Then he opened the notebook and copied the route exactly as Tallow had given it, notating the hinge, the tiles, the drain, the iron brackets. Once the directions were down, his hand kept going. He added timings from the cellar stairs, probable choke points if the upper corridors jammed, the likely interval between first alarm and full crowding if the school had to move students quickly. Method came to him with hideous relief. It always had edges. It did not ask whether the thing being done deserved doing. Only whether it would hold.

When the notes were finished he sat with the pencil lifted and listened to the castle.

Above him, footsteps crossed and recrossed. Somewhere deeper on the right a pipe knocked twice, then settled. The lake pressed at the walls in its old slow rhythm. Underneath all that, or in between it, he could feel the other structure he had spent months teaching himself to notice: tension in the older stone, a wrongness not yet motion but preparing for it. The anchor was not a voice. It had no intention he could claim to hear. But it was under strain, and strain had its own texture. He knew it now the way a carpenter knew warp in timber by touch. The school could go on setting essays and baking bread and revising defensive charms right up to the hour something larger used all of that life as load.

Prue's fingers had gone red where the string bit. The cellar first. Neville in a watchroom with lists. Martha on her bad leg carrying something heavier than she should. First-years who would bolt if the walls moved. Martha was elsewhere in the castle, angry enough not to want him near her. Prue had walked away on her own feet. Neville counted him now as a problem at the edge of planning. He could still turn left when the thing came. He could still go where the shouting was and leave the service stair alone.

He looked down at his hand. Ink along the side of the finger. Chalk dust in the cut at his knuckle. The old burn in the palm gone pale and shiny where skin had healed wrong. A schoolboy's hand at first glance. Then the burn showed itself, pale and wrong across the palm.

Then he closed the notebook, packed the pencil into its spine, slid the candles, chalk, bandage, and bread under his bed where he could reach them in the dark, and pulled his satchel from under the frame. He shoved in enough ordinary school things to give it shape, then tucked the ash-linen notebook under his jumper.

When he went back through the common room, Mae looked up from the same rough cellar map and said, "You all right?"

It was the easiest question in the world to answer badly.

"Yeah," he said. "Just tired."

She nodded and bent back over the arrows on the page. The room accepted the lie because rooms like this had to.

At the round door he paused. Left would take him toward the corridor that eventually rose to the rest of the school, to watchrooms, whispered plans, other bodies, the whole miserable beloved scale of immediate things. Right would take him past the kitchens, past the heat and the bread smell and the third rack in the scullery, down toward the blue-cracked tiles and the old dark waiting under the house.

He stood long enough that the fire behind him shifted and somebody complained of the draught.

Then he turned right.

Later, if there was one, could do what it liked with him. The choice itself was smaller and uglier than that. It was only a boy taking the corridor like it was any other evening, satchel on one hip, the real work hidden under his jumper.


# Chapter 15

The first sign was not the shouting. It was the floor.

Owen had just turned right at the round door, his satchel knocking one hip and his ash-linen notebook under his jumper, meaning to get back to the kitchens before curfew hardened into head count, when a low note went through the stone and up his legs. The nearest table rattled its teacups. A fourth-year girl revising Arithmancy slapped both hands flat on her page to keep it from skidding. Half a second later the old burn across Owen's right palm turned hot against the notebook's cover.

He stopped.

The room did not. Someone burst in from the cellar passage with his tie half off and shouted, "He's here."

That should have required a question. It did not. Every head came up at once. The common room, which had spent the year learning the downward shape of itself, altered in a single breath.

"Harry," the boy said, louder now because he had them. "Harry Potter. In the castle."

The name ran through the room like a match through dry lint. A first-year actually laughed, one quick bark of sound with terror under it. Two girls by the fireplace grabbed each other's sleeves. Someone said, "No, don't be thick," and someone else said, "Neville said it, I heard him," and chairs were already scraping back before any of them had decided what to do with their bodies.

Owen should have felt the same thing they did. He had spent months waiting for a break in the pattern, for some older and more dangerous person to kick a hole in the miserable ordinary of detentions, searches, and Amycus Carrow's breathing. Potter was exactly that kind of break. Potter in the castle meant the year had finally stopped pretending it could go on.

What Owen felt instead was the second pulse through the floor, harder than the first. The ward-scar in his hand tightened. Somewhere above them, high enough that the sound came down as a dull struck thud rather than a clear bang, old magic had begun taking new orders.

The anchor won't take a battle, he thought, and then, a beat behind it, Harry's here.

He was moving before he had decided to move. By the time he reached the cellar steps the passage was full of students pulling jumpers over their heads, dragging shoes on without laces, shouting contradictory versions of the same miracle.

"He got in through the portrait."

"No, he was in Ravenclaw Tower."

"Alecto Carrow's dead."

"Nobody said dead, don't say dead."

"Snape's gone up."

"Shut up and move."

Hope moved faster than instruction. It made everyone clumsy. It made them bright. A boy from the year below Owen, who had spent most of March speaking with one side of his mouth because Amycus had cracked a tooth, ran past grinning so hard the broken edge showed white in the torchlight. Owen had never seen him grin before. The sight of it landed in the chest like grief.

He climbed into the crush. The stone kept talking through the soles of his shoes. Every time a stair took a new load, every time some higher passage woke an old defensive charm, he felt the answer in his bad hand and behind his eyes. Hogwarts had room for punishing children, for corridor curses, for the Carrows' little public festivals of pain. It had been carrying those all year. This was different. This was the whole castle drawing breath.

At the first turn above the cellar, a portrait of a Tudor witch flung her own painted door open and cried, "They're rousing the statues," to nobody Owen could see. Three portraits away, a friar was ringing an invisible handbell with such conviction that the frame shook.

By the time Owen hit the wider corridor near the kitchens, the change had become visible. Armour that had spent the year standing decorative and stupid along the walls was no longer still. One suit rolled its shoulders with a metal groan. Another stepped down off its plinth and nearly trod on a Ravenclaw's foot. The torches had gone brighter without anyone touching them. Farther off, up toward the moving staircases, a woman's voice cracked through the air in clipped command and was answered by a thunder of feet.

The castle was choosing sides.

He got carried with the flow as far as the broad landing below the Entrance Hall before he could cut free. Bodies kept knocking him off line: girls in dressing gowns over trousers, boys with school ties hanging loose and wands gripped wrong from haste, a pair of sixth-years hauling a box of green-glass potion phials between them. Nobody looked like a soldier. That was the worst part. Nobody had turned noble or abstract on the strength of Potter's name. They were still the same underslept, half-fed schoolchildren they had been an hour earlier, only now they were moving with direction.

Neville stood three steps up from the landing, making direction by force.

He had lost a button from his shirt and not noticed. His sleeves were rolled unevenly. A broken watch with a frayed leather strap kept sliding out from under his cuff, and every few seconds, between one instruction and the next, he shoved it back with the heel of his hand, trying to bully time into behaving. His face was bruised yellow and purple under one eye. He looked older than seventeen and much more tired, but there was nothing grand about him. He was simply the person who had been doing this all year and was now doing it with the walls awake around him.

"If you're leaving, leave now," he was saying. "If you're staying, get armed and get back. First-years go with the little ones from Ravenclaw. Herbology lot, west corridor. No, not the front doors, don't be stupid, they'll watch the front doors first. Move."

Martha was below him on the landing with a roll of bandages under one arm and a canvas satchel bumping her hip. Her dark plait had mostly come loose and kept catching at the corner of her mouth when she turned her head. She kept spitting hair out of the way without using her hands because both hands were busy: one holding the satchel open with her strapped right hand, the other shoving things into it as people passed, dittany, strips of linen, a squat jar of honey-colored salve, a packet of biscuits flattened nearly to crumbs.

A first-year Hufflepuff girl stood frozen at Martha's elbow with tears hanging off her chin. Martha pulled one of the crushed biscuits free, shoved it into the girl's hand, and said, "Eat. I know it tastes like wall, just eat it. Then go with Dennis, all right? Don't stand here looking at me."

The girl nodded and went.

Neville leaned down without breaking stride and caught Martha's sleeve. "Your leg."

"Still attached."

"That wasn't the question."

"It's my knee tonight, actually. Hip's only joining in." She winced as she shifted her weight, then looked annoyed at her own face for doing it. "I'm fine. Keep shouting at people."

For half a second his hand stayed on her arm. It was not leadership or symbol. It was just Neville making sure Martha was still upright before he let the next demand have him. Then somebody yelled from above that the courtyard wanted more bodies and he turned away.

Martha saw Owen in the same instant.

Whatever she might have said to him a week ago was not available now. There was too much happening for the luxury of old anger. Her eyes hit him, assessed him, and moved on again in a single hard motion.

"Phipps," Neville said, seeing him because Neville was seeing everyone. "Good. Get to the kitchens. We need anything they can spare, bandages as well if Tallow's got sense enough not to hide them. Then come up to the courtyard."

The floor answered with another long subterranean shiver. Owen's burned palm flashed hot.

He knew, all at once and with the sick steadiness of a calculation finally resolving, that the first battle-spell had not even been thrown yet. This was only preparation: chairs being dragged, shields waking, professors pulling old protections back into use. The true load had not arrived. If he waited for the first curses to start crossing the grounds, if he waited for what everyone else meant by the battle, he would be too late.

He said, not loudly enough to stop the next order, "I need to go below."

Neville was already looking past him to the next face. "Below, above, I don't care, just get through the kitchens faster. Move."

It was not permission. Neville had heard the shape of a delay and thrown a task over it. Owen knew that, and let the mistake stand because correcting it would have meant saying under the castle, not after, now.

Martha had heard. He saw that much in the small flattening of her mouth. Then a third-year came up half-carrying a Ravenclaw boy whose shoe had gone dark with blood. Martha caught him under the elbow and jerked her chin at Owen.

"Take his other side," she said. "Hospital overflow. Now."

The boy lurched toward Owen at once, trusting the order more than the face. Owen could have taken the weight. Instead he stepped back.

"I can't," he said. "I need to go below now."

Martha's face shut hard. She braced on her bad leg, swore through her teeth, and a girl from the bucket line had to drop what she was carrying and catch the boy before he went to one knee.

"Fine," Martha said. "Then move."

Owen stood there long enough to feel useless and seen, then slipped out of the stream and back toward the kitchen passage while the whole landing kept pouring upward.

On the turn down, he looked back once.

Neville was still on the stair, voice gone rough already, one hand lifted to stop a panic before it started. Martha had braced the satchel against her side and was tying a bandage around the ankle of the bleeding boy with quick angry fingers because anger was what she had left for fear. Somebody farther up was laughing too hard. Somebody else was crying and pretending not to. Harry Potter's name kept erupting in scraps, something to put in the mouth while the hands hunted for work.

He fixed the landing in his head, trying to turn looking into a smaller kind of staying: Neville on the stair, Martha bent over the boy, the noise of children trying not to sound frightened. It did not work. Then he took the turn for the kitchens anyway.

***

The kitchens were no refuge from it. They had become a lung.

Heat hit him first, thick with steam and yeast and wet wool. Then sound: pans, shouted orders, boots on stone, house-elves underfoot, the hollow iron noise of cauldrons being lifted too fast. The long worktables that usually held bread and pies now carried armfuls of torn linen, baskets of potatoes, stacks of plates nobody had bothered to clear, and one alarming mound of old silver cutlery that had apparently been collected because in a crisis everything became either food, bandage, or weapon. For one traitorous second the room struck him as ordinary in the old way, supper chaos, Tallow swearing over a split sauce, too many bodies and not enough table. Then the burn in his palm thudded again and the fantasy dropped dead.

Tallow stood on a flour bin near the central range so he could see over the human bodies blundering through his workspace. He had tied a strip of blue cloth around one singed ear to keep it folded back from the heat, and his apron was dusted white to the knees. One hand held a ladle. The other held a spoon blackened at the bowl where something had caught and burned. He was staring at a cauldron with the affronted concentration he gave any pot stupid enough to misbehave.

"Lower that flame," he snapped at an older Slytherin who was trying to help and failing. "It's soup, not vengeance. If it splits now, you'll be feeding fighters greasy water and Tallow won't be blamed for your panic."

The Slytherin lowered the flame.

Tallow looked up and found Owen. Whatever warmth he had once reserved for him did not appear. Owen felt the absence of it at once, another earned thing. For a beat he nearly said bandages or soup or anything that would let him look less exactly like what he was.

"Young sir," he said. "You are either late for carrying or early for hiding."

Both charges hit cleanly because both were near enough true. Owen pushed through to the edge of the bin. "I need the route."

Tallow's eyes flicked once toward the ceiling, where a deep impact had just rolled dust from the joints between stones. The house-elf flinched, almost too small to see unless you were already looking for it.

"Of course you do," he said. "Because there is a war in the dining rooms and a clever boy always wants the passage under the spoons."

"Tallow."

"Do not call me Tallow like that. It doesn't make the sentence shorter." He set the burned spoon down with exaggerated care. "Third flue beyond the scullery. Left of the coal bins. The house is tightening there, so mind your shoulders. And if a rung gives under you, you deserve the bruise."

Owen started to move. Tallow spoke again, quieter.

"The route is open. That is not the same as blessing it."

There was no answer to that which would not sound childish. Owen gave a brief useless nod and turned away.

Students kept forcing him sideways as he cut across the kitchen. A pair of Gryffindors wrestled a cauldron of boiling water toward the service stair, red-faced with steam and fright. A house-elf ran under Owen's elbow with a sack of crusts nearly as big as his own body. Somebody had started slicing bread on the nearest table and left the knife standing upright in the loaf because there had not been time to lay it down properly. Every person in the room seemed to be hurrying toward the fight or hurrying so that somebody else could. Owen kept cutting across the grain toward the flue behind the coal bins.

A Hufflepuff third-year he half recognized shoved a stack of folded cloth into his chest, assuming he was part of the same traffic. Owen caught it on reflex.

"Upstairs, please," she said, breathless. She had tied her dressing gown shut with green twine. "Hospital wing's already full. They're using the Charms corridor."

"Right," he said.

The cloth stayed in his arms for a beat too long. His body knew the upward route before his mind could start lying about it. Then he laid the stack on the nearest table and kept moving. Behind him the girl swore, snatched it back off the wood, and ran for the stair herself. Shame came sharp and useless, then had to make room for the next pulse in his hand.

The scullery passage was narrower and less bright. Steam filmed the stone. The floor had gone slick with old water and fresh ash tracked in from above. Owen ducked past the coal bins, found the third flue behind a stack of dented tureens, and got both hands on the iron ladder bolted into the throat of the wall.

Another impact ran through the castle.

It was not close yet, but it was stronger. The iron bit his palms. In his right hand the old burn answered so hard he had to press his shoulder against the brick until the wave passed.

He put one foot on the first rung.

"No," Prue said from behind him. "No. Stop there."

He turned too fast and cracked the back of his head on the flue wall. For a stupid second all he saw was black brick and orange sparks.

Prue stood at the mouth of the passage with one hand braced on the coal bin, lungs still catching up with her. Her spectacles were in place but badly smeared, one lens grey with kitchen steam and fingerprint. Her curls had come loose from whatever she had tried to do to them and were frizzing damply around her face. She had an old book satchel across her body, the strap twisted, and three stiff index cards were still stuck under the buckle from sheer habit or accident. In one hand she held her wand. In the other, absurdly, a stub of carpenter's pencil.

He said, "What are you doing here?"

"Following the obvious," she said, still breathing harder than she wanted him to notice. "You vanished the second Neville gave you a job and let him keep talking, so now he thinks you took it. There weren't that many ways this was going to go that weren't awful."

Above them, feet hammered the service stair. Someone shouted for more buckets. The sound came through the stone blurred and bodied, the castle swallowing the words before handing them down.

Owen stepped off the rung but did not come away from the flue. "I don't have time for this."

"That isn't a serious sentence."

"It's true."

"Yes, and so is half the rubbish in your notebook. That doesn't make it the point."

He might have laughed on another night. He did not have it in him. "Prue."

"No. No, don't do that. Don't say my name like that and hope I'll lose the thread." She pushed her glasses up with the back of her wrist. The hand holding the pencil shook once and steadied. "Tell me properly. Are you actually going below now?"

"Yes."

"Tell me why."

Another shudder moved through the wall. This one came with grit. Dust loosened from the ceiling and caught in Prue's hair. Owen felt it in his teeth before he felt it in his feet.

"If the fighting starts properly," he said, "the anchor lights. Once it lights, the seam below goes with it. I've got a window now. After the first real exchange, I may not."

A pair of sixth-years came pounding down the service stair carrying a crate of bottles between them. Prue had to flatten against the wall to let them pass. Owen turned sideways as well, and for an instant they were pressed almost shoulder to shoulder, not touching, both forced to face the damp stone while the crate knocked by and one of the boys muttered, "Sorry, sorry, sorry," without meaning any one apology more than the others.

When the passage cleared, Prue looked at him again over the smear on her spectacles.

"Neville's upstairs," she said. "Martha's upstairs, and she can barely stand straight. I just watched her take that boy's weight because you wouldn't. The stairs are full of kids carrying water and sand and trying not to be sick. I'm not saying forget it. No, that's not fair. I'm saying not yet. Come upstairs first and do one thing for the people actually here. Carry, fight, hold a shield, anything. Then, if we're alive in ten minutes, I'll go below with you."

Above them, from somewhere out beyond the kitchens, a surge of sound rose and broke into many separate noises at once: cheering, screaming, a command Owen could not make out, then the unmistakable crack of curses hitting shield or stone. Dust fell in a soft rush from the flue throat onto his shoulder.

That was it. The first proper answer from the grounds.

The heat in his hand turned savage.

Owen caught the ladder before the sudden weakness in his knees could show on his face. Below that shaking iron, below the kitchen, below the service stone, he could feel the anchor responding like a live thing under a knife.

"It's begun," he said.

He scrubbed the back of his wrist under his nose. It came away dark.

Prue's voice went raw. "I know it's begun. That's why I'm here."

A line of older students forced down into the passage carrying sacks of sand and two shields between them. Owen and Prue had to flatten apart to let them through. One of the shields clipped Owen's shoulder hard enough to spin him half back toward the ladder. A girl at the rear saw where he was facing and did a double take that was almost comic in its plainness.

"Are you going down?" she asked, because people under pressure said obvious things.

Prue answered for him without taking her eyes off his face. "Not if he's got any sense."

The girl accepted that at face value and kept moving.

When they were alone again, Prue said, much quieter, "You can still turn round."

He could. Neville was three corridors away. Martha was somewhere above the kitchen traffic, probably swearing at anyone who tried to take the satchel off her. He could go back into the noise with both hands and whatever courage he possessed.

Below them the deeper seam kept opening, and every fresh strike upstairs tugged at it.

He heard himself say, very calmly because panic would only have sounded like a lie, "No. I'm not turning back."

Prue's face did not change at once. Then it did, subtly and much worse than tears would have been. She became more exact.

"Right," she said. "Fine. I wanted you to say it plain."

His mouth opened on nothing. Instead he put his foot back on the rung.

Prue did not grab him. That would have been mercy. She moved one step aside so he could pass cleanly if he meant it.
